Entering a House in Oaths: Customary Interpretation
Commentary on oath rulings for entering houses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that oaths are interpreted by custom ('urf) to determine whether entering a specific structure breaks an oath.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, oaths are interpreted by custom, not by dictionary definitions.

This passage from al-Hidayah and its commentaries explains the Hanafi ruling on oaths relating to entering a 'house' (bayt). The central principle is that oaths are built on customary usage ('urf), not on literal linguistic meanings or Quranic usage. The ruling then applies this principle to various structures: a suffah (roofed structure for sleeping) counts as a house, but the Kaaba, mosque, church, synagogue, dehliz, and canopy over a door do not, because custom does not consider them places for sleeping (baytutah). The commentary also resolves an intra-Hanafi dispute about the definition of suffah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It's swearing not to enter a 'house'. The ruling is that entering a suffah breaks the oath, but entering the Kaaba, mosque, etc., does not.
The ruling is specific to the context of oaths; it does not change the general language.
2
Locate the dalil and the legal principle
The main dalil is not a direct text but the principle that oaths are based on custom. The Quranic verses calling the Kaaba a 'house' are addressed by explaining that custom overrides linguistic usage in oaths.
The Hanafi school uses rational argument (dalil aqli) that custom governs oaths.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The ruling uses the custom-based definition of 'bayt': a place prepared for staying overnight. The suffah fits this; the other places do not, even if they are called 'house' in other contexts.
The wajh al-dalalah is the customary understanding, not a textual imperative.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a suffah must have four walls (like a winter room) or three (like a summer room). The preferred view is absolute application.
There is also inter-madhhab disagreement: Shafi'i relies on linguistic reality, Maliki on Quranic usage.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that custom determines the meaning in oaths, and the suffah is a house regardless of the number of walls, as long as it is used for sleeping.
This is the mufta bihi view from al-Hidayah and al-Binayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحلف بالفعل
فصل اليمين في الدخول والسكنى
من حلف لا يدخل بيتاً يحنث بدخول صفة لا الكعبة أو مسجد أو بيعة أو كنيسة أو دهليز أو ظلة باب دار لأن البيت موضع أعد للبيتوتة فالصفة بيت لا هذه المواضع
ثم شرح: الأصل في هذا الباب أن مبنى الأيمان عندنا على العرف ما لم ينو محتمل لفظه لأن المتكلم إنما يتكلم بالكلام العرفي وعلى هذا الأصل يتفرع فروع هذا الباب وعند الشافعي مبنى الأيمان على الحقيقة اللغوية وعند مالك على الاستعمال القرآني كذا في الفتح قوله صفة بضم الصاد وتشديد الفاء قال في الهداية لأنه تبنى للبيتوتة في بعض الأوقات فصار كالشتوي والصيفي وقيل هذا إذا كانت الصفة ذات حوائط أربعة وهكذا كانت صفاتهم وقيل الجواب مجرى على إطلاقه وهو الصحيح انتهى وقال العيني في البناية الشتوي هو الذي يبني لأن يبات فيه الشتاء والصيفي وهو الذي يبني لأن يبات فيه في الصيف فالشتوي له جدران أربعة في واحد منها باب والصيف له ثلاثة جدران وهو الصفة وذكر في الفتح أن السقف ليس شرطا في مسمى البيت والدهليز قوله لا الكعبة الخ أي لا يحنث بدخول الكعبة وإن كان يطلق عليها بيت الله وقد قال الله جعل الله الكعبة البيت الحرام قياما للناس وكذا لا يحنث بدخول المسجد وإن كان يطلق عليه بيت الله وقال الله في شأن المساجد في بيوت أذن الله أن ترفع ويذكر فيها اسمه الآية وذلك لأن البيت عرفا يفهم منه ما جعل وهيئ للبيتوتة أي النوم والسبات والقرار ليلا فلا يتبادر الذهن من لفظ البيت إلى الكعبة والمسجد ولهذا بعينه لا يحنث بدخول بيعة وهو بكسر الباء الموحدة وفتح العين بينهما ياء ساكنة هي معبد النصارى وكذا بدخول الكنيسة وهي على وزن كريمة معبد اليهود وكذا بدخول الظلة وهو بضم الظاء المعجمة وتشديد اللام المفتوحة الساباط الذي يكون على الباب من سقف له جذوع أطرافها على جدار الباب وأطرافها الآخر على جدار الجار المقابل له وكذا بدخول دهليز هو بكسر الدال المهملة وكسر اللام بينهما هاء ساكنة بعد اللام ياء ساكنة ثم زاي معجمة ما بين الباب والدار كذا في البحر والفتح قوله أو ظلة باب دار قال في المغرب قول الفقهاء ظلة الدار يريدون به السدة التي فوق الباب انتهى وفي زيادة لفظ باب الدار إشارة إلى أن الظلة لو كان داخل الدار يحنث بدخوله لأنه مما يبات فيه وكذا إذا كان الدهليز كبيرا بحيث يصلح لأن يبات فيه
Chapter of Oaths by Action: Section on Oaths Regarding Entering and Residing. Whoever swears not to enter a house breaks his oath by entering a Suffah (roofed structure), but not by entering the Kaaba, or a mosque, or a church, or a synagogue, or a dehliz (passageway), or a canopy over a house door, because a house is a place prepared for staying overnight; so a Suffah is a house, but these places are not. Then commentary: The basis in this chapter is that oaths with us (Hanafis) are based on custom, unless he intends a meaning that his utterance can bear, because the speaker only speaks in customary speech. On this basis branch the sub-rulings of this chapter. According to al-Shafi'i, oaths are based on linguistic reality, and according to Malik, on Quranic usage. Thus in al-Fath. He said: 'Suffah' with dammah on the sad and tashdid on the fa. He said in al-Hidayah: Because it is built for staying overnight at some times, so it becomes like the winter house and summer house. And it is said: This is when the Suffah has four walls, and such were their Suffahs. And it is said: The answer is given on absolute terms, and that is correct. End. And al-Ayni said in al-Binayah: The winter house is built to be slept in during winter, and the summer house is built to be slept in during summer. The winter house has four walls with a door in one, and the summer house has three walls, and that is the Suffah. And it is mentioned in al-Fath that a roof is not a condition for the name 'house' or 'dehliz'. He said: 'Not the Kaaba, etc.' i.e., he does not break his oath by entering the Kaaba, even though it is called the House of Allah. Allah said: 'Allah has made the Kaaba, the Sacred House, a standing (place) for the people' (5:97). Likewise, he does not break his oath by entering a mosque, even though it is called the House of Allah. And Allah said concerning mosques: 'In houses (mosques) which Allah has permitted to be raised and His name mentioned therein' (24:36). That is because 'house' in custom is understood to mean what is made and prepared for staying overnight, i.e., sleeping, resting, and settling at night. So the mind does not jump from the word 'house' to the Kaaba or mosque. For this same reason, he does not break his oath by entering a 'bi'ah' (church) – with kasrah on the ba, fathah on the 'ayn, between them a sukun on the ya – it is the place of worship of Christians. Likewise, by entering a 'kanisah' (synagogue) – on the pattern of 'karimah' – it is the place of worship of Jews. Likewise, by entering the 'zullah' (canopy) – with dammah on the dhal, tashdid on the lam with fathah – it is the sabat (covered passage) over the door, with a roof having beams, one end on the wall of the door and the other end on the wall of the opposite neighbor. Likewise, by entering a 'dihliz' (dehliz) – with kasrah on the dal and kasrah on the lam, between them a sukun on the ha, after the lam a sukun on the ya, then a zay – it is what is between the door and the house. Thus in al-Bahr and al-Fath. He said: 'Or canopy over a house door.' He said in al-Mughrib: The saying of the jurists 'canopy of the house' means the covering over the door. End. And the addition of the word 'house door' indicates that if the canopy were inside the house, his entry would break the oath because it is a place where one sleeps. Likewise, if the dehliz is large enough to be suitable for sleeping.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from al-Wiqayah, discussing the Hanafi ruling on oaths related to entering a 'house' (bayt). The central principle is that in the Hanafi school, oaths are interpreted according to custom ('urf), not according to strict linguistic definitions or Quranic usage. The commentator explains the ruling: if someone swears not to enter a house, they break the oath by entering a 'suffah' (a roofed structure with three or four walls, used for temporary dwelling), because such structures are customarily used for sleeping. However, they do not break the oath by entering the Kaaba, a mosque, a church (bi'ah), a synagogue (kanisah), a dehliz (dihliz, the passageway between door and house), or a canopy (zullah) over a house door. The reason is that these places, though called 'house' in some contexts, are not customarily understood as places for staying overnight (baytutah). The commentator then clarifies an intra-Hanafi dispute: some scholars require the suffah to have four walls (like a winter house), while others accept three walls (like a summer house). The preferred view is the absolute application of the ruling. The text also provides a detailed definition of each exception: the Kaaba is called 'House of Allah' in the Quran, but custom excludes it; likewise for mosques. The passage then defines bi'ah, kanisah, zullah, and dihliz. Finally, it notes that if a canopy is inside the house (not over the outside door) or if the dehliz is large enough to sleep in, they are considered houses and entry would break the oath. This is a good example of how the Hanafi school balances textual evidence (Quranic verses calling Kaaba/mosques 'houses') with customary understanding, prioritizing 'urf in matters of oaths.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath or vow that creates a legal obligation or prohibition, the breaking of which requires expiation (kaffarah).
البيت (al-bayt): In the context of oaths, a house is a structure customarily prepared for staying overnight (بيتوتة), not every building called a house.
الصفة (al-suffah): A roofed structure without full walls, often with three walls, used for temporary dwelling. It is considered a 'house' for oath purposes.
العرف (al-'urf): Customary usage of language among people, which is the basis for interpreting oaths in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following would break an oath not to enter a 'house' (bayt)?
A) Entering the Kaaba
B) Entering a mosque
C) Entering a suffah (roofed structure used for sleeping)
D) Entering a church

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, oaths are interpreted by custom ('urf), so only structures customarily used for sleeping (baytutah) count as 'houses' for oath purposes.

Indefinite Oath Not to Enter a Ruined House
Explains why entering a ruined house does not violate an indefinite oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between indefinite and definite nouns in Hanafi oath law to determine whether entering a ruined house violates an indefinite oath.

Core Idea
In an indefinite oath, the building is an essential description; without it, the house is not the intended subject.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Hanafi fiqh on oaths when the object is indefinite. When someone swears an indefinite oath not to enter 'a house' and then enters a ruined house with no building, the oath is not violated. The reasoning hinges on the principle that an indefinite noun in an oath is qualified by its customary description. Understanding this distinction prevents incorrect rulings in similar cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath type
Determine if the oath uses an indefinite noun (e.g., 'a house') or a definite noun (e.g., 'this house'). Here it is indefinite.
Indefinite (munkar) vs definite (mu'arraf).
2
Define the subject's essential description
For 'house', the building (walls and roof) is the customary description (wasf). Without it, the house is not a house in common usage.
Hanafi: the core meaning of dar is the plot; building is descriptive.
3
Check the actual object entered
Was the building completely absent? If the house is a ruined plot (sahra') with no building, the description is missing.
Partially ruined (some walls remain) still counts as a building.
4
Apply the principle: indefinite is qualified by description
Since the building is absent, the entered object does not match the description, so the oath is not violated.
Contrast with definite oaths where description is irrelevant.
5
Note the exception for partial ruin
If some walls remain and it has not become an open plot, the oath is violated unless the swearer had a specific intention narrowing the scope.
Intention can override the default interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما في لا يدخل دارا، فدخل دارا خربة، حيث لا يحنث. قوله: داراً خربة؛ - بفتح الخاء المعجمة وكسر الراء المهملة – ، والمراد بها: ما لا بناء فيه، فإنّه ذكر في مقابله: إن دخلها منهدمةً صحراء، وإنما تقع المقابلة بين المنكر والمعرّف في الحكم إذا تواردا في محل واحد، فإن دخل داراً خربة بعدما زالَ بعض حيطانها، ولم تصر صحراء يحنث في المنكر، إلا أن تكون له نيّة كذا في «الفتح»
As in: 'He swears by Allah not to enter a house, then enters a ruined house' – where he does not break the oath. [Commentary:] His saying: 'a ruined house' – with fatha on the kha' and kasra on the ra' – and what is meant by it is: that which has no building. For it is mentioned in contrast: 'if he enters it demolished as an open plot'. And the comparison between the indefinite and the definite in the ruling occurs only when they both apply to the same object. For if he enters a ruined house after some of its walls have fallen but it has not become an open plot, he breaks the oath in the indefinite case, unless he had a specific intention. So it is in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling for an indefinite oath not to enter a house. The base text states that if someone swears 'by Allah I will not enter a house' (indefinite, i.e., any house) and then enters a ruined house (one with no building), he does not break his oath. The commentator explains that 'ruined house' means a house with no building. The comparison is made with the case where he enters a completely demolished plot (sahra'); the ruling differs between the indefinite and definite oath if both apply to the same object. However, if the house is partially ruined (some walls still standing) and not yet an open plot, then entering it does break the oath in the indefinite case, unless he had a specific intention that would limit the scope. The reasoning is that in an indefinite oath, the building is considered a description (wasf) of the house; when the description is absent, the subject is not covered. This is based on the principle that the indefinite noun is defined by its description. The distinction between indefinite (munkar) and definite (mu'arraf) oaths is crucial: in the indefinite case, the description matters; in the definite case, it does not.

Key Terms
دار (dar): In this context, a house with a building and enclosing walls.
خربة (khariba): A ruined house with no building left.
منكر (munkar): Indefinite noun, e.g. 'a house' without specification.
معرف (mu'arraf): Definite noun, e.g. 'this house' with a specific reference.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'By Allah I will not enter a house' (indefinite) and then enters a completely ruined house with no building, what is the ruling?
A) He breaks his oath and must expiate.
B) He does not break his oath because the building is missing.
C) He breaks his oath only if he entered with intent.
D) The oath is invalid because the house is ruined.

Key Takeaway
In an indefinite oath, the customary description of the noun determines its scope; without the description, the oath is not violated.

Oath Not to Enter a House: Effect of Demolition, Conversion, and Roof
Ruling on definite oath not to enter a specific house under transformation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a definite oath not to enter a specific house when the house is demolished, rebuilt, converted to another structure, or when entering its roof, and explain the Hanafi distinction between the name of the house and its physical location.

Core Idea
An oath refers to the named entity; changing the name breaks the oath's applicability.

When someone swears not to enter a specific house, what happens if the house is demolished, rebuilt as something else, or if he merely stands on its roof? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rulings for these scenarios, distinguishing between the legal identity of the house and its physical form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling for entering the demolished plot
The text states that entering the house when it is demolished to an open plot (sahra') still breaks the oath because the plot is still called 'house' in Hanafi legal definition.
Key: the 'arshah (plot) is the core; the building is a description.
2
Understand the effect of name change: conversion to another building
If the house is rebuilt as a mosque, bathhouse, garden, or another house, entering does not break the oath because the new building has a different name, not 'dar'.
Even if the new building later collapses and a house is rebuilt, the oath remains unbroken because it is a new entity.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on standing on the roof
Early scholars (mutaqaddimin) consider the roof part of the enclosure, so standing on it breaks the oath. Later scholars (muta'akhkhirin) rely on custom ('urf) that 'entry' does not include the roof for non-users.
The later view is preferred for fatwa in many regions.
4
Apply the distinction between dar and bayt
The difference between 'house' (dar) and 'dwelling' (bayt) affects the scope; bayt specifically means a place to spend the night. The oath's binding power depends on the precise term used.
This nuance is not fully explored in the text, but the author signals it as a future point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي هذه الدار يحنث إن دخلها منهدمةً صحراء، أو بعدما بنيت أخرى، أو وقف على سطحها، كما لو جعلت مسجداً، أو حماماً، أو بستاناً، أو بيتاً. قوله: أو وقف على سطحها؛ يعني إذا حلف لا يدخل داراً فصعد على سطحه من خارج ووقف هناك حنث، هذا عند المتقدمين، وذلك لأنّ الدار عبارة عن ما أحاطت به الدائرة، وهذا حاصل في علوّ الدار وسفلها، وعند المتأخرين لا يحنث؛ لأن السطح وإن كان من أجزاء الدار، لكن لا يقالُ للقائم عليه والصاعد إليه إنه داخل عرفاً. كذا في «الفتح». قوله: كما لو جعلت مسجداً، أو حماماً، أو بستاناً، أو بيتاً؛ يعني لو حلف لا يدخل هذه الدار فانهدمت، وبني موضعها مسجد أو بستان أو بيت أو حمام وهو – بالفتح وتشديد الميم – : بيت يبنى للغُسل بالماء الحار، أو غلب عليها الماء فدخلها لا يحنث؛ لأنها لا تُسمّى داراً لحدوث اسم آخر لها، فإن انهدمَ الحَمّام ونحوه وصلح ذلك الموضع لبناء الدارِ فبنيت مرّة أخرى لا يحنث بالدخول فيها أيضاً؛ لأنه عاد له اسم الدار بسبب جديد، منزل منزلة الاسم الآخر. كذا في «الذخيرة». قوله: أو بيتاً؛ الفرق بين الدار والبيت: أنّ البيت اسم لما يُباتُ فيه، وهو أن
And in the case of 'this house' he breaks the oath if he enters it demolished as an open plot, or after it was built as another [structure], or if he stands on its roof. [Commentary:] His saying: 'or if he stands on its roof' – meaning if he swears not to enter a house and then climbs onto its roof from outside and stands there, he breaks the oath according to the early scholars, because the house is defined by what its enclosure surrounds, and that applies to its upper and lower parts. According to the later scholars, he does not break the oath because the roof, although part of the house, is not customarily called 'entering' for one standing on it. So it is in al-Fath. His saying: 'just as if it is made into a mosque, or a bathhouse, or a garden, or a house' – meaning if he swears not to enter this house and it collapses, and in its place is built a mosque, garden, house, or bathhouse (which is a building for washing with hot water), or water overwhelms it and he enters it, he does not break the oath because it is no longer called a 'house' due to the occurrence of another name. If the bathhouse etc. collapses and the site becomes suitable for building a house and is rebuilt again, he does not break the oath by entering it either, because the name 'house' returns only due to a new cause, analogous to a different name. So it is in al-Dhakhir. His saying: 'or a house' – the difference between dar and bayt is that bayt is a name for what one spends the night in, which is...
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling for a definite oath (e.g., 'by Allah I will not enter this house'). The base ruling affirms that if the house becomes a totally demolished plot, or if it is rebuilt as another structure, or if one stands on its roof, the oath is broken. The commentator provides details: For standing on the roof, the early scholars (mutaqaddimin) hold that it is a violation because the roof is part of the house's enclosure; later scholars (muta'akhkhirin) hold it is not an 'entry' in the customary sense. The relied-upon position may vary by region. For conversion to a mosque, bath, garden, or another house, the key principle is that the name 'house' is replaced by a new name, so entering the new structure does not break the oath. If the new structure later collapses and the site is used for a house again, the name 'house' returns, but the oath is still not broken because it is a new house, not the original one. The distinction between 'dar' and 'bayt' is noted: bayt specifically refers to a place for overnight stay.

Key Terms
دار (dar): A house with an enclosure (da'ira); the plot is the core, building is a description.
عرصة (arsa): The open plot or courtyard of a house.
سطح (sath): Roof of a house.
حمام (hammam): Bathhouse, specifically for hot water bathing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Someone swears not to enter a specific house. The house collapses and in its place a mosque is built. He enters the mosque. What is the ruling?
A) He breaks his oath because it is the same location.
B) He does not break his oath because it is no longer called a house.
C) He breaks his oath only if he entered during construction.
D) The oath becomes invalid due to the destruction.

Key Takeaway
In a definite oath, the ruling depends on the name and identity of the object, not the physical location; a change of name voids the oath's applicability.

Ruling on Stopping on the Roof in an Oath Not to Enter a House
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether roof entry breaks an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether standing on a roof breaks an oath not to enter a house, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Custom ('urf) can override literal definitions in determining whether an oath is broken.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: if a person swears not to enter a house and then climbs onto the roof from outside, does he break his oath? The early Hanafi scholars (mutaqaddimūn) held that the roof is part of the house, so the oath is broken. The later Hanafi scholars (muta'akhkhirūn) disagreed, arguing that custom does not consider the roof as 'inside'. Understanding this ikhtilaf illustrates how custom can influence legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath
The act is climbing onto the roof of a house from outside while under an oath not to enter that house. The legal question is whether this act constitutes a breach of the oath.
2
State the early scholars' position and its proof
The early scholars rule that the oath is broken because 'house' (dār) literally includes everything within its boundaries, both upper and lower parts. The roof is physically part of the house.
This is a literalist approach to the definition of dār.
3
State the later scholars' position and its proof
The later scholars rule that the oath is not broken because custom ('urf) does not consider a person on the roof to be 'inside' the house. The term 'entry' (dukhūl) implies being within the walls.
This relies on customary understanding rather than strict definition.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: under the early view, the person must offer kaffarah for breaking the oath; under the later view, no kaffarah is required. This affects fatwa in different regions.
The later view is generally preferred in non-Arab lands where custom is stronger.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and reasons
In contemporary Hanafi fatwa, especially in non-Arab contexts, the later view prevails because custom ('urf) is given weight. This is mentioned in sources like Fath al-Qadir.
The early view remains valid but is not commonly relied upon in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : أو وقف على سطحها؛ يعني إذا حلف لا يدخل داراً فصعد على سطحه من خارج ووقف هناك حنث، هذا عند المتقدمين، وذلك لأنَّ الدار عبارة عن ما أحاطت به الدائرة، وهذا حاصل في علوّ الدار وسفلها، وعند المتأخرين لا يحنث ؛ لأن السطح وإن كان من أجزاء الدار، لكن لا يقالُ للقائم عليه والصاعد إليه إنه داخل عرفاً. كذا في «الفتح». قوله : وقيل ... الخ ؛ وكذا إذا ارتقى شجرة في الدار من خارج الدار أو حائط من حائط الدار حنث عند المتقدمين خلافاً للمتأخرين. كذا في «البحر».
He said: 'Or stopped on its roof'; meaning if he swore not to enter a house and climbed onto its roof from outside and stood there, he breaks the oath according to the early scholars, because 'house' refers to what the boundary encloses, and that applies to its upper and lower parts. According to the later scholars, he does not break the oath because the roof, although part of the house, one standing on it or climbing to it is not called 'inside' customarily. As in al-Fath. (2) He said: 'And it is said...etc.'; similarly, if he climbs a tree in the house from outside the house or a wall from the wall of the house, he breaks the oath according to the early scholars, contrary to the later scholars. As in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between the early (mutaqaddimeen) and later (muta'akhkhireen) Hanafi scholars. The issue is: if a person swears 'I will not enter this house' and then climbs onto the roof from outside or climbs a tree in the courtyard or a wall from outside, does he break his oath? The early scholars say yes, because the definition of 'house' (dar) includes everything within its boundaries, both upper and lower parts. The later scholars say no, because in common custom ('urf), a person on the roof is not considered 'inside' the house; the term 'entry' implies being within the walls. This ikhtilaf has practical consequences for fatwa. The relied-upon position in later fatwa is the view of the later scholars (muta'akhkhireen) in non-Arab lands, as mentioned in some sources. The passage analogizes the tree and wall case to the roof case, showing consistency in the early and later positions.

Key Terms
المتقدمون (al-mutaqaddimūn): Early Hanafi scholars (e.g., Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and their immediate students).
المتأخرون (al-muta'akhkhirūn): Later Hanafi scholars (from the 6th century onwards) who relied more on custom and legal maxims.
الدار (al-dār): A building with a courtyard and boundaries, including its upper and lower parts.
عرف (‘urf): Customary usage; determines the common understanding of terms in oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the later Hanafi scholars' ruling that standing on the roof does not break the oath?
A) The roof is not part of the house.
B) Custom does not consider the roof as 'inside' the house.
C) The oath only applies to entering through the door.
D) The roof is considered a separate property.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes that custom ('urf) can override literal definitions in oath disputes, leading to different rulings between early and later scholars.

The Effect of Name Change on Oath: Distinction between Dar and Bayt
Explains the effect of name change on an oath about a specific building

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an oath on a specific house (dar) is not broken by entry after destruction if the new building is not a dar, but is broken if it is rebuilt as a dar, and distinguish the different rule for bayt.

Core Idea
An oath on a specific house sticks to the land; rebuilding as a house breaks the oath. An oath on a bayt sticks to the building; destruction ends the oath.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains a nuance in oaths: when a building is demolished and replaced, does the oath apply to the new structure? The answer depends on whether the original structure was a dar (house) or a bayt (room), and on definiteness. Understanding this helps in applying the correct ruling in real oath scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath
Determine what the person swore not to enter – is it a specific house (hadhihi al-dar) or a room (bayt)?
The wording of the oath determines the ruling.
2
Classify the building type
Is it a dar or a bayt? Dar primarily refers to the open space (arsah) with the building as an accessory; bayt's name is tied to the building structure.
This distinction is key in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Analyze the transformation
If the building is demolished and replaced with a different structure (e.g., mosque, bath, garden), does the new structure share the same name?
If the new name is different, the oath is not broken.
4
Apply the rule for dar
For a definite dar (al-dār), if it is rebuilt as another dar, the oath is broken because the land still carries the name. If rebuilt as something else, it is not.
For indefinite dar, destruction removes the name, so no breach.
5
Apply the rule for bayt
For bayt, the name is attached to the building. If the building becomes an empty plot, the name is lost; even if rebuilt as another bayt, it is a new name, so the oath is not broken.
The poet's line illustrates: 'The house is a house even if its walls are gone, and the room is a room…'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : كما لو جعلت...الخ؛ يعني لو حلف لا يدخل هذه الدار فانهدمت، وبني موضعها مسجد أو بستان أو بيت أو حمام وهو – بالفتح وتشديد الميم – : بيت يبنى للغُسل بالماء الحار، أو غلب عليها الماء فدخلها لا يحنث؛ لأنها لا تُسمّى داراً لحدوث اسم آخر لها، فإن انهدمَ الحَمّام ونحوه وصلح ذلك الموضع لبناء الدارِ فبنيت مرّة أخرى لا يحنث بالدخول فيها أيضاً؛ لأنه عاد له اسم الدار بسبب جديد، منزل منزلة الاسم الآخر. كذا في «الذخيرة». الفرق بينهما أن الدار اسم للعرصة حقيقة،وعرفاً والبناء فيها من التوابع والأوصاف إلا أن الوصف في الغائب معتبر، وفي الحاضر لغو فكأنه قال : لا أدخل هذه العرصة المبنية فيعلو الوصف مع الإشارة إذ الوصف للتعريف والإشارة أبلغ فيه. ينظر : رمز الحقائق (۱: ٢٥٧). قوله : أصلاً؛ أما في صورة المسجد والحَمام والبستان فظاهر؛ لأنه لا يقال لها: داراً، وأما في صورة بناء الدار الأخرى فلما في «تلخيص الجامع الكبير»: إنَّ العائد بصنع غير الأول، قال شارحه : أي الاسم العائد إلى الشيء بعدما زال بصنع جديد غير الأول؛ لأنَّ عود الاسم بسبب حادث غير السبب الأول، واختلاف السبب يوجب اختلاف المسبب. قوله : كهذا البيت.... الخ؛ وكذا في : لا يدخل بيتاً؛ لأنه إذا اعتبر وصف البناء في المعرف ففي المنكر أولى. والحاصل : إنَّ الدار يفرق بين معرفه ومنكره في أنه عند حلفه بعدم الدخول فيه يحنث بالدخول فيه بعد انهدامه في معرفه دون منكره، والبيت لا فرق فيه بين المنكر والمعرف، فإذا دخله وهو صحراء لا يحنث؛ لزوال الاسم بزوال البناء، قال الشاعر : الدار دار وإن زالت حوائطها والبيت بيت وإن ... كذا في «البحر» و«الذخيرة». قوله : لزوال اسم البيت؛ أما في صورة كونه صحراء فظاهر، وأما في صورة بناء بيت آخر فلعودِ اسم البيت بسبب جديد فينزل منزلة اسم آخر.
He said: 'As if it were made...etc.'; meaning if he swore not to enter this house and it collapsed, and its site was built as a mosque, garden, house, or bath—which is a house built for bathing with hot water—and he entered it, he does not break the oath because it is not called 'house' due to the occurrence of another name. If the bath or similar collapsed and the site became suitable for building a house and was built again, he also does not break the oath by entering it because the name 'house' returned due to a new cause, treated like another name. As in al-Dhakhira. The difference between them is that 'dar' is literally the open space, and customarily the building is part of its accessories and descriptions, except that description is considered for the absent, but for the present it is insignificant; so it is as if he said: 'I will not enter this built open space', with the description included due to the indication, as description is for identification and indication is more emphatic. See Ramz al-Haqa'iq (1:257). He said: 'Originally'; as for the case of mosque, bath, and garden, it is obvious because it is not called 'dar'; as for the case of building another dar, it is from Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir: 'The returning is by a different making', meaning the name returns after it was removed by a new making different from the first; because the return of the name is due to a new cause, and difference of cause necessitates difference of effect. He said: 'Like this house...etc.'; similarly in 'do not enter a house': because if the description of building is considered in the definite, then in the indefinite it is more so. The conclusion: for 'dar' there is a difference between its definite and indefinite forms: when he swears not to enter it, he breaks the oath by entering it after its collapse in the definite case but not in the indefinite; for 'bayt' there is no difference between indefinite and definite, so if he enters it while it is an empty plot, he does not break the oath because the name has gone with the building. The poet said: 'The house is a house even if its walls are gone, and the room is a room even if...' As in al-Bahr and al-Dhakhira. He said: 'Due to the removal of the name of the room'; as for the case of it being a bare plot, it is obvious; as for the case of building another room, it returns due to a new cause and is treated like another name.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage addresses the effect of a building's transformation on an oath. If someone swears 'I will not enter this house' and the house is demolished and replaced with a mosque, bath, garden, or a single room (bayt), entering does not break the oath because the new structure has a different name. However, if the site is rebuilt as another house (dar), entering does break the oath because the name 'dar' remains. The reason is that 'dar' primarily refers to the open space (al-'arsah) and the building is only an accessory; when the building changes, the name still applies to the land. For 'bayt' (room), the name is tied to the building itself, so if it becomes an empty plot, the name is lost. The passage also distinguishes between definite and indefinite 'dar': for a specific house (al-dar), entering after collapse breaks the oath because the name remains attached to the plot; for an indefinite house (daran), it does not. For 'bayt', there is no such distinction. The discussion emphasizes that legal rulings hinge on whether the name remains or changes.

Key Terms
الدار المعرفة (al-dār al-mu‘arrafah): A specific, definite house referred to by 'this' or with the definite article.
الدار المنكرة (al-dār al-munkarah): An indefinite, non-specific house.
البيت (al-bayt): A roofed, walled room without a courtyard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone swears not to enter a specific room (hadhā al-bayt) and the room is demolished and replaced with another room on the same plot, does entering the new room break the oath?
A) Yes, because the name returns due to a new cause.
B) No, because the name is lost with the original building and the new room is a different entity.
C) Yes, because the location is the same.
D) No, because the oath was on the building, not the land.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the effect of destruction on an oath depends on whether the name 'dar' or 'bayt' is used, and on definiteness, because the name determines the scope of the oath.

Distinction Between Definite and Indefinite 'Dar' in Oaths After Destruction
Clarifies the ruling on entering ruins after an oath for definite vs indefinite 'dar' and for 'bayt'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling on entering ruins of a house versus a room when the oath uses a definite or indefinite term, and apply this to real cases.

Core Idea
For a definite house, the location carries the name; for an indefinite house, the name vanishes with the building.

This passage refines a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh on oaths: whether swearing not to enter a 'house' is broken by entering its ruins. The answer depends on whether the house was specified (e.g., 'this house') or unspecified ('a house'), and also on whether the object is a house (dar) or a room (bayt). Understanding this nuance is essential for accurate fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object of the oath
Determine whether the oath refers to a specific house (definite) or any house (indefinite), and whether it is a house (dar) or room (bayt).
2
Apply the rule for dar
If the house is definite (al-dār al-mu‘arrafah), the name persists with the location; entering ruins breaks the oath. If indefinite (nakirah), the name is tied to the building, so ruins do not count.
3
Apply the rule for bayt
For a room (bayt), the name is always tied to the building; whether definite or indefinite, entering ruins does not break the oath.
4
State the practical consequence
The mufti must ask whether the oath mentioned a specific or generic dwelling to give the correct ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: واعلم...الخ؛ إيراد على ما ذكروه من الفرق بين الدار المنكرة والدار المعرفة، وحكمهم بالحنث بدخول دار خربة في المعرفة دون المنكر، وعلى ما ذكروه من الفرق بين الدار والبيت في ذلك، والبيت لا فرق فيه بين المنكر والمعرف، فإذا دخله وهو صحراء لا يحنث؛ لزوال الاسم بزوال البناء.
He said: 'And know...etc.'; an objection to what they mentioned of the difference between the indefinite and definite house, and their ruling that entry into a ruined house breaks the oath in the definite case but not the indefinite, and to what they mentioned of the difference between house and room in that regard. For the room, there is no difference between indefinite and definite; if he enters it while it is a bare plot, he does not break the oath because the name disappears with the building.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This is a critical refinement (hashiya) on the earlier discussion. The author notes that the distinction between definite and indefinite applies specifically to 'dar' (house) but not to 'bayt' (room). For a house, if it is specific (mā‘rifah), entering it even after it has become ruins still breaks the oath because the name 'dar' remains attached to the location. If it is indefinite (nakirah), the name is lost with the building, so entering ruins does not break the oath. For a room, whether definite or indefinite, the name is tied to the building, so if it is destroyed and becomes an empty plot, the oath is not broken. This distinction is important for accurate fatwa in oath-related cases.

Key Terms
الدار المعرفة (al-dār al-mu‘arrafah): A specific house (e.g., 'this house' or with definite article).
الدار المنكرة (al-dār al-munkarah): An indefinite house (e.g., 'a house').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Entering ruins of an indefinite house.
C) Entering ruins of a definite room.
D) Entering ruins of an indefinite room.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between definite and indefinite 'dar' in oath-breaking: only a definite house retains its name after destruction, making entry a breach.

Oath Not to Enter a House: Effect of Demolition and Definiteness
Commentary on rulings for oaths about entering demolished houses and the role of definiteness and description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on oaths regarding houses (dar) and rooms (bayt) to determine whether an oath is broken after demolition or rebuilding, distinguishing between definite and indefinite objects and the role of description.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'house' persists on ruins for definite but not indefinite; descriptions matter only if they are conditions or motivators.

When someone swears not to enter a house, what happens if the house is demolished or rebuilt? The Hanafi school gives nuanced answers based on two key factors: the type of building (dar vs bayt) and whether the noun is definite (e.g., 'this house') or indefinite (e.g., 'a house'). Additionally, descriptions like 'your house' may or may not affect the oath. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning from classical Hanafi texts, guiding you through the distinctions and their practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the building type
Determine whether the oath involves a 'dar' (house) or 'bayt' (room). The name 'dar' can survive demolition as ruins, but 'bayt' loses its name entirely when the building is gone.
Also note if it is a mosque, bathhouse, or garden – these are never called 'dar'.
2
Check definiteness (ta'rif/tankir)
If the noun is definite (with 'al-', 'hādhā', or pronoun), the object is considered 'present' (hadir). If indefinite (without al-), it is 'absent' (gha'ib). This affects how demolition and descriptions are treated.
For a definite 'dar', entering ruins breaks the oath; for indefinite, it does not.
3
Classify any description (wasf)
If the oath includes a description (e.g., 'your house', 'riding'), determine if it is a condition (shart) restricting the action, a motivator (da'i) for the oath, or merely identification (laghw). Only conditions and motivators are considered; laghw descriptions are ignored.
For present objects, descriptions are default laghw unless they are shart or da'i.
4
Apply the demolition rule
For definite dar: entering ruins = hanth. For indefinite dar: no hanth. For bayt (any definition): no hanth after demolition. If rebuilt by someone else, the name returns by a new cause, so no hanth either.
The reasoning for dar: the name remains on ruins; for bayt: the name is gone with the building.
5
Consider the commentator's objection
The commentator critiques the distinction between definite and indefinite dar, arguing that the same reasoning (name persists on ruins) should apply to both. However, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position retains the distinction.
This highlights the importance of the principle that descriptions in present objects are laghw unless they are conditions or motivators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان اليمين في الدخول والسكنى والخروج والإتيان والركوب أو دخلها بعد هدم الحمام وكهذا البيت ودخله منهدماً صحراء، أو بعدما بني بيتاً آخر أو دخلها بعد هدم الحمام) ، حيث لا يحنث ؛ لأنها لم تبق داراً أصلا". (وكهذا البيت ، ودخله منهدماً صحراء ، أو بعدما بني بيتاً آخر) ؛ فإنَّه لا يحنث لزوال اسم البيت". واعلم أنهم قالوا في لا يدخل هذه الدار فدخلها منهدمة : إنَّه يحنث. يكون مُسقفاً مُحاطاً بجدران، من دون أن يكون له صحن، فإن كان فيه صحن وما يتعلق به فهو المنزل، وإن كان فيه مع ذلك اصطبل ونحو ذلك فهو الدار. [1] قوله : أصلاً ؛ أمّا في صورة المسجدِ والحَمام والبستان فظاهر ؛ لأنه لا يقال لها : داراً، وأما في صورة بناء الدار الأخرى فلما في «تلخيص الجامع الكبير»: إنّ العائد بصنع غير الأول ، قال شارحه : أي الاسم العائد إلى الشيء بعدما زال بصنع جديد غير الأول ؛ لأنَّ عود الاسم بسبب حادث غير السبب الأول، واختلاف السبب يوجب اختلاف المسبب. [٢] قوله : كهذا البيت.... الخ ؛ وكذا في : لا يدخل بيتاً ؛ لأنه إذا اعتبر وصف البناء في المعرف ففي المنكر أولى. والحاصل : إنّ الدار يفرق بين معرفه ومنكره في أنه عند حلفه بعدم الدخول فيه يحنث بالدخول فيه بعد انهدامه في معرفه دون منكره ، والبيت لا فرق فيه بين المنكر والمعرف، فإذا دخله وهو صحراء لا يحنث ؛ لزوال الاسم بزوال البناء، قال الشاعر : الدار دار وإن زالت حوائطها والبيت (1) كذا في «البحر» و«الذخيرة». . تهـ [٣]قوله : لزوال اسم البيت ؛ أما في صورة كونه صحراء فظاهر، وأما في صورة بناء بيت آخر فلعودِ اسم البيت بسبب جديد فينزل منزلة اسم آخر. [٤]قوله : واعلم...الخ ؛ إيراد على ما ذكروه من الفرق بين الدار المنكرة والدار المعرفة، وحكمهم بالحنث بدخول دار خربة في المعرفة دون المنكر، وعلى ما ذكروه من (۱) (البحر الرائق (٥ : ٣٢٥).  ٤٨ عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية لأنَّ اسم الدار يطلق على الخربة، فهذه العلة توجب الحنث في لا يدخل دارا، فدخل داراً خَرِبةً، ثُمَّ فرقُهم بأن الوصف في الحاضر لغو فرق واه ؛ لأنَّ معناه أَنَّه إذا وصف المشار إليه بصفة نحو : لا يكلّمُ هذا الشاب، فكلمه شيخاً يحنث ؛ لأنَّ الوصف بالشباب صار لغوا. وفي قولنا : لا يدخلُ هذه الدار ، أو لا يدخلُ داراً، أين الوصف حتَّى يكون لغوا في أحدهما غير لغو في الآخر. الفرق بين الدار المعرفة والبيتِ المعرف، حيث حكموا بالحنث بدخول الدارِ بعد انهدامها وكونها صحراء، وعدم الحنث بدخول البيت بعد انهدامه. [1] قوله : لغو ؛ قال في «تلخيص الجامع الكبير» و «شرحه»: فالوصف يراعى في أي الغائب جزماً، وفي الحاضر إن كان شرطاً كإن دخلت راكبة، أو داعياً كدارك هذه ، فالأصل أنه إذا كان في الكلام وصف فالموصوف إما أن يكون غائباً – أي مبهماً غير متعين – أو حاضر – أي متعيّناً - ، فإن كان غائباً فالوصف يراعى فيه قطعاً بكل حال، سواء كان الوصف داعياً إلى اليمين أو لم يكن. وإن كان حاضراً، فإن كان الوصف شرطاً - أي قيداً للفعل المسند إلى الحاضر - ، كما في قوله : إن دخلت الدار راكبة، وقعَ حالاً ، والحال قيد للفعل فيكون شرطاً فيعتبر؛ لأنّ ذات الموصوف وإن كان مستغنياً عنه لكنه لتقييد الفعل فلا يكون لغواً. وكذا إذا كان الوصف داعياً إلى اليمين كما في قوله : لا أدخل دارك هذه، فإنّ وصف الدار وهو كونها منسوبة إلى المخاطب يصلح داعياً إلى اليمين ؛ لأنَّ الدار قد تعادى لبغض صاحبها فيراعى هذا الوصف، حتى لو باعها المخاطب، ثم دخلها الحالف لم يحنث. وإذا لم يكن شرطاً، أو داعياً، يكون لغواً ؛ لأنَّ الحاضر لا يحتاج إلى التعريف ، حتى يحمل الوصف على التعريف، وليس هاهنا معنى آخر يقتضي التقييد به فلغى. انتهى. [٢] قوله : أين الوصف...الخ ؛ حاصل إيرادات الشارح له أنّ تعليلهم بالحنثِ بدخول الدار المنهدمة في الدار المعرفة يقتضي الحنث في المنكرة أيضاً، والفرق المبني على عدم اعتبار الوصف في الحاضر، واعتباره في الغائب ليس بصحيح ؛ لأنَّ تلك الضابطة
Book of Oaths: The oath regarding entering, residing, leaving, coming, and riding. Or if he enters it after the demolition of a bathhouse, and likewise a house, and he enters it as a demolished desert, or after he built another house. Or if he enters it after the demolition of a bathhouse, he does not break his oath because it no longer remains a house at all. And likewise a house: if he enters it as a demolished desert, or after he built another house, he does not break his oath due to the removal of the name 'house'. Know that they said regarding 'Do not enter this house' and he entered it demolished: he breaks his oath. It is roofed and surrounded by walls without having a courtyard. If it has a courtyard and what pertains to it, it is a residence; and if it also has a stable and the like, it is a house. [1] His saying: 'at all' – as for the case of a mosque, bathhouse, and garden, it is obvious because they are not called 'house'. As for the case of building another house, according to what is in 'Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir': The returning [name] is by a different action. Its commentator said: meaning the name returning to the thing after it has disappeared by a new action different from the first, because the return of the name is due to an incident different from the first cause, and difference of cause necessitates difference of effect. [2] His saying: 'like this house...etc.' – likewise in: 'Do not enter a house' because if the description of construction (building) is considered in the definite, then in the indefinite it is even more so. The conclusion: The house (dar) differs between its definite and indefinite forms in that when he swears not to enter it, he breaks his oath by entering it after its demolition in the definite form but not in the indefinite. The room (bayt) does not differ between the indefinite and definite. So if he enters it while it is desert, he does not break his oath due to the removal of the name with the removal of the building. The poet said: 'The house is a house even if its walls are gone, but a room...' (It is in 'al-Bahr' and 'al-Dhakhira'). [3] His saying: 'due to the removal of the name of the room' – as for the case of it being desert, it is obvious. As for the case of building another room, it is due to the return of the name of the room by a new cause, so it is treated as another name. [4] His saying: 'Know that...etc.' – an objection to what they mentioned concerning the difference between the indefinite house and the definite house, and their ruling of breaking the oath upon entering a ruined house in the definite case but not in the indefinite, and concerning what they mentioned regarding the difference between the definite house and the definite room, where they ruled breaking the oath upon entering the house after its demolition and being desert, and no breaking of the oath upon entering the room after its demolition. [1] His saying: 'irrelevant' – it is said in 'Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir' and its commentary: The description is considered in the absent (unidentified) definitely, and in the present (identified) only if it is a condition, like 'if you enter riding', or a motivator, like 'your house this'. The principle is that if there is a description in the speech, the described thing is either absent (i.e., unspecified, not definite) or present (i.e., definite). If it is absent, the description is considered absolutely in all cases, whether the description is a motivator for the oath or not. If it is present, then if the description is a condition – i.e., a restriction on the action attributed to the present object – as in saying 'if you enter the house riding', it occurs as a state (hal), and the state is a restriction on the action, so it is a condition and is considered; because although the essence of the described thing is independent of it, because it restricts the action, it is not irrelevant. Likewise, if the description is a motivator for the oath, as in saying 'I will not enter your house this', because the description of the house as belonging to the addressee is suitable as a motivator for the oath, since the house may become odious due to hatred of its owner, so this description is considered. Thus, if the addressee sells it and then the one who swore enters it, he does not break his oath. And if it is neither a condition nor a motivator, it is irrelevant, because the present object does not need definition, so the description is taken as definition, and there is no other meaning requiring restriction, so it is irrelevant. End. [2] His saying: 'Where is the description...etc.' – the gist of the commentator's objections: Their reasoning of breaking the oath upon entering the demolished house in the definite case necessitates breaking in the indefinite as well. The distinction based on not considering the description in the present and considering it in the absent is not correct, because that principle...
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage from the Kitab al-Ayman (Book of Oaths) discusses the legal effect of oaths concerning entering houses (dar) and rooms (bayt) after they have been demolished or rebuilt. The commentator, building upon the matn (original text), explains the rulings and the reasoning behind them. The core issue is whether the oath is broken (hanth) when the thing sworn about has changed its physical identity or name. The text distinguishes between definite (ma'rifa) and indefinite (nakira) nouns, and between different types of buildings (dar vs. bayt). For a definite house (e.g., 'this house'), if the house is demolished and becomes a desert, entering it breaks the oath because the name 'house' can still apply to ruins; but if it is rebuilt by another person, the oath is not broken because the name returns due to a new cause. For an indefinite house (e.g., 'a house'), entering the ruins does not break the oath because the description of being a house is not fixed. For a room (bayt), whether definite or indefinite, if it is demolished and becomes a desert, entering it does not break the oath because the name 'bayt' is lost with the loss of the building; if rebuilt by another, the name returns by a new cause. The commentator then addresses an objection regarding the role of description (wasf) in oaths. The principle from Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir is that if the described thing is absent (gha'ib, indeterminate), the description is always considered. If it is present (hadir, determinate), the description is considered only if it serves as a condition (shart) restricting the action or as a motivator (da'i) for the oath; otherwise it is irrelevant (laghw). For example, 'I will not enter your house this' – the description 'your' is a motivator, so if you sell it, entering does not break the oath. In contrast, 'I will not enter this house' – the description 'this' is merely for identification, not a condition or motivator, so it is irrelevant; entering ruins breaks the oath. The commentator critiques this distinction as applied to the house cases, arguing that the reasoning for definite houses would also apply to indefinite ones, and that the claim about description being laghw in the present is flawed. The passage thus explores the interplay of language, intent, and physical reality in determining the fulfillment of oaths.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath; a binding promise or vow invoking Allah or swearing by Allah's name to do or not do something.
حنث (hanth): The act of breaking an oath, incurring sin and obligation of expiation (kaffarah).
دار (dar): A house; a building with a roof, surrounded by walls, possibly with a courtyard, stable, etc. Differentiated from bayt (room) and manzil (residence).
بيت (bayt): A room or a single dwelling unit; a covered structure without necessarily a courtyard. Its name is lost when the building is demolished.
وصف (wasf): Description; an adjective or attribute qualifying the object in the oath. It may be considered or ignored depending on context.
حاضر (hadir): Present; a definite object known by the speaker and listener (e.g., 'this house').
غائب (gha'ib): Absent; an indefinite or unspecified object (e.g., 'a house' without further specification).
شرط (shart): Condition; a restriction on the action, such as 'riding' in 'if you enter the house riding'. Such a description is always considered.
داعٍ (da'i): Motivator; the reason that prompted the oath, such as hatred for the owner of the house. If the description reflects the motivator, it is considered.
لغو (laghw): Irrelevant; a description that is not considered in the ruling of the oath because it serves no purpose (not condition or motivator).
تعريف/تنكير (ta'rif/tankir): Definiteness/indefiniteness; whether the noun is definite (with al- or demonstrative) or indefinite. Affects whether the description is considered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person swears 'I will not enter this house' (definite). The house is completely demolished and nothing remains but bare ground. He then walks across the plot. According to the Hanafi ruling in the text, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the name 'house' can still apply to the ruins, so the essence remains.
B) No, because the building is gone and the name 'house' is lost with it.
C) Yes, because the oath is indefinite and the description is irrelevant.
D) No, because he entered the plot but not a building.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths about houses, the definiteness of the noun and the type of building (dar vs bayt) determine whether entering ruins breaks the oath; descriptions are only considered if they are conditions or motivators.

Oath on Entering a Demolished House: The Distinction Between Definite and Indefinite References
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of demolition on oaths regarding houses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on oaths regarding demolished houses (dar and bayt) and distinguish the effect of definiteness (ma'rifah) versus indefiniteness (nakirah) on perjury.

Core Idea
In oaths, definite 'dar' nullifies the building descriptor; indefinite considers it—so entering a demolished plot breaks a definite oath but not an indefinite one.

This lesson dives into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about oaths involving houses. When a person swears not to enter a specific house (definite) or any house (indefinite), what happens if the house is demolished? The al-Hidayah relies on the linguistic custom that 'dar' refers to the plot, with the building as a descriptor. A later commentator, Sadr al-Shari'ah, raised objections, but Mulla Khusrow defended the Hidayah position. Understanding this distinction is crucial for properly applying oath rulings in changing circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the legal question
The act is swearing not to enter a house (dar/bayt). The legal question is: does entering the plot after the building is demolished constitute perjury?
2
Understand the definition of 'dar' according to al-Hidayah
'Dar' primarily means the plot (arsah); the building is a descriptor (wasf). This is supported by Arabic poetry and custom.
The descriptor is not an essential attribute but an added property.
3
Apply the rule of 'wasf' in definite vs. indefinite references
In definite references (e.g., 'this dar'), the descriptor is nullified (laghw) because the essence is identified by pointing. In indefinite references (e.g., 'a dar'), the descriptor is considered, so if the building is gone, the term 'dar' does not apply.
Nullification of descriptor in definite is a key Hanafi principle.
4
Distinguish between 'dar' and 'bayt'
'Bayt' is itself the building; 'bayt-ness' is a final cause, not an additional descriptor. For 'bayt', the same rule applies: definite nullifies 'bayt-ness', indefinite considers it.
The difference lies in what the term primarily names: plot vs. structure.
5
Analyze the objections and the Hanafi response
Sadr al-Shari'ah raised four objections; Mulla Khusrow refuted each, showing that the Hidayah position is consistent with linguistic custom and Hanafi principles. The mufta bihi is the Hidayah ruling.
The objections stemmed from misunderstanding the definition of 'wasf' and custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان اليمين في الدخول والسكنى والخروج والإتيان والركوب إنما هي * فيما إذا كان المشار إليه موصوفاً بوصف، كما إذا حلف لا أكلّم هذا الشاب فيلغو وصفُ الشباب، ويتعلّق اليمين بذاتِ الموصوف لما مر تقريره وفيما نحن فيه يعني : لا أدخل هذه الدار أو لا أدخل داراً، لا وصف حتى يحكم بإلغائه في المعرف دون المنكر، فهذان إيرادان أوردهما الشارح عليهم، والإيراد الثالث المذكورة بقوله : «ثم هذا المعنى...الخ. حاصله : إنه لو تم تقرير إلغاء الوصف واعتباره لزم الحنث في البيت المعرف أيضاً، وعدمه في البيت المنكر ؛ لأنّ البيتوتة وصف، فيلغو في الحاضر، ويعتبر في الغائب، فلا يصح فرقهم بين الدار المعرفة وبين البيت المعرف بالحنث في الأول في دخولها منهدمة، وعدمه في دخوله منهدماً. وحاصل الإيراد الرابع الذي أشار إليه بقوله : «ثم قالوا... الخ ؛ إنهم حكموا بعدم الحنث في : لا يدخل هذه الدار بدخولها بعدما انهدمت وبنيت حماماً، وعللوه بأنه لم يبق داراً، مع أنَّ إلغاءهم الوصف الحاضر يقتضي أن يحنث به في هذه الصورة. وقد رد محمد بن فراموز الرومي الشهير بملا خسرو في «الدرر شرح الغرر» على الشارح الله ، وأجاب عن إيراداته، حيث قال بعد نقل عبارة «الهداية» في مسألة الدار : لأنَّ الدار اسم للعرصة عند العرب والعجم ، يقال : دار عامرة، ودار غامرة، وقد شهدت أشعار العرب بذلك، فالبناء وصف فيها، غير أنّ الوصف في الحاضر لغو، وفي الغائب معتبر». انتهت (1) هذه عبارة «الهداية»، وتحقيقها : إن مراده بالوصف ليس صفة عرضية قائمة بجوهر، كالشباب والشيخوخة ونحوهما، بل ما يتناولها ويتناول جوهراً قائماً بجواهر آخر يزيد قيامه به حسناً له وكمالاً، ويورث انتقاصه عنه قبحاً له ونقصاناً، حتى فرقوا بين الوصـف والـقـدر كما سيأتي في أوائل البيوع»: بأنّ الأوّل ما يورث تشقيصه ضرراً لأصله، والثاني : ما لا يورثه، وجعلوا ما يساوي الذرع في المذروعات وصفاً، وما يساوي الكيل في المكيلات قدراً.
ثُمَّ هذا المعنى يوجبُ الحنث في لا يدخلُ هذا البيت، وعدمه في لا يدخل بيتاً إن دخله منهدماً صحراء ؛ لأنَّ البيتوتة وصفٌ فيلغو في المشار إليه ، فزوال اسم البيت ينبغي أن لا يعتبر في المشار إليه. وإذا كانت الدار اسماً للعرصة، وكان البناء وصفاً، وكانت الدار منكرة كانت غائبة ، فيعتبر فيها البناء، فإذا لم يوجد لم يحنث، وإذا كانت معرّفة، كانت حاضرة فلا يعتبر فيها البناء، وإذا لم يوجد يحنث. فإذا عرفت هذا فاعلم أنّ ما صدر هنا من صدر الشريعة له من الغرائب ؛ لأنه خالف جمهور الأئمة برأي غير صائب. أما أولاً فلأن قوله : «فهذه العلة توجب.... الخ ؛ ناشئ من الغفلة من قول صاحب «الهداية»: «غير أن الوصف في الحاضر لغو». وأما ثانياً فلان قوله : «لأن معناه أنه إذا وصف ... الخ ؛ ناشئ من الغفلة عن معنى الوصف، وقد مرَّ أنَّ البناء وصفٌ في الدار، كما صرح به في «الهداية». وأما ثالثاً فلأن قوله : «ثم هذا المعنى ... الخ ، غلط محض ناشئ من عدم التفرقة بين البيت والدار ؛ لأنه كما عرفت عبارة عن أمر زائد على الذات قائم به ، والبيتوتة ليست كذلك، بل هي علّة غائيّة لبنائه بخلاف الدار ، فإنّ البناء زائد على الدار التي هي العرصة. وأما رابعاً فلان حاصل قوله : «ثم قالوا... الخ ؛ إنّ الدار إذا كانت عبارة عن العرصة كان ينبغي أن يحنث فيما إذا بنيت حماماً ؛ لوجود العرصة، وهو فاسد ؛ لأنَّ الدار يطلق على العرصة المجرّدة، وعلى عرصة مع ما بني عليها من بناء الدار، فأما إذا بني . عليها من بناء غير الدار وتصرّف فيها تصرّفاً يزولُ اسم الدار عنه عرفاً، فلا يكون داراً، وكأن هذا الفاضل لم ينظر في ألفاظِ «الهداية» وعباراته ، فضلاً عن التأمل والتفكر في عباراته». انتهى كلامه(۱). [١]قوله : إن دخله ؛ قيد لكلّ من الحنث في المعرف، وعدمه في المنكر.
Book of Oaths: The oath regarding entering, dwelling, exiting, coming, riding... It is only * when what is pointed to is described with a descriptor, such as if he swore: 'I will not speak to this youth'—then the descriptor of 'youth' is nullified, and the oath attaches to the essence of the described, as previously established. In our case, meaning: 'I will not enter this house' or 'I will not enter a house'—there is no descriptor such that one rules to nullify it in the definite but not indefinite. So these are two objections raised by the commentator against them. The third objection mentioned by his statement 'then this meaning...' etc. Its summary: If the nullification and consideration of the descriptor were established, then perjury would necessarily occur in the known bayt (house) as well, and not in the indefinite bayt; because 'bayt-ness' is a descriptor, so it is nullified in the present and considered in the absent, so their distinction between the known dar and the known bayt is invalid—with perjury in the former upon entering it demolished, and not in the latter upon entering it demolished. The summary of the fourth objection indicated by his statement 'then they said...' etc.: They ruled no perjury in 'he will not enter this dar' upon entering it after it was demolished and built as a bath, and they rationalized that it is no longer a dar, despite the fact that their nullification of the present descriptor would necessitate that he perjures in this situation. And Muhammad ibn Famaruz al-Rumi, famous as Mulla Khusrow, in 'al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar' refuted the commentator (may Allah have mercy on him) and answered his objections. He said, after quoting the passage from al-Hidayah on the issue of the dar: 'Because 'dar' is a name for the plot among Arabs and non-Arabs—it is said: a populated dar, a ruined dar, and Arab poetry attests to that. Thus the building is a descriptor in it, except that the descriptor is nullified in the present and considered in the absent.' End. This is the wording of al-Hidayah, and its verification: That what is meant by 'descriptor' is not an accidental attribute subsisting in a substance, like youth, old age, etc., but rather that which encompasses it and encompasses a substance subsisting by other substances that increases its beauty and perfection, and whose diminishment causes ugliness and deficiency—so much so that they distinguished between the descriptor and the quantity, as will come in the beginning of sales: that the first is that whose division harms its essence, and the second is that which does not harm it, and they made that which equals the measure in things measured by cubit a descriptor, and that which equals the measure in things measured by volume quantity. Then this meaning necessitates perjury in 'he will not enter this bayt', and not in 'he will not enter a bayt' if he enters it demolished as bare land; because 'bayt-ness' is a descriptor so it is nullified in what is pointed to, so the loss of the name 'bayt' should not be considered in what is pointed to. And when the dar is a name for the plot, and the building is a descriptor, and if the dar is indefinite, it is absent, so the building is considered; if it does not exist, he does not perjure; and if it is definite, it is present, so the building is not considered; if it does not exist, he perjures. Now that you know this, know that what emanated here from Sadr al-Shari'ah is among strange things; because he opposed the majority of imams with an unsound opinion. As for first: because his statement 'this cause necessitates...' etc. arises from heedlessness of the statement of the author of al-Hidayah: 'except that the descriptor in the present is nullified.' And as for second: because his statement 'because its meaning is that when he describes...' etc. arises from heedlessness of the meaning of 'descriptor', and it has passed that the building is a descriptor in the dar, as stated in al-Hidayah. And as for third: because his statement 'then this meaning...' etc. is pure error arising from lack of distinction between bayt and dar; because it [the statement] as you know refers to something additional to the essence subsisting in it, and 'bayt-ness' is not like that; rather it is a final cause for its building, unlike the dar, for the building is additional to the dar which is the plot. And as for fourth: because the summary of his statement 'then they said...' etc. is that if the dar is an expression for the plot, then he should perjure when it is built as a bath, due to the existence of the plot; and this is corrupt; because 'dar' is used for the bare plot and for a plot with the building of a dar built upon it; but if something other than a dar building is built upon it and it is disposed of in a disposal that removes the name 'dar' customarily, then it is not a dar; and it is as if this virtuous man did not look at the words of al-Hidayah and its expressions, let alone pondering and reflecting on its expressions.' End of his speech. [Footnote:] His statement 'if he enters it' applies to both perjury in the definite and its absence in the indefinite.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding oaths involving houses (dar and bayt). The core issue is whether an oath not to enter a house is broken when the house is demolished and only the land remains. The author of al-Hidayah distinguishes between definite (mu'arrif) and indefinite (nankir) references. For 'dar', he defines it as the plot (arsah) itself, with the building being a mere descriptor (wasf). In a definite reference ('this house'), the descriptor is considered superfluous (laghw) because the essence is specified by pointing; thus, even if the building is gone, entering the plot breaks the oath. In an indefinite reference ('a house'), the descriptor (building) is integral, so if the building is gone, the term 'dar' no longer applies, and no oath is broken. For 'bayt', he similarly treats 'bayt-ness' as a descriptor, so in a definite bayt, the descriptor is nullified, but in an indefinite bayt, it is considered. The commentator Sadr al-Shari'ah raises four objections, arguing that al-Hidayah's position is inconsistent. Mulla Khusrow refutes each: 1) The objection that the cause should apply equally to definite and indefinite is based on misunderstanding, as the hidayah's rule is that descriptiveness is nullified in present references. 2) The claim that the building is not a descriptor is wrong; al-Hidayah explicitly calls it a descriptor. 3) The objection confuses 'bayt' with 'dar'; 'bayt-ness' is a final cause, not an additional attribute like the building on a plot. 4) The objection assumes that 'dar' only means the bare plot, but customarily it includes the building; once a non-dar building is erected, the name 'dar' is removed by custom. Thus, the hidayah's ruling holds. This debate illustrates how Hanafi jurists use linguistic custom and the concept of 'wasf' in oath rulings.

Key Terms
دار (dar): In this text, 'dar' is defined primarily as the plot of land (arsah), with the building being an additional descriptor. It can refer to either the bare plot or the plot with a house built upon it.
عرصة (arsah): The vacant plot of land without any building. This is the core meaning of 'dar' according to the hidayah.
وصف (wasf): A descriptor attached to a substance; in this context, the building is a wasf of the dar. It is nullified in definite references and considered in indefinite ones.
بيت (bayt): A home or dwelling; its essence is the building itself. 'Bayt-ness' (baytutah) is a descriptor that is nullified in definite references similar to dar's building.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, if a person swears 'I will not enter this house' (definite) and the house is later demolished to bare land, does entering the land break the oath?
A) Yes, because the building is a descriptor nullified in definite references, so only the plot matters.
B) No, because the house no longer exists, so the oath is not applicable.
C) Yes, but only if the land is still called 'house' customarily.
D) No, because the building is essential for the oath.

Key Takeaway
For definite oaths on 'dar', entering the plot after demolition breaks the oath; for indefinite, it does not.

The Hanafi Interpretation of 'Dar' in Oaths: Indefinite vs. Definite
Commentary on defining 'dar' in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between indefinite (nakira) and definite (ma'rifa) forms of 'dar' in oaths, state the resulting ruling when the building is ruined or changed, and apply the principle that the absolute is referred to the perfect (kamil).

Core Idea
An oath not to enter 'a house' is not broken by entering ruins; an oath not to enter 'this house' is.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah refines the earlier rulings on oaths regarding entering a house (dar). It explains that the indefinite form ('a house') defaults to the perfect inhabited house, while the definite form ('this house') refers to the specific entity regardless of condition. This subtle linguistic distinction determines whether the oath is broken when the building becomes ruined or is converted to another use. Understanding this requires applying the Hanafi usul principle that al-mutlaq yusrafu ila al-kamil (the absolute is referred to the perfect) and recognizing the role of ishara (pointing) in definite expressions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context and the two forms
The passage deals with oaths about entering a house. It distinguishes between the indefinite form 'dāran' (a house) and the definite form 'hādhihi al-dār' (this house). Recognize that the indefinite is general while the definite points to a specific entity.
The indefinite form is often used in oaths with a general scope.
2
Apply the principle al-mutlaq yusrafu ila al-kamil
For the indefinite form, the Hanafi rule is that an absolute (mutlaq) term is referred to the perfect (kamil) individual of its category. Here, the perfect 'dar' is an inhabited house (ma'mura). Thus, if the house is ruined, the indefinite oath is not broken.
This principle is based on istihsan and customary usage.
3
Recognize the effect of ishara for the definite form
For the definite form 'this house', the reference is determined by ishara (pointing) to the specific entity, irrespective of its condition. Therefore, even if the house is ruined, entering the land breaks the oath because the oath is tied to that particular location.
The definite form overrides the default interpretation of 'dar'.
4
Distinguish between ruin and complete change
If the house is completely changed to another type of building (e.g., bathhouse, garden), the name 'dar' is removed entirely. In such a case, entering does not break the oath even if the oath was in the definite form, because the identity of the place has changed.
A mere ruin retains the name 'dar' (though imperfect), so the definite oath is still binding.
5
Compare with 'bayt' (room/house)
The term 'bayt' is more specific; it is only applied to a built structure prepared for spending the night. It is never used for a ruin or empty plot. Consequently, whether the oath is indefinite or definite, entering a 'bayt' that has become barren does not break the oath.
This shows that different terms may have different legal implications based on linguistic custom.

Book of Oaths: The oath regarding entering, residing, exiting, coming, and riding. Then they said regarding: 'He does not enter this house, then he entered it after it was built as a bathhouse' that he does not break his oath because it did not remain a house. I say: The word 'dar' (house) is predominantly used for the inhabited house, and it may also be applied to the ruined one. So if one says: 'I will not enter a house', the preferred interpretation is that the inhabited house is intended. Also, the obligation to refer the absolute to the perfect necessitates intending the inhabited one. And if one says: 'He will not enter this house', then its building collapses, the correctness of applying it to the ruined one... The gist of the difference between the indefinite house and the definite house is that if he says: 'I will not swear by a house', the word 'house' is taken as the inhabited one because it is a perfect individual of house and because it is frequently used, so he does not break his oath if he enters it ruined. As for the definite, since it is pointed to, and pointing is to the essence regardless of other considerations, it is applied to the absolute house, whether inhabited or ruined. It is outweighed by the pointing, so he breaks his oath if he enters it ruined. And if another house is built on it, he breaks his oath by entering it. But if it is made into a bathhouse or garden, he does not break his oath because the name 'house' has completely left it. As for 'bayt' (room/house), it is only applied to a place prepared for spending the night... And that is because applying 'dar' to the uninhabited is correct without doubt, although this usage is rare compared to its use for the inhabited, and it is outweighed by the indication, so he breaks his oath if he enters it ruined. [...] He says: 'Zalat' (it left) means with a building other than the house's building, for although the 'dar' is essentially the courtyard, the name 'dar' is applied to it only if it has a house building or no building at all, but if another building is built on it, like a mosque, bathhouse, garden, etc., the name 'dar' is not applied customarily, not commonly nor rarely, so he does not break his oath by entering then. If you say: This would entail that if the bathhouse etc. collapses and another house is built there, and he enters it, he breaks his oath due to the existence of the name 'dar' then. I say: Since the name 'dar' completely left with the other building, building a house afterwards does not cause breaking of the oath because the name 'dar' is then established for a new reason after something that removed it intervened, so it is like another house different from the first which he swore not to enter. [...] As for 'bayt', it is not a name for the courtyard only, nor is it used for the ruined, not commonly nor rarely, but its usage is specific to the built structure, unlike 'dar' which is also applied to the ruined.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a critical refinement of the rulings on oaths related to entering a house (dar). The commentator explains the distinction between indefinite and definite 'dar' and the effect of the building being ruined or changed. He begins by stating the basic ruling: if one swears not to enter a specific (definite) house and it is later converted into a bathhouse, entering it does not break the oath because it no longer remains a 'dar'. He then provides a linguistic analysis: the word 'dar' is primarily used for an inhabited house but can also be applied to a ruined one. When the oath is indefinite ('a house'), it is preferably interpreted as the inhabited one because of the principle that absolute (mutlaq) is referred to the perfect (kamil). Thus, entering a ruined indefinite house does not break the oath. However, when the house is definite ('this house'), the reference is to the specific entity regardless of its state, so entering it even when ruined does break the oath, unless the structure is completely changed to a different kind of building (e.g., bathhouse, garden) which removes the name 'dar' entirely. He further distinguishes between 'dar' and 'bayt', noting that 'bayt' is exclusively used for a built structure prepared for staying overnight, so it is never applied to a ruined or empty plot. Consequently, whether the oath is indefinite or definite, entering a 'bayt' that has become barren does not break the oath. The commentary clarifies that these distinctions are based on customary usage ('urf) and the principle of istihsan, not on stringent logical necessity.

Key Terms
دار (dār): A house or dwelling, which in legal context includes the courtyard and building, but primarily refers to an inhabited structure.
بيت (bayt): A room or house prepared for spending the night (baytutah); it is only used for built structures.
مطلق (muṭlaq): An absolute or unqualified term; here referring to 'dar' without any definite article or specification.
كامل (kāmil): The perfect or complete instance of a category; for 'dar', it is an inhabited house.
منكرة (munkara): Indefinite (nakira) form of a noun; here 'dāran' (a house) without the definite article.
معرفة (ma‘rifa): Definite form; here 'al-dār' (the house) or 'hādhihi al-dār' (this house).
إشارة (ishāra): Pointing or indicating; in the text, it refers to the deictic reference (e.g., 'this house') that prioritizes the specific entity over the typical meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not enter this house', and the building is subsequently demolished but the land remains, does entering the land break the oath?
A) Yes, because the land is still considered the same house.
B) No, because the name 'house' no longer applies to bare land.
C) Yes, if the land is still enclosed by walls.
D) No, because the oath was only about the building.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between indefinite and definite forms of 'dar' in oaths: indefinite defaults to the inhabited house, definite refers to the specific entity regardless of condition, and complete change of building type removes the name and the oath's applicability.
