When a House Is Destroyed: The Oath Ruling
Explains rule when a house is destroyed and the legal distinction between definite and indefinite references

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath involving a house that is completely or partially destroyed, and explain the linguistic distinction between genus nouns and descriptive nouns that underpins the ruling.

Core Idea
A destroyed house is no longer a 'bayt' in law – the oath is not broken because the named entity no longer exists.

When someone swears not to enter a specific house, what happens if the house is later demolished? Intuitively, one might think the oath still binds the person from entering that location. However, Hanafi jurists ruled that the oath is not broken, because the term 'bayt' (house) is a genus noun that requires the physical structure to exist. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, including the distinction between descriptive nouns and genus nouns, and examines the edge case of partial destruction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is swearing not to enter a house (definite or indefinite). The ruling: if the house is completely destroyed, the oath is not broken by entering the plot.
Focus on complete destruction first.
2
Understand the legal definition of 'bayt'
'Bayt' is a genus noun (ism jins), not a descriptive noun. It refers to a physical structure with walls and roof that allows spending the night. If the structure is gone, the name no longer applies.
This is the core principle.
3
Analyze the objection and response
An objector argues that since 'bayt' is derived from 'baytutah', it is an attribute, and attributes are disregarded when a demonstrative pronoun is used. The author responds that 'bayt' is a genus noun, not a descriptive noun; the rule for genus nouns is different: the reality must persist for the name to apply.
This clarifies the linguistic basis.
4
Distinguish between complete and partial destruction
If only the roof is removed but walls remain, the name 'bayt' partially applies, leading to a difference of opinion. Due to doubt, the oath is not considered broken.
Partial destruction introduces complexity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا خَرِبَ، لم يصح إطلاق البيت عليه أصلاً، ولا يقال: إن البيتوتة وصف، والوصف في المشار إليه لغو؛ لأن البيت اسم جنس مع أنه مشتق من البيتوتة، وليس اسم صفة كالشَّاب ونحوه، فاسم الإشارة إذا دَخَلَ في الصفات يكون الوصف لغواً، نحو: لا يكلّم هذا الشاب، فكلمه شيخاً يحنث لكونها اسماً للعرصة حقيقة، فلهذا افترق الحكم بين الدار المنكرة وبين المعرفة على ما مر تقريره واتَّحد الحكم في البيت المعرف والمنكر، وهو عدم الحنث بدخوله منهدماً. [١] قوله: فإذا خرب؛ أي بالانهدام وصيرورته عرصة خالية عن البناء، أما لو هدم السقف فقط دون الحيطان فدخله حنث في المعرّف؛ لعدم زوال اسم البيت عنه، لإمكان البيتوتة فيه كصفة. أو نقول: اسم البيتِ ثابت لهذه البقعة لأجل الحيطان والسقف جميعاً، فإذا زالَ السقف فقد زال الاسم من وجه دون وجه، فلا تبطل اليمين بالشك. وعلى قياس الأوّل يحنث في المنكر أيضاً؛ لأنَّ اسم البيت لم يزل، وعلى قياس الثاني لا يحنث؛ لأنه بيت من وجه الحاجة هاهنا لعقد اليمين، فلا ينعقد عليه بالشك، بخلاف المعين، فإنّ اليمين كانت منعقدة على هذهِ العين، فلا تبطل بالشك. كذا في «الذخيرة». [٢] قوله: ولا يقال...الخ؛ إيراد على ما ثبت مما قبله من عدم الحنث في البيتِ بدخوله بعد انهدامه، وحاصله: إنّه قد تقرر عندهم أنَّ الوصف في الحاضر لغو، بمعنى أنه يتعلق الحكم هناك بنفس ذاتِ الحاضر، مع قطع النظر عن الوصف. ومن المعلوم أن البيتوتة وصف في البيت، فينبغي أن يلغو في المعرف، وتتعلق اليمين بنفس الذات، مع قطع النظر عن هذا الوصف، فيلزم الحنث بدخوله منهدماً أيضاً. [٣] قوله: لأنّ؛ علة لقوله: لا يقال وجواب عنه، وحاصله: إن البيت وإن كان مشتقاً من البيتوتة لكنه اسم جنس وُضِعَ لما يُبات فيه مع عزل النظر عن هذا الوصف، والضابطة المذكورة إنما هي في أسماء الأوصاف لا في أسماء الأجناس، والمعتبر فيها هو بقاء الحقيقة وزوالها.
When it is destroyed, it is not correct to call it a house at all. And it is not said: 'Baytutah (spending the night) is an attribute, and the attribute in the referred object is superfluous' because 'bayt' is a genus noun even though it is derived from baytutah, and it is not a descriptive noun like 'shabb' (young man) and similar. So when a demonstrative pronoun enters into attributes, the attribute becomes superfluous, like 'He will not speak to this young man' – if he then speaks to him as an old man, he breaks his oath because it is a name for the place (arṣah) in reality. For this reason the ruling differs between the indefinite house and the definite house as previously explained, and the ruling is the same for the definite and indefinite 'bayt': that he does not break his oath by entering it in its destroyed state. [1] His statement: 'When it is destroyed' – meaning by collapse and its becoming an empty plot without buildings. But if only the roof is demolished without the walls, then entering it breaks the oath for the definite case because the name 'bayt' has not been removed from it, due to the possibility of baytutah in it as an attribute. Or we say: The name 'bayt' is fixed for this spot on account of both walls and roof. So if the roof is removed, the name is removed from one aspect but not another, so the oath is not invalidated by doubt. By analogy with the first case, he breaks the oath in the indefinite case too because the name 'bayt' has not been removed. By analogy with the second case, he does not break the oath because it is a 'bayt' from one aspect. Here, for the purpose of the oath, the oath is not established upon doubt, unlike the definite [house], for the oath was established upon this specific entity, so it is not invalidated by doubt. Thus in al-Dhakhira. [2] His statement: 'and it is not said...' – an objection to what was established before, that he does not break the oath by entering the house after its destruction. Its summary: It has been established among them that the attribute in the present referent is superfluous, meaning that the ruling attaches to the essence of the present referent itself, disregarding the attribute. And it is known that baytutah is an attribute in 'bayt', so it should be disregarded in the definite case, and the oath should attach to the essence itself, disregarding this attribute, and consequently he breaks the oath by entering it in its destroyed state as well. [3] His statement: 'because' – a cause for his statement 'and it is not said' and a response to it. Its summary: 'Bayt' although derived from baytutah, is a genus noun placed for what is spent the night in, disregarding this attribute. The mentioned rule is only for descriptive nouns, not for genus nouns. The criterion is the preservation or removal of the reality.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling regarding oaths about houses. It addresses the case where a person swears not to enter a specific house, and then the house is destroyed. The ruling is that the oath is not broken because the name 'bayt' no longer applies to the ruined structure. The author first clarifies that if the house is completely demolished, the term 'bayt' cannot be used. He then responds to a potential objection: since 'bayt' is derived from 'baytutah' (spending the night), one might think that 'baytutah' is an attribute and, following the principle that attributes are disregarded when a demonstrative pronoun is used (e.g., 'this young man' – if he becomes old, the oath is still broken), the oath should remain. However, the author explains that 'bayt' is a genus noun, not a descriptive noun like 'young'. For genus nouns, the reality must persist. If the reality (the structure) is gone, the name is gone. He further discusses the difference between definite and indefinite references, noting that for a specific house (definite), the oath was on that exact entity, but for an indefinite house (e.g., 'a house'), the ruling is the same because the name no longer applies. The commentary also notes that if only the roof is removed but walls remain, the name 'bayt' partially remains, leading to a difference of opinion: by one analogy he breaks the oath, by another he does not; out of doubt, the oath is not invalidated.

Key Terms
البيت (al-bayt): A house, defined as a structure with walls and roof where one spends the night.
البيتوتة (al-baytutah): The act of spending the night, from which the word 'bayt' is derived.
اسم جنس (ism jins): A genus noun that names a category of things, not a specific attribute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'Wallahi I will not enter this house', and then the house is completely demolished, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the oath was on the specific entity.
B) No, because the name 'bayt' no longer applies.
C) Yes, because the house is still considered a house by its foundation.
D) No, because oaths are not affected by changes in the object.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling hinges on the classification of 'bayt' as a genus noun: when the physical reality of a house is destroyed, the name ceases to apply, and the oath is not broken.

Standing on the Threshold: Entry or Not?
Expands the base matn on oaths of entry/exit with threshold details.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on standing on a doorstep to determine whether an oath of entry or exit is broken, distinguishing between the doorstep's position relative to the closed door and the number of feet inside.

Core Idea
Standing on a doorstep breaks an oath only if the doorstep would be inside when closed; complete entry requires both feet inside.

This passage expands on a specific scenario in oaths: if a person swears not to enter or exit a house and then stands on the doorstep. The ruling hinges on where the doorstep lies when the door is closed – a seemingly minor detail that changes the outcome. Additionally, the text addresses a partial entry with one foot inside, resolving a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oath scenarios
Note that the passage covers both oaths not to enter and oaths not to exit. The ruling reverses depending on the scenario.
The key variable is the doorstep's position relative to the closed door.
2
Apply the 'closed door' test
Determine whether the doorstep would be inside or outside if the door were closed. If it would be outside, standing there is not entry (and vice versa for exit).
This is based on customary understanding of 'inside' and 'outside'.
3
Analyse the partial entry case
If one foot is on the doorstep and the other inside, check whether the inside foot is lower. The stronger opinion says no entry unless both feet are fully inside.
The minority view considered a lower inside foot as entry, but the tarjih favours absolute non-entry.
4
Connect to the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that standing on a threshold does not break an oath unless the doorstep is inside when closed, and entry requires both feet.
This ruling appears in Zahir al-Riwayah and is confirmed by later authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو هذه الدار، فوقف في طاق باب لو أُغلق كان خارجاً. [٢] قوله: أو هذه الدار...الخ؛ عطف على قوله: هذا البيت؛ أي لا يحنث إذا حلف لا يدخل هذه الدار بوقوفه في طاق باب - أي عتبته - التي بحيث لو أغلق باب الدارِ كانت العتبة خارجة؛ لأنّ مثل هذا يعد خارجاً من الدار، فلا يكون الوقوف فيه دخولاً. ولو كانت بحيث لو أغلق الباب كانت داخلة، حنث في حلفه لا يدخل، ولو قال: لا أخرج من هذه الدار فوقف في العتبة انعكس الحكم في الصورتين، ففي الوجه الأول يحنث، وفي الوجه الثاني لا يحنث كذا في «البحر»، وغيره. [٣] قوله: فوقف; أي بقدميه، فلو وقف بإحدى رجليه على العتبة وأدخل الأخرى فإن استوى الجانبان أو كانَ الجانب الآخر أسفل لم يحنث، وإن كان الجانب الداخل أسفل حنث; لوجود الدخول، وقيل: لا يحنث مطلقاً، وهو الصحيح; لأنَّ الانفصال التام لا يكون إلا بالقدمين كذا في «المحيط»، و«الظهيرية».
Or this house, and he stood on the threshold of a door such that if it were closed, he would be outside. [2] His statement: 'Or this house...' – conjoined to his statement: 'this house'; meaning he does not break his oath if he swore not to enter this house by standing on the threshold of the door – i.e., its doorstep – such that if the door of the house were closed, the doorstep would be outside; because such a place is considered outside the house, so standing there is not entry. And if it were such that if the door were closed it would be inside, then he breaks his oath in his oath not to enter. And if he said, 'I will not exit from this house' and he stood on the doorstep, the ruling reverses in the two scenarios: in the first scenario he breaks his oath, and in the second he does not. Thus in al-Bahr and others. [3] His statement: 'he stood' – i.e., with his two feet. So if he stood with one foot on the doorstep and brought the other inside, then if the two sides are equal or the other side is lower, he does not break his oath; but if the inside side is lower, he breaks his oath due to the occurrence of entry. And it is said: he does not break his oath absolutely, and that is the correct view because complete separation is only with both feet. Thus in al-Muhit and al-Zahiriyyah.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario in oaths about entering or exiting a house. The ruling: if a person swears not to enter a specific house, and then stands on the doorstep (threshold) in such a way that if the door were closed, the doorstep would be outside the house, then he does not break his oath because the doorstep is considered outside. Conversely, if the doorstep would be inside when the door is closed, standing there counts as entry and breaks the oath. For oaths about exiting, the ruling reverses. The commentary also discusses the case of standing with one foot on the doorstep and one inside: if the inside foot is lower, that is considered entry; otherwise, the stronger opinion is that no entry occurs because complete entry requires both feet inside. These rulings are cited from authoritative Hanafi works like al-Bahr, al-Muhit, and al-Zahiriyyah.

Key Terms
طاق باب (taq bab): The threshold or doorstep of a door.
عتبة (utbah): The doorstep, synonymous with 'taq bab'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the doorstep is inside when closed.
B) No, because the doorstep is still at the entrance.
C) Yes, if he has both feet on the doorstep.
D) No, because oaths are judged by intention.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, standing on a doorstep breaks an oath of entry only if the doorstep would be inside when the door is closed, and for partial entry, both feet must be fully inside.

Continuity as Initiation in Oaths
States that continuity of riding, wearing, and dwelling is like initiating them in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that continuity of an action is like initiation (al-dawam ka al-insha) in oaths and apply it to cases of riding, wearing, and dwelling to determine when an oath is broken.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, continuing an action is legally identical to starting it.

This passage introduces a key Hanafi principle regarding oaths: the continuity of an action is treated like its initiation. This means that if a person swears not to do something while already engaged in it, the oath is broken immediately. Understanding this principle is crucial for correctly applying oath rulings in everyday scenarios like riding, wearing, or dwelling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Consider a person who is already performing an action (e.g., riding a horse) and swears an oath not to do that action. The oath is uttered while the action is ongoing.
The act must be continuous; the person is in the middle of it.
2
Apply the principle of al-dawam ka al-insha
According to the Hanafi school, the continuity (dawam) of an action is legally equivalent to its initiation (insha). This means that for the purposes of oaths, the present continuous state is treated as if the person is starting the action anew.
This principle is cited from al-Bahr and Manh al-Ghaffar.
3
Determine the ruling
Since the oath is considered to cover the current moment, swearing not to do X while already doing X results in an immediate breach. The person has violated the oath right when they uttered it.
The consequence is that expiation (kaffara) becomes wajib.
4
Generalize to other actions
The same applies to wearing a garment while already wearing it, or dwelling in a house while already living there. The oath is broken instantly.
This principle is consistent across riding, wearing, and dwelling as explicitly mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو لا يسكنها، وهو ساكنها، أو لا يلبسه، وهو لابسه [٤] قوله: أو لا يسكنها...الخ; ذكر في «البحر» (۳) و«منح الغفار» وغيرهما: إنّ دوام الركوب واللبس والسكن كالإنشاء، فلو حلف لا يركب الدابة حال ركوبها
Or he swears he will not dwell in it while he is dwelling in it, or he will not wear it while he is wearing it. [4] His statement: 'Or he will not dwell in it...' – mentioned in al-Bahr (3) and Manh al-Ghaffar and others: that the continuity of riding, wearing, and dwelling is like initiation. So if he swears he will not ride the animal while he is riding it...
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This short passage introduces rulings on oaths about continuous actions. The base text gives examples: swearing not to dwell while dwelling, or not to wear while wearing. The commentary cites that in Hanafi fiqh, the continuity of an action (like riding, wearing, dwelling) is treated like initiating the action. Therefore, if a person already riding swears 'I will not ride this animal', he breaks his oath immediately because the continuity is considered as riding. The passage is incomplete here, but the principle is clear: one cannot use the fact that one is already doing the action to avoid the oath; the oath applies to the present continuous state.

Key Terms
الإنشاء (al-insha'): Initiating an action. In oaths, continuity (dawam) is legally equated with initiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle discussed, if a person is wearing a garment and swears 'I will not wear this garment', when does he break his oath?
A) Only when he takes it off and puts it on again.
B) Immediately because continuity is like initiation.
C) After a period of time, if he continues wearing.
D) He never breaks it because he is already wearing.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that 'al-dawam ka al-insha' in oaths means that an oath not to do something is broken immediately if uttered while already engaged in that action.

The Threshold in Oaths of Entry and Exit
Explains the fiqh of threshold in oaths of entering/exiting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether standing in a door threshold constitutes entry or exit in oaths, and apply it to cases where the threshold lies inside or outside when the door is closed.

Core Idea
In oaths of entry and exit, the threshold is part of the house only if it lies inside the closed door.

This passage clarifies a common scenario in oaths: what if someone swears not to enter a house but stands in the doorway? The Hanafi school gives a precise rule based on the physical position of the threshold relative to the closed door. You will learn how to determine whether the oath is broken, and the reasoning behind the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath type
Determine whether the oath is an entry oath (لا أدخل) or exit oath (لا أخرج).
2
Determine threshold position
Imagine the door closed. Is the threshold inside or outside the house? This is the decisive factor.
3
Apply the rule
For entry oaths: if threshold is inside, standing there is entry (oath broken); if outside, not entry (oath not broken). For exit oaths, the ruling is reversed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو هذه الدار، فوقف في طاق باب لو أُغْلِق كان خارجاً. قوله : أو هذه الدار ... الخ؛ عطف على قوله : هذا البيت؛ أي لا يحنث إذا حلف لا يدخل هذه الدار بوقوفه في طاق باب - أي عتبته - التي بحيث لو أغلق باب الدارِ كانت العتبة خارجة؛ لأنّ مثل هذا يعد خارجاً من الدار، فلا يكون الوقوف فيه دخولاً. ولو كانت بحيث لو أغلق الباب كانت داخلة، حنث في حلفه لا يدخل، ولو قال : لا أخرج من هذه الدار فوقف في العتبة انعكس الحكم في الصورتين، ففي الوجه الأول يحنث، وفي الوجه الثاني لا يحنث كذا في «البحر»، وغيره. قوله : فوقف؛ أي بقدميه، فلو وقف بإحدى رجليه على العتبة وأدخل الأخرى فإن استوى الجانبان أو كانَ الجانب الآخر أسفل لم يحنث، وإن كان الجانب الداخل أسفل حنث؛ لوجود الدخول، وقيل : لا يحنث مطلقاً، وهو الصحيح؛ لأنَّ الانفصال التام لا يكون إلا بالقدمين كذا في «المحيط»، و«الظهيرية».
Or this house, so he stood in the arch of a door which, if closed, would be outside. He said: 'Or this house... etc.'; it is conjunct to his saying 'this house'; meaning he does not break his oath if he swore not to enter this house by standing in the arch of the door - i.e., its threshold - which is such that if the house door were closed, the threshold would be outside; because such a thing is considered outside the house, so standing in it is not entering. And if it were such that if the door were closed it would be inside, he breaks his oath in his oath not to enter. And if he said: 'I will not exit from this house' and stood in the threshold, the ruling is reversed in the two cases: in the first case he breaks his oath, in the second he does not, as in 'al-Bahr' and others. He said: 'He stood'; meaning with his two feet. If he stood with one foot on the threshold and inserted the other, if the two sides are equal or the other side is lower, he does not break his oath; but if the inside side is lower, he breaks his oath due to the existence of entry. And it is said: he does not break his oath absolutely, and that is the correct view because complete separation is only achieved with both feet, as in 'al-Muhit' and 'al-Zahiriyya'.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on a matn about oaths. It clarifies the fiqh of standing in a threshold when one has sworn not to enter or exit a house. The key principle is that the threshold is considered part of the house if it lies inside the closed door, and outside if it lies outside. Therefore, if a person swears not to enter a house and stands in a threshold that would be outside when the door is closed, he does not break his oath. Conversely, if the threshold would be inside, he does break the oath. For oaths of not exiting, the ruling is reversed. The passage also discusses the case of standing with one foot: if one foot is inside, it may constitute entry depending on which side is lower. The correct view, according to the sources cited, is that one does not break the oath unless both feet are fully inside, because complete separation from outside requires both feet.

Key Terms
طاق الباب (taq al-bab): The arch or threshold of a door; the part of the doorway that serves as the boundary between inside and outside.
عتبة (atabah): The threshold of a door, the wooden or stone strip at the bottom of a doorway.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person swears 'I will not exit this house' and stands in a threshold that lies inside the closed door. Does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the threshold is inside, so standing there is considered exiting.
B) No, because the threshold is inside, so standing there is not exiting.
C) Yes, because any standing in the threshold counts as exit.
D) No, because exit requires stepping out completely.

Key Takeaway
In oaths, the threshold's position relative to the closed door determines whether standing there constitutes entry or exit, with the rule reversed for entry vs exit oaths.

Entry with One Foot: The Complete Separation Requirement
Explains the subrule on standing with one foot in threshold oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the Hanafi ruling on whether placing one foot inside the threshold constitutes entry, and explain the dispute and the preferred view.

Core Idea
Complete entry requires both feet inside; one foot alone does not break an oath of entry.

The previous lesson dealt with standing with both feet on the threshold. Here we consider a partial entry: one foot on the threshold and the other inside or outside. The Hanafi scholars have two views, and the preferred view is that entry is not established unless both feet are fully inside. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this position.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
He stands with one foot on the threshold and the other foot inserted inside (or outside).
2
Identify the two positions
First view: if the inside foot is lower, he breaks the oath due to entry. Second view: he never breaks the oath regardless of which side is lower.
3
Evaluate the preferred view
The correct view is that he does not break the oath, because complete separation from outside requires both feet to be inside.

Source Text

A) When the inside foot is lower.

Oaths of Continuous Acts: Immediate Cessation and the Zufar Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oaths of continuous acts with Zufar's objection and the general principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on oaths of continuous acts made while engaged, explain the reason for the immediate cessation requirement, and apply the principle that for continuous acts, continuity has the ruling of initiation.

Core Idea
For oaths on continuous acts, immediate cessation is required; the time to achieve piety is exempted.

This passage tackles a subtle yet common scenario: what happens if you swear not to do something you are already doing? Does a moment's delay break the oath? The Hanafi school, based on the purpose of oaths (piety), grants a brief grace period for cessation, but Zufar disagrees. Understanding this ruling helps you apply the broader principle distinguishing continuous from instantaneous acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath moment
Determine that the person swears while already engaged in a continuous act (dwelling, riding, wearing). The act has duration, so the oath prohibits the continuation.
Continuous acts are those that extend over time, as opposed to instantaneous acts like entering or marrying.
2
State the Hanafi ruling and the immediate cessation requirement
The person must take immediate steps to cease the act without delay. If he delays (e.g., an hour) without starting to cease, he breaks his oath.
Immediate cessation means beginning the process of stopping, not necessarily instantaneous completion.
3
Understand Zufar's objection
Zufar holds that any continuation, no matter how brief, constitutes the prohibited act and thus breaks the oath.
Zufar's view is a minority opinion within the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is based on the purpose of oaths: they are legislated for piety (birr). The time needed to achieve piety (i.e., to cease the act) is exempted from the prohibition.
This is a rational (aqli) argument; the exemption is not a textual concession but a legal principle.
5
Apply the general principle (dabitah)
The passage states a principle: What extends (i.e., continuous acts), its continuity has the ruling of initiation; what does not extend (instantaneous acts), continuity does not have that ruling. This governs the distinction.
Examples of instantaneous acts: exiting, marrying, purifying. For them, the oath only applies at the moment of initiation, not continuation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : أو لا يسكنها...الخ؛ ذكر في «البحر» و«منح الغفار» وغيرهما : إنّ دوام الركوب واللبس والسكن كالإنشاء، فلو حلف لا يركب الدابة حال ركوبها أو لا يلبسه، وهو راكبه، فأخذ في النقلة، ونزع ونزل بلا مكث) : أي إذا حلف لا يسكن هذه الدار، وهو ساكنها، فلا بُدَّ من أن يأخذ في النقل بلا مكث)، حتّى لو مكث ساعة يحنث، وهذا عندنا، وأما عند زُفَرَ له يحنث؛ لوجودِ السكني، وإن قل. قلنا : اليمينُ شُرِعَت للبر، فزمانُ تحصيل البري كون مستثنى. أو لا يلبس هذا الثوب حال لبسه أو لا يسكن هذه الدار حال كونه فيها، حنث لو مكث ساعة، لا دوام الخروج والتزوج والتطهير. والضابطة : أنَّ ما يمتد فلدوامه حكمُ الابتداء، وما لا فلا، وهذا كله لو كان اليمين حال الدوام؛ أي لو حلف وهو متلبس بالفعل، ولو حلف قبله فلا، فلو قال: كلّما ركبت فأنت طالق، أو فعلي دراهم، ثم ركب ودامَ لزمه طلقة ودرهم، ولو كان راكباً لزمه في كلّ ساعة يمكنه النزول فيها طلقة ودرهم. وذكر في «المجتبى»: إنّ في عرفنا لا يحنث إلا في ابتداء الفعل في الفصول كلها وإن لم ينو.
He said: 'Or not to dwell in it... etc.'; it is mentioned in 'al-Bahr', 'Manh al-Ghaffar' and others: The continuity of riding, wearing, and dwelling is like initiation. So if he swore not to ride the mount while he is riding it, or not to wear it while he is wearing it, then he takes to transferring, removing, and dismounting without delay. That is, if he swore not to dwell in this house while he is dwelling in it, it is necessary that he take to transferring without delay, such that if he delays an hour, he breaks his oath. This is according to us. But according to Zufar, he breaks his oath due to the existence of dwelling, even if it is little. We said: The oath was legislated for piety, so the time of achieving piety is exempted. Or not to wear this garment while he is wearing it, or not to dwell in this house while he is in it, he breaks his oath if he delays an hour, not the continuity of exiting, marrying, and purifying. And the principle is: What extends, its continuity has the ruling of initiation, and what does not, not. And all this is if the oath was at the time of continuity; i.e., if he swore while he is engaged in the act. But if he swore before it, then no. So if he said: 'Whenever you ride, you are divorced,' or 'upon me is a dirham,' then he rode and continued, a divorce and a dirham become incumbent. And if he was riding, then for every hour in which he could dismount, a divorce and a dirham become incumbent. And it is mentioned in 'al-Mujtaba': In our custom, he does not break his oath except at the initiation of the act in all categories, even if he did not intend.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about oaths involving continuous actions like dwelling, riding, and wearing. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) is that if someone swears not to do a continuous act while he is already engaged in it, he must immediately cease the act (e.g., take steps to move out, remove the garment, dismount). If he delays even for a moment, he breaks his oath. Zufar disagrees, arguing that any amount of continuation, however small, constitutes the act and thus breaks the oath. The Hanafi response is based on the purpose of oaths: they are legislated for piety (al-birr), and the time needed to achieve piety (i.e., to cease the act) is exempted. The passage then states a general principle (ضابطة): for acts that have duration (like dwelling, riding, wearing), the continuity of the act has the same ruling as the initiation; for instantaneous acts (like entering, marrying), it does not. This applies only if the oath is made while already engaged in the act. If the oath is made before starting, the ruling is different. The passage provides examples: conditional oaths like 'whenever you ride, you are divorced' — if the person is already riding, a divorce occurs with each hour he could dismount. In custom, however, he only breaks the oath at the initiation of the act.

Key Terms
دوام (dawam): Continuity; the ongoing state of an action that has duration, such as dwelling, riding, or wearing.
الضابطة (al-dabitah): A general principle or rule that governs multiple cases; here: 'What extends, its continuity has the ruling of initiation.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person swears 'I will not dwell in this house' while he is living there, what must he do to avoid breaking his oath?
A) He must leave the house immediately without any delay.
B) He must take steps to move out without delay, even if moving takes time.
C) He cannot avoid breaking the oath because he is already dwelling.
D) He must recite an expiation for the oath.

Key Takeaway
For continuous acts in oaths, immediate cessation is required; the time to achieve piety is exempted, and Zufar's stricter view is not relied upon.

Oaths When Already Engaged: The Hanafi Rule of Immediate Cessation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oaths when already performing the act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for avoiding hanth when swearing not to do a continuous action while already doing it, and apply it to residence, wearing, and riding.

Core Idea
To avoid breaking an oath on a continuous act, immediately cease; delay of even a moment causes hanth.

Imagine someone already living in a house suddenly swears, 'By Allah, I will not reside here.' Does he break his oath the moment he utters it? Or does he have time to pack and leave? The Hanafi school provides a clear answer: he must begin moving out without any delay. This lesson dissects the intra-Hanafi dispute with Imam Zufar, exploring the rationale behind the ruling and the crucial distinction between continuous and instantaneous actions in oath jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath scenario
Recognize that the person is already engaged in the prohibited act (e.g., residing, wearing, riding) at the moment of swearing. The oath is to cease that act.
The classic examples: 'I will not reside in this house' while residing; 'I will not wear this garment' while wearing; 'I will not ride this animal' while riding.
2
State the Hanafi position and the immediate cessation requirement
According to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad (Sahibayn), the swearer must immediately take steps to cease (e.g., start moving out, remove garment, descend). If he delays even a moment (e.g., sits for an hour), he breaks his oath.
"Immediate" means no delay at all; even a brief pause constitutes hanth.
3
Contrast with Zufar's position
Zufar holds that the mere existence of the prohibited act after swearing, regardless of duration, breaks the oath. Even if the action continues for a split second, hanth occurs.
Zufar's reasoning: the prohibition covers every moment of the act; any moment of continuation violates the oath.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil)
Oaths are legislated for birr (virtue/righteousness) – to enable the swearer to fulfill the sworn matter. Therefore, the time needed to achieve compliance (e.g., to move out, remove garment) is necessarily excluded. If hanth were instantaneous, it would be an impossible obligation (taklif ma la yutaq).
This is a rational proof (dalil 'aqli) based on the purpose of oaths.
5
Apply the rule: continuous vs. instantaneous actions
For actions that have duration (residence, wearing, riding), continuance has the ruling of commencement – so the oath applies to the continuation. For instantaneous actions (entering), the act is over at the moment of entrance; staying after does not break an oath not to enter.
The distinction is rooted in linguistic and legal usage: one says 'I rode for an hour' but not 'I entered for an hour.'

The Book of Oaths: The oath regarding entering, residing, exiting, coming, and riding. 'Or that he does not ride it while he is riding it, so he takes to moving, takes off, and descends without delay.' Meaning: If he swore that he would not reside in this house while he is residing in it, then he must take to moving out without delay; so if he stays for an hour, he breaks his oath. This is our view. As for Zufar, he breaks his oath due to the existence of residence, even if it is little. We said: The oath was legislated for virtue (birr), so the time of attaining virtue is excluded. 'Or that he does not wear this garment while he is wearing it, or that he does not reside in this house while he is in it; he breaks his oath if he stays for an hour.' Not continuous exiting, marrying, and purification. The rule is: that which extends, its continuance has the ruling of its commencement, and that which does not, does not. All this is if the oath was made during the continuance; i.e., if he swore while engaged in the act. If he swore before it, then no. So if he said: 'Whenever you ride, you are divorced,' or 'upon me are dirhams,' then he rode and continued, one divorce and one dirham become binding. If he was already riding, then for every hour in which he can descend, a divorce and a dirham become binding. It is mentioned in al-Mujtaba: According to our custom he does not break his oath except at the commencement of the action in all seasons, even if he did not intend. [1] His saying: 'in the transfer' – with a dammah on the noon and sukoon on the qaf – meaning transferring from that house after swearing not to reside. [2] His saying: 'and take off' – i.e., he removes that garment from his body immediately in his oath that he will not wear it. [3] His saying: 'and descend' – i.e., from the animal immediately after swearing not to ride. [4] His saying: 'he breaks his oath' – said in al-Hidayah: because these actions have continuity by the occurrence of their likes; do you not see that a duration is assigned to them; one says: 'I rode for a day' and 'I wore for a day', contrary to entering; because it is not said: 'I entered for a day' meaning duration and timing. And if he intended purely the commencement, he is believed because his speech is subject to it. [0] His saying: 'and that' – i.e., the non-breaking if he did not stay. [6] His saying: 'even if little' – the waw is conjunctive, meaning that residence existed even if little, so he breaks his oath. [7] His saying: 'we said' – a response to Zufar's statement, its gist: The oath was contracted for virtue, because if he stays in it for a day or more he breaks his oath; because continuance upon residence has the ruling of commencement. Likewise in 'he does not wear it while he is wearing it' and 'he does not ride it while he is riding it'. 'Or that he does not enter, so he sits in it; he does not break his oath by it, because entering is the transfer from outside to inside, so he does not break his oath by staying, contrary to residence, wearing, and riding, because in the state of staying he is resident, wearing, and riding. So from our saying: 'And it is said: in our custom he does not break his oath...' up to here, the ruling is non-breaking. [2] 'Unless he exits then enters' – this is an exceptive construct, of the type of circumstance. So his saying: 'unless he exits' means except exiting. And it was legislated because he is to perform the sworn matter, so there must be a time in which he can attain virtue. So it is excluded by necessity. If breaking the oath were binding for that amount, it would entail an impossible obligation. [1] His saying: 'by staying' – he indicates by it that the mention of sitting in the text is incidental, for the ruling is the same for any staying, whether sitting, standing, or lying down. [2] His saying: 'contrary to...' – its gist: Staying in residence, wearing, and riding, the resident, wearer, and rider are described by it. Staying in the house does not describe one as 'enterer'. Hence, he does not break his oath by staying in the case of entering, but he breaks his oath by staying in riding and the like. [3] His saying: 'So from our saying' – meaning that the author's saying in the issue of breaking the oath by standing on the roof while having sworn not to enter the house, and 'it is said: in our custom he does not break his oath', up to the issue of sitting while having sworn not to enter the house, is to clarify the non-breaking. So the ruling in the issues mentioned after that statement up to here is non-breaking. [4] His saying: 'munfarigh exceptive' – on the form of the passive participle, and it is that from which the excluded-from is omitted. Its gist: His saying 'unless he exits' means except exiting, because 'an makes what follows it infinitive, and it is a circumstance, so its meaning is except the time of exiting then entering, and the excluded-from is the times.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on al-Wiqayah, discusses the rulings of oaths when the swearer is already engaged in the act he swears to avoid. The passage is an expansion of the matn (base text) and includes an intra-Hanafi dispute with Imam Zufar.

The key issue: If someone swears not to reside in a house, not to wear a garment, or not to ride an animal, and he is currently doing that action, what is required to avoid breaking the oath? According to the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad), he must immediately cease the action. For residence, he must start moving out without any delay (even a moment). If he stays even for an hour, he breaks his oath. Zufar, however, holds that he breaks immediately due to the mere existence of the prohibited action, regardless of duration.

The Hanafi proof: Oaths are legislated for the purpose of fulfilling righteousness (birr), i.e., to achieve the sworn matter. Therefore, the time needed to achieve that is excluded by necessity. If breaking the oath were instantaneous, it would be an impossible obligation (taklif ma la yutaq). The principle is: For actions that have duration (like residing, wearing, riding), the continuance has the same ruling as the commencement—so the prohibition applies to the continuation. But for instantaneous actions (like entering), the prohibition applies only at the moment of the act; staying afterwards does not break the oath. Hence, if one swears not to enter a house and then sits inside, he does not break his oath unless he leaves and re-enters. This is because 'entering' is a single transfer from outside to inside, not a continuous state.

The passage also mentions a custom (urf) reported in al-Mujtaba: In our custom, he does not break the oath except at the commencement of the action in all cases, even without intention. This indicates that the ruling may be applied according to local custom.

The commentary clarifies that the mention of 'sitting' in the matn is incidental; the same ruling applies to any form of staying (standing, lying). The exception 'unless he exits then enters' is explained as an exceptive construct (istithna mufarrag) where the excluded-from is omitted, meaning 'except the time of exiting then entering'.

In summary, the Hanafi school distinguishes between continuous and non-continuous actions in oaths of avoidance when already engaged, based on the purpose of oaths and practical feasibility.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath; a binding statement by which one swears to do or not do something, often with legal consequences if broken.
الحنث (al-hanth): Breaking the oath by doing or failing to do the sworn matter.
السكنى (al-sukna): Residence; continuous dwelling in a place.
الدخول (al-dukhul): Entering; the act of moving from outside to inside, considered an instantaneous act in this context.
الاستثناء المفرغ (al-istithna' al-mufarrag): An exceptive construction where the excluded-from is omitted, leaving the exceptive particle (illa) to operate on the remaining clause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone swears 'I will not wear this garment' while he is already wearing it, what must he do to avoid breaking his oath?
A) He must remove it immediately without any delay.
B) He must wear it for at least one hour before removing it.
C) He must keep wearing it until the end of the day.
D) He does not break his oath regardless of what he does.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires immediate cessation without any delay when swearing not to do a continuous act already in progress; any delay breaks the oath, but the time needed to actually cease is excluded by the purpose of oaths (birr).

Three Hanafi Views on When an Oath of Non-Dwelling is Broken
Intra-Hanafi dispute on when oath of non-dwelling is broken

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on when an oath of non-dwelling is broken, explain the dalil for each, and identify the mufta bihi position according to later authorities.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school has three views; the preferred view is that only essential belongings matter for breaking the oath.

When a person swears not to dwell in a house, what exactly constitutes 'dwelling'? The Hanafi imams differed on whether the oath is broken only when all belongings are removed, most belongings, or just what is necessary. This lesson unpacks that intra-madhhab dispute and shows how later scholars resolved it based on custom (urf).

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the issue
The oath is 'I will not dwell in this house.' The question is: when does the oath become broken (hanth)? The answer depends on what is meant by 'dwelling' in customary terms.
The ruling triggers kaffarah (expiation) upon hanth.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and dalil
Abu Hanifa holds that the oath is broken only when all belongings, even a peg, are removed. His dalil is the literal custom: dwelling means full occupation.
This is the strictest view.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and dalil
Abu Yusuf considers removal of the majority sufficient, as removing everything may be impractical (ta'addur). The majority takes the ruling of the whole.
A middle position based on necessity.
4
State Muhammad's position and dalil
Muhammad considers removal of what is necessary for dwelling (e.g., essential household goods). Dwelling is defined by items that make habitation possible.
This is the most lenient view.
5
Analyze the tarjih among later scholars
Later scholars differed on preference. Al-Hidayah and Fath al-Qadir preferred Muhammad's view as more convenient and aligned with custom. Al-Nahr argued that custom (urf) is the criterion: if a person leaves with essentials and intends not to return, he is no longer a dweller.
The mufta bihi is Muhammad's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي لا يسكن هذه الدار، لا بد من خروجه بأهله ومتاعه أجمع حتى يحنث بوتد بقي. هذا عند أبي حنيفة. وأما عند أبي يوسف، فيعتبر نقل الأكثر. وأما عند محمد الله فيعتبر ما يقوم به، كَدْخُدَائِتِه، قالوا: هذا أحسن، وأرفق بالناس. [1] قوله: وأما عند أبي يوسف له الخ؛ وجه قول أبي يوسف له: إنه قد يتعذر نقل الكل فيعتبر الأكثر، وللأكثر حكم الكل، وقد مر وجه قول أبي حنيفة، ووجه قول محمد الله: إن الاعتبار في السكنى وعدم السكنى إنما هو ما تقوم به السكنى، فلا ضرر في بقاء ما وراء ذلك. وقد اختلف المشايخ في ترجيح هذه الأقوال، فرجح الفقيه أبو الليث قول أبي حنيفة، واستثنى منه المشايخ ما لا يتأتى به السكنى كقطعة حصير، ووتد، وذكر صاحب «المحيط» و «الفوائد الظهيرية» و«الكافي»: إن الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف الله. ورجح في «الهداية» قول محمد له بأنه أحسن وأرفق، وصرح في «الفتح» وغيره: إن الفتوى عليه. كذا في البحر وفيه أيضاً: الإفتاء بقول الإمام الله لأنه أحوط، وإن كان غيره أرفق. ورد عليه في «النهر» بأنه ليس المدار إلا على العرف، ولا شك أن من خرج على نية ترك المكان وعدم العود إليه، ونقل من أمتعته ما يقوم به أمر سكناه، وهو على نية نقل الباقي، يقال: ليس ساكناً فيه، بل انتقل منه وسكن في المكان الفلاني، وبهذا يترجح قول محمد له.
And regarding [the oath] 'I will not dwell in this house', it is necessary that he moves out with his family and all his belongings, even a peg that remains, for him to break the oath. This is according to Abu Hanifa. As for Abu Yusuf, the removal of the majority is considered. As for Muhammad, what is necessary for dwelling is considered, like his household goods. They said: this is better and more convenient for the people. [Commentary:] The reasoning for Abu Yusuf is that removing everything may be difficult, so the majority is considered, and the majority has the ruling of the whole. The reasoning for Abu Hanifa has already passed. The reasoning for Muhammad is that the consideration in dwelling and non-dwelling is only what constitutes dwelling, so there is no harm in the remainder. The scholars differed in preferring these views: al-Faqih Abu al-Layth preferred Abu Hanifa's view, but the scholars excepted from it what does not enable dwelling, like a piece of mat or a peg. The author of al-Muhit, al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyya, and al-Kafi stated that the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view. In al-Hidaya, Muhammad's view was preferred as being better and more convenient, and it was stated in al-Fath and others that the fatwa is on it. So it is in al-Bahr, and in it also: giving fatwa on Abu Hanifa's view is more precautionary, even if other views are more convenient. But al-Nahr rebutted this, saying that the only criterion is custom, and there is no doubt that one who leaves with the intention of departing and not returning, and takes what is necessary for his dwelling, while intending to take the rest, is not considered a dweller there, but rather has moved and resides elsewhere. By this the view of Muhammad is preferred.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific oath: a person swears 'I will not dwell in this house.' The question is: when does the oath become broken (hanth)? The answer depends on what constitutes 'dwelling' in customary terms. The passage presents the intra-Hanafi dispute. Abu Hanifa holds that the oath is broken only when the person removes all belongings, including even a peg, because the presence of any belonging indicates continued dwelling. Abu Yusuf considers the removal of the majority of belongings sufficient, as removing everything may be impractical. Muhammad considers the removal of what is necessary for dwelling (e.g., essential furniture), arguing that dwelling is defined by the presence of items that make habitation possible. The commentators then discuss which view is preferred for fatwa. Some prefer Abu Hanifa's view as precautionary (ahwat). Others prefer Abu Yusuf's view as practical. Al-Hidayah and later works like Fath al-Qadir prefer Muhammad's view as more in line with custom and convenience. Al-Nahr argues that custom ('urf) is the criterion: if a person leaves with the intention of not returning and takes what is necessary for dwelling, he is no longer a dweller. Thus, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Muhammad's, although there is ikhtilaf among the later scholars.

Key Terms
سكنى (sukna): Dwelling or residing in a house; the act of living in a place.
حنث (hanth): The breaking or violation of an oath, triggering the expiation (kaffarah).
الكدخدائية (al-kadkhuda'iyyah): Household goods necessary for dwelling, derived from Persian 'khaneh dari' (housekeeping).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (mufta bihi) in this passage, when is an oath 'I will not dwell in this house' considered broken?
A) When all belongings are removed, including a peg.
B) When most belongings are removed.
C) When what is necessary for dwelling is removed.
D) When the person physically leaves the house.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position for oaths of non-dwelling is that the oath is broken when essential belongings are removed, in line with custom (urf).

Transfer of Residence in Oaths: The Three Hanafi Positions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the criterion for transferring residence in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions on what constitutes moving out in an oath not to reside, and identify the mufta bihi position with its rationale.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, moving house means transferring enough belongings to dwell—Muhammad's view, based on custom, is the relied-upon position.

When someone swears not to live in a house, what exactly constitutes moving out? The Hanafi school has three distinct answers from its founding imams. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and shows how later authorities settled on the most practical view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The core question: when a person swears not to reside in a house, what act of moving belongings breaks the oath? The answer determines whether expiation (kaffarah) is required.
This is a condition-dependent ruling; the oath is broken upon fulfillment of the transfer criterion.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his evidence
Abu Hanifa held that total transfer of all belongings and family is required. His reasoning: complete departure demands complete removal.
This is the strictest view, later softened by excluding trivial items.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and evidence
Abu Yusuf considered transferring the majority sufficient, because the majority has the same ruling as the whole, especially when total transfer is difficult.
Supported by the principle that difficulty brings ease.
4
State Muhammad's position and evidence
Muhammad al-Shaybani held that only what is necessary for dwelling must be moved. The criterion is custom: what enables habitation.
This view became mufta bihi due to its harmony with 'urf and convenience.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and identify the mufta bihi
Later authorities (al-Hidayah, Fath al-Qadir, al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) preferred Muhammad's view. The refutation in al-Nahr emphasizes that custom is the sole criterion.
Despite Abu Hanifa's view being more precautionary, Muhammad's view is standard for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عند أبي يوسف، فيعتبر نقل الأكثر. وأما عند محمد الله فيعتبرُ ما يقوم به، كَدْخُدَائِيتِه، قالوا : هذا أحسن، وأرفق بالناس. قوله : وأما عند أبي يوسف له الخ؛ وجه قول أبي يوسف له : إنه قد يتعدَّرُ نقل الكل فيعتبر الأكثر ، وللأكثر حكم الكلّ ، وقد مرَّ وجه قول أبي حنيفة ، ووجه قول محمد الله : إنّ الاعتبار في السكنى وعدم السكنى إنّما هو ما تقوم به السكني، فلا ضرر في بقاء ما وراء ذلك. وقد اختلف المشايخ في ترجيح هذه الأقوال، فرجح الفقيه أبو الليث قول أبي حنيفة ، واستثنى منه المشايخ ما لا يتأتى به السكنى كقطعة حصير، ووتد، وذكر صاحب «المحيط» و «الفوائد الظهيرية» و«الكافي»: إنّ الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف الله. ورجح في «الهداية» قول محمد له بأنه أحسن وأرفق، وصرّح في «الفتح» وغيره : إن الفتوى عليه. كذا في البحر وفيه أيضاً : الإفتاء بقول الإمام الله لأنه أحوط ، وإن كان غيره أرفق. وردّ عليه في «النهر» بأنه ليس المدارُ إلا على العرف، ولا شَكٍّ أنَّ مَن خرج على نية ترك المكان وعدم العود إليه، ونقل من أمتعته ما يقوم به أمر سكناه ، وهو على نية نقل الباقي ، يقال : ليس ساكناً فيه، بل انتقل منه وسكن في المكان الفلاني، وبهذا يترجح قول محمد له. قوله : كَدْخُدائيته؛ الكَدْخُدائية : - بفتح الكاف وسكون الدال المهملة، الخاء المعجمة، ثمّ دال مهملة بعدها ألف بعدها،همزة، بعدها ياء مثناه تحتية وضم مشدّدة - بالفارسية : خانه داری
As for according to Abu Yusuf, he considers the transfer of the majority. As for according to Muhammad [al-Shaybani], he considers what suffices for dwelling, like his housekeeping. They said: this is better and more convenient for people. His statement: 'As for according to Abu Yusuf' etc.; the reasoning of Abu Yusuf: it may be difficult to transfer everything, so the majority is considered, and the majority has the ruling of the whole. The reasoning of Abu Hanifa has already passed. The reasoning of Muhammad [al-Shaybani]: the consideration in dwelling and not dwelling is what establishes dwelling, so there is no harm in leaving behind what is beyond that. The scholars differed on the preference of these statements. Faqih Abu al-Layth preferred Abu Hanifa's statement, but the scholars excluded from it what does not enable dwelling, like a piece of mat and a peg. The author of 'al-Muhit', 'al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyya', and 'al-Kafi' mentioned that the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's statement. In 'al-Hidaya', Muhammad's statement was preferred because it is better and more convenient, and it was stated in 'al-Fath' and others that the fatwa is upon it. Thus in 'al-Bahr'. Also in it: giving fatwa by Imam Abu Hanifa's statement because it is more precautionary, although others are more convenient. It was refuted in 'al-Nahr' by saying that the only criterion is custom, and there is no doubt that one who leaves with the intention of leaving the place and not returning, and transfers enough of his belongings to manage his dwelling, intending to transfer the rest, it is said: he is not dwelling in it, rather he has moved from it and dwells in such-and-such place. By this, Muhammad's statement is preferred. His statement: 'like his housekeeping'; 'al-kadkhuda'iyya': with fathah on the kaf, sukun on the dal, the first letter with 'i'jam, then a dal with fatha after an alif, then a hamza, then a ya with tashdid and dammah - in Persian: housekeeping.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding what constitutes moving out (nuqla) when one swears not to live in a particular house. Three positions are presented: (1) Abu Hanifa originally held that one must move all belongings and family; a total transfer. (2) Abu Yusuf held that moving the majority suffices, because the majority has the same ruling as the whole, especially when total transfer is difficult. (3) Muhammad al-Shaybani held that it suffices to move what is necessary for dwelling, as the legal criterion for 'dwelling' is having sufficient items to reside; leaving behind non-essential items does not break the oath. The scholars then discuss which view is preferred for fatwa. Some preferred Abu Hanifa's view for caution, but with an exception for items too insignificant for dwelling (e.g., a small mat or peg). Others favored Abu Yusuf's view, citing convenience. The majority of later authorities, including 'al-Hidayah' and 'Fath al-Qadir', held Muhammad's view as the most reasonable and practical, based on custom (urf). The Persian term 'kadkhuda'iyya' is explained as 'housekeeping' (خانه داری), referring to the minimal household items needed for habitation.

Key Terms
السكنى (al-sukna): Dwelling or residence; the legal concept of inhabiting a place.
كدخدائية (kadkhuda'iyya): Persian term for housekeeping or household management; here means the minimal belongings needed for dwelling.
نقل الأكثر (naql al-akthar): Transfer of the majority of belongings; Abu Yusuf's criterion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the Hanafi school (mufta bihi), what constitutes moving out of a house when one swears not to live there?
A) Moving all belongings and family.
B) Moving the majority of belongings.
C) Moving only what is necessary for dwelling.
D) Simply leaving the house without any belongings.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths of residence, moving sufficient belongings for dwelling is the operative criterion for breaking the oath.

Fulfilling an Oath Not to Live in a City or Village
States the ruling for city/village oath fulfillment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on what suffices to fulfill an oath not to dwell in a city or village, and distinguish it from the ruling for a specific house.

Core Idea
For a city or village, personal departure with intention not to return suffices; moving belongings is unnecessary.

This lesson clarifies a distinction in the Hanafi school regarding oaths of dwelling. While leaving a specific house requires moving oneself, belongings, and dependents, leaving a larger settlement like a city or village is more lenient. Understanding this distinction prevents unnecessary hardship when fulfilling oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath
Recognize that the person has sworn not to reside in a particular city or village (e.g., 'I will not live in Kufa').
The oath must be explicit and binding.
2
State the sufficing action
Personal departure from the city or village, with a genuine intention not to return, is sufficient to break the oath.
Moving family or belongings is not required.
3
Contrast with a house
If the oath concerns a specific house, personal departure plus moving all belongings and dependents is necessary.
This is the general rule, as per al-Quduri in al-Dhakhira.
4
Understand the condition
The intention not to return is a necessary condition (shart) for fulfillment; mere exit without resolve is insufficient.
The intention is evaluated inwardly; if one returns soon, the oath may be violated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلاف المصر والقرية : فإنَّه لا يشترط نقل الأهل والمتاع. قوله : بخلاف؛ يعني إذا حلف لا يسكنُ هذا المصر أو هذه القرية يكفي في البر حينئذ أن يخرج بنفسه من دون اشتراط نقل الأمتعة والأولاد، ولكن يشترط فيه أن يكون في نيته عدم العود. قوله : فإنه لا يشترط؛ قال في «الذخيرة»: قال القُدُوري : الخروج من الدار المسكونة، أن يخرج بنفسه ومتاعه وعياله، والخروج من البلدة والقرية أن يخرج ببدنه خاصة.
Unlike the city and the village: for them, transferring family and belongings is not required. His statement: 'unlike'; meaning if he swears not to dwell in this city or this village, then it suffices for him to fulfill the oath by exiting himself without requiring transfer of belongings and children, but it is required that his intention be not to return. His statement: 'for them it is not required'; it is said in 'al-Dhakhira': al-Quduri said: exit from an inhabited house: that he exits with himself, his belongings, and his dependents; exit from a town or village: that he exits with his body alone.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage clarifies an exception to the previous dispute. For oaths concerning a city (misr) or village (qarya), the ruling is different: one does not need to move family or belongings. Simply leaving the locality with one's body suffices to break the oath, provided one intends not to return. This is based on the understanding that a city or village is a larger area where one's residence is defined by physical presence, not by the location of movable property. The quotation from al-Dhakhira citing al-Quduri confirms this distinction: exiting a specific house requires moving person, belongings, and dependents; exiting a city or village only requires personal exit.

Key Terms
المصر (al-misr): A city or metropolitan area; in this context, any large settlement.
القرية (al-qarya): A village or smaller settlement; ruling same as city.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Moving all belongings and family to another city.
B) Exiting the city with the intention not to return.
C) Exiting the city without any intention.
D) Moving the majority of belongings.

Key Takeaway
For oaths concerning cities or villages, personal departure with the intention not to return suffices; no need to move family or belongings, unlike a specific house.

Oath Not to Dwell in a Town: Exiting with Body Only
Ruling on oath fulfillment for town vs. house

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fulfilling an oath not to dwell in a town, distinguishing it from the ruling for a house.

Core Idea
For a town oath, only personal exit is required; for a house oath, family and goods must also leave.

When a person swears not to dwell in a specific house, the oath is only fulfilled by physically leaving the house along with one's family and movable property. But if the oath concerns an entire town or village, the Hanafi school takes a more lenient view: it is sufficient to exit with one's body alone, provided one intends not to return. This distinction rests on customary understanding of what 'dwelling' entails in each context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath type
Determine whether the oath prohibits dwelling in a specific house or in a broader geographic area like a town or village.
The ruling differs based on this distinction.
2
For a house: list fulfillment conditions
The oath is fulfilled only when the person exits the house along with their family members and all movable belongings.
This is from the house ruling mentioned in the text.
3
For a town/village: list fulfillment conditions
It is sufficient for the person to exit the town/village with his body alone. He need not move his family or belongings.
The key condition is personal physical departure.
4
Add intention condition
The person must have the intention not to return to that town/village. If he intends to return, the oath is not fulfilled.
This is an additional requirement mentioned in the text.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given an actual oath, check the geographic scope and then apply the appropriate conditions to determine if the oath is fulfilled.
Practice with examples to internalize the rule.

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah states a clear ruling in Hanafi fiqh concerning oaths about dwelling. The author distinguishes between an oath not to dwell in a specific house and an oath not to dwell in a entire town or village. For a house, the oath is fulfilled only by physically leaving the house along with one's family and movable possessions. For a town or village, it is sufficient to exit with one's body alone; one need not relocate one's family or belongings. Additionally, the person must intend not to return. This ruling is based on the customary understanding that a house is intimately connected with one's household and property, whereas a town or village is a larger geographic area. The source cited is al-Dhakhirah quoting al-Quduri, confirming this as part of the established Hanafi position.

Key Terms
البر (al-birr): Fulfillment or discharge of an oath by performing the required action.
السكنى (al-sukna): Dwelling or residing in a particular place.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Move to another town permanently.

Key Takeaway
For an oath not to dwell in a town, personal exit with the intention not to return is sufficient; family and belongings need not be moved.

Breaking an Oath Not to Exit: Command vs. Consent
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath-breaking by removal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on when being removed by another breaks an oath not to exit and distinguish between command, coercion, and mere consent.

Core Idea
Attribution of action is by explicit command, not by mere willingness.

This passage addresses a scenario where a person swears not to exit a place and is physically removed by another. The key question: does willing acceptance of being carried out count as 'exiting' for the oath? The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply between commanding the removal and merely consenting to it, with practical implications for when an oath is broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the act
The oath is 'I will not exit' from a specific place. The act is being carried out by another person.
We need to determine whether this act is attributed to the swearer.
2
Distinguish the three scenarios
The text considers three cases: (1) carried out by order, (2) carried out against one's will (mukrahan), (3) carried out with consent (radiyan) but no order. Note that 'mukrahan' here means physical compulsion, not legal coercion (ikrah).
Legal coercion (ikrah) would not nullify the oath if the swearer exits himself.
3
Apply the principle of attribution
Hanafi rule: action is attributed to the one who commands it, not to the one who merely consents. If the swearer ordered the removal, he breaks the oath. If he did not order, the action is not his, regardless of consent.
The opposing view (some Hanafi scholars) says consent plus ability to prevent makes one like a commander.
4
Analyze the property law analogy
The correct view is supported by an agreed branch: if the owner orders destruction, the destroyer is not liable (action attributed to owner). If the owner is silent, the destroyer is liable. This shows that consent (silence) does not transfer action.
This analogy refutes the opposing argument.
5
Conclude the correct ruling
The text declares the correct view (al-asahh): the oath is not broken when carried out without command, even if willing. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether expiation is required.
Ibn al-Humam supports this position.

فصل اليمين في الخروج والاتيان والركوب وغير ذلك وحنث في لا يخرج لو حُمِل وأخرج بأمره، لا إن أُخْرِجَ بلا أمره مكرهاً أو راضياً، ومثله لا يدخل أقساماً وحكماً. قوله: وحنث...الخ؛ يعني لو حلف لا يخرج من هذا البيت أو هذا المسجد، فحمله إنسان وأخرجه بأمر الحالف حنث؛ لأن فعل المأمور مضاف إلى الأمر، فتحقق منه الخروج، بخلاف ما لو أخرج بلا أمره مكرهاً أو راضياً؛ لأنَّ الانتقال بالأمر لا بمجرد الرضاء. قوله: مكرهاً؛ على صيغة اسم المفعول؛ أي حال كون الحالف مكرهاً أو راضياً، قال ابن الهمام في الفتح: "المراد بالإخراج مكرهاً هاهنا أن يحمله ويخرجه كارهاً لذلك، لا الإكراه المعروف، وهو أن يتوعده حتى يفعل، فإنه إذا تواعده فخرج بنفسه حنث لما عرف أن الإكراه لا يعدم الفعل عندنا. قوله: أو راضياً؛ وهذا هو الأصح، وقيل: يحنث إذ حمله برضاه لا بأمره، لأنه لما كان يقدر على الامتناع فلم يفعل صار كالآمر. وجه الصحيح: إن انتقال الفعل بالأمر لا بمجرد الرضى، ولم يوجد الأمر ولا الفعل منه، فلا ينسب الفعل إليه، ولو قيل: إن الرضى ناقل دُفع بفرع اتفاقي، وهو ما إذا أمره أن يتلف ماله ففعل، لا يضمن المتلف لانتساب الإتلاف إلى المالك بالأمر، فلو أتلفه وهو ساكت ينظر ولم ينهه ضَمِنَ بلا تفصيل لأحد بين كونه راضياً أو لا. كذا في الفتح. قوله: أقساماً؛ من الحمل والإدخال بالأمر أو بغيره مكرهاً أو راضياً.
Chapter: Oaths regarding exiting, coming, riding, and other things. He breaks the oath in [the case of] "he does not exit" if he is carried and taken out by his order. Not if he is taken out without his order, whether coerced or willing. And similarly, "he does not enter" in terms of categories and ruling. His saying: "He breaks the oath... etc." means if he swore not to exit from this house or this mosque, and a person carried him and took him out by the order of the one swearing, he breaks the oath; because the action of the commanded one is attributed to the commander, so the exiting is realized from him. Unlike if he is taken out without his order, whether coerced or willing; because the transfer [of action] is by command, not merely by consent. His saying: "coerced" is in the form of the passive participle, meaning while the one swearing is coerced or willing. Ibn al-Humam said in al-Fath: "What is meant by being taken out coerced here is that he is carried and taken out while disliking that, not the well-known coercion which is threatening until he does it; for if he threatened him and he exited by himself, he breaks the oath as it is known that coercion does not nullify the action according to us." His saying: "or willing" — this is the correct view. It was said: he breaks the oath if he is carried with his consent but not by his order, because when he was able to refrain and did not, he became like the commander. The basis of the correct view is that the transfer of action is by command, not merely by consent; and neither command nor action occurred from him, so the action is not attributed to him. And if it is said that consent transfers, it is refuted by an agreed-upon branch: if he ordered him to destroy his property and he did, the destroyer is not liable because the destruction is attributed to the owner by command. If he destroyed it while he was silent, looking on and not forbidding him, he is liable without any distinction between him being willing or not. This is from al-Fath. His saying: "categories" refers to carrying and entering by order or otherwise, coerced or willing.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah deals with oaths concerning exiting and entering. The central principle is that an action is attributed to the person who commands it, not merely to the one who is willing. Thus, if someone swears not to exit a house or mosque, and he orders another person to carry him out, he breaks his oath because the exit is attributed to him through his command. However, if he is taken out without his command—whether he is coerced (reluctant) or willing—the action is not attributed to him, so the oath is not broken. The text clarifies that 'coerced' here means being carried out against his will, not the legal coercion (ikrah) where one is threatened to act. The author then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: some scholars argued that if he is willing, he becomes like the commander because he could have prevented it, so the oath should be broken. The correct view (al-ashah), supported by Ibn al-Humam, is that willingness alone does not transfer the action; explicit command is required. This is reinforced by an analogy from property law: if the owner orders someone to destroy his property, the destroyer is not liable, but if the owner merely remains silent, the destroyer is liable. This shows the difference between explicit command and mere consent. The lesson is important for understanding when an oath is broken based on the attribution of actions in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Key Terms
حنث (hanath): To break an oath by doing what one swore not to do or failing to do what one swore to do.
مكرهاً (mukrahan): Being taken out against one's will, physically compelled without consent.
راضياً (radiyan): Being willing or consenting to the removal without actively commanding it.
إكراه (ikrah): Legal coercion where one is threatened to act; under ikrah, actions may be excused.
آمر (aamir): One who gives a command; the person whose order causes the action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view, if someone swears not to exit a house and is carried out by another person with his consent but without his explicit order, does he break the oath?
A) Yes, because he was willing and could have prevented it.
B) Yes, because the action of being carried out is attributed to him.
C) No, because the action is not attributed to him without his command.
D) No, because he was coerced.

Key Takeaway
An oath not to exit is only broken if the person commands the removal, not if he merely consents or is physically forced.

Oaths of Exiting and Entering: Command vs. Satisfaction in Attribution
Ruling on oaths of exiting/entering when physically carried.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on incurring an oath when carried out/entered, distinguish between command and satisfaction, and explain the principle of transferring action by command.

Core Idea
The act is attributed by explicit command, not mere satisfaction – a key Hanafi principle in oath rulings.

In the chapter of oaths, a common issue arises: if someone swears not to exit a place and is physically carried out by another, does he break his oath? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on whether the swearer explicitly commanded the action or merely allowed it. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the legal principle behind it, and an internal Hanafi difference of opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Swearing 'I will not exit' and being carried out by command leads to hanath (incurrence). Without command, no hanath.
The ruling is based on whether the act is attributed to the swearer.
2
Understand the legal principle
The principle is: 'انتقال الفعل بالأمر لا بمجرد الرضى' – transfer of the act is by command, not by mere satisfaction.
This is a key usul principle applied in oaths.
3
Examine the internal Hanafi dispute
Some scholars argued that satisfaction with ability to resist makes one like a commander; the relied-upon view rejects this.
The dispute is about whether satisfaction alone can attribute the act.
4
Analyze the supporting subsidiary example
The property destruction case: command transfers liability; silent watching does not. This supports the principle that command is necessary.
This example is agreed upon (ittiqaqi).
5
Conclude the operative ruling
The mufta bihi is that without a verbal command, the oath is not incurred, even if the swearer is satisfied.
This is the relied-upon position in Fath al-Qadir.
