Chapter: Oaths regarding exiting, coming, riding, and other matters. He incurs [the oath] in [the case of] 'I will not exit' if he is carried and taken out by his command; not if he is taken out without his command, coerced or satisfied. Similarly, 'I will not enter' in terms of categories and rulings. Chapter: Oaths regarding exiting, coming, riding, and other matters. [1] 'He incurs...' etc.: meaning if he swore 'I will not exit from this house or this mosque,' then a person carried him and took him out by the command of the one who swore, he incurs the oath because the act of the commanded is attributed to the command, so the exit is realized from him. Contrary to if he was taken out without his command, coerced or satisfied, because the transfer [of action] is by command, not by mere satisfaction. (2) His statement: 'coerced': in the form of the passive participle, i.e., the one swearing being coerced or satisfied. Ibn al-Humam said in al-Fath: 'What is meant by being taken out coerced here is that he carries him and takes him out while he dislikes that, not the well-known coercion, which is that he threatens him until he does it. For if he threatens him and he goes out himself, he incurs the oath because coercion does not negate the act in our view.' [3] His statement: 'or satisfied': this is the most correct. It was said: he incurs [the oath] if he carries him with his satisfaction but not with his command, because when he was able to refrain and did not do so, he becomes like the commander. The basis of the correct view: the transfer of the act is by command, not by mere satisfaction; command was not present nor was the act from him, so the act is not attributed to him. If it were said that satisfaction transfers [attribution], it is refuted by an agreed-upon subsidiary: if he ordered him to destroy his property and he did so, the destroyer is not liable because the destruction is attributed to the owner by command. If he destroyed it while he was silent looking and did not stop him, he is liable without differentiation between whether he was satisfied or not. Thus in al-Fath. (r) [4] His statement: 'categories': of carrying and entering by command or otherwise, coerced or satisfied. (1) i.e., he does not incur the oath because the act is not transferred to him due to the absence of command, even if he was satisfied with exiting because transfer is by command not by mere satisfaction. See: Durar al-Hukkam (2:47). (2) End from Fath al-Qadir (5:108). (3) Fath al-Qadir (5:109). Book of Oaths: Oaths regarding entering, residing, exiting, coming, and riding. Page 61. And not in [the case of] 'I will not exit except to a funeral': if he exits to it, then to another matter. And he incurs in [the case of] 'I will not exit to Mecca': if he exits intending it and returns. That he exits by his command. And that he exits without his command, either coerced or satisfied. The ruling: incurrence in the first, and its absence in the latter two. (And not in [the case of] 'I will not exit except to a funeral': if he exits to it, then to another matter) : for he does not incur the oath because his exit was only to the funeral. [4] (And he incurs in [the case of] 'I will not exit to Mecca': if he exits intending it and returns). [11] His statement: 'that he exits by his command...' etc.: it would be better to say 'that he enters by his command and that he enters without his command, either coerced or satisfied' because the purpose is to mention the categories of entering. [2] His statement: 'if he exits to it': i.e., intending the exit to the funeral at the moment of his departure from the door of his house, whether he walks with it or not, because the excepted matter is the exit with the intention of the funeral, and exiting is the departure from inside to outside, and it does not require arriving at it to walk with it or pray over it. (2) Hence it was said in al-Bada'i: If he said: 'If you exit except to the mosque then you are divorced,' and she exited intending the mosque, then it occurred to her [otherwise] and she went to other than the mosque, she is not divorced. It was mentioned in al-Bahr: If he was in the courtyard of his house and exited to its yard then returned, he does not incur the oath unless he exits from the door of the house, because he is not considered 'one who exits to the funeral of so-and-so' as long as he is in his house. [3] His statement: 'then to another matter': i.e., after exiting to the funeral he exited to another matter. [4] His statement: 'and he incurs': because the condition in exiting, and likewise in going and returning, is the intention at the moment of departure, not arrival. In coming, visiting, and calling, arrival at the place is required. So if he swore to return to someone or to visit him, and he came to his door but was not permitted to enter and returned, not reaching him, he does not (1) Because exiting is the departure from inside to outside, and it exists with respect to the funeral but not with respect to the other matter, for what exists in the case of 'coming' is arrival, which is not exiting, and continuity of exiting is also not exiting because it lacks [initial] extension. See: Fath Bab al-'Inaya (2:265). (2) Bada'i' al-Sana'i' (3:42). (3) al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (4:337).
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of rulings in the chapter of oaths (kitab al-ayman) from the Hanafi madhhab. The base text (matn) likely from al-Wiqayah presents the core rules, and the commentary (sharh) explains them with legal reasoning. The opening section (فصل) outlines oaths regarding exiting, coming, riding, and similar actions. The first ruling: If someone swears 'I will not exit' from a specific place, then a person carries him and takes him out by his command, he incurs the oath (حنث) because the act of the commanded person is attributed to the commander. The proof given is that the action of the commanded is attributed to the command (فعل المأمور مضاف إلى الأمر). Conversely, if he is taken out without his command—whether he is coerced (مكرهاً) or satisfied (راضياً)—he does not incur the oath because the transfer of the action is by command alone, not mere satisfaction. The commentary clarifies that 'coerced' here means being carried out unwillingly, not the usual coercion where one is threatened to the point of acting. The same ruling applies to oaths of not entering. Internal Hanafi difference: Some said that if he is carried out with his satisfaction (without verbal command), he incurs the oath because he could have resisted. However, the relied-upon position (الأصح) is that satisfaction alone does not attribute the action to him; explicit command is required. A supporting example: If A orders B to destroy A's property, B is not liable; but if A merely watches silently while B destroys it, B is liable. Thus, satisfaction without command does not transfer attribution. The passage then moves to two specific exceptions: First, if someone swears 'I will not exit except to a funeral' and he exits intending the funeral but then goes to another matter, he does not incur the oath because the initial exit was for the funeral. The commentary explains that exiting is defined as the physical departure from inside to outside; the intention at the moment of departure matters, not the destination. Second, if someone swears 'I will not exit to Mecca' and he exits intending Mecca but returns without reaching it, he does incur the oath. The reason is that for oaths of exiting, going, and returning, the condition is the intention at the moment of departure (النية عند الانفصال), not actual arrival. However, for oaths of coming (إتيان), visiting (عيادة), and calling (زيارة), actual arrival at the place is required. The commentary emphasizes the distinction between exiting (خروج) and coming (إتيان).

Key Terms
حنث (hanath): Incurrence of the legal consequences (usually expiation) for violating an oath.
مكرهاً (mukrahan): Being physically carried out against one's will, not the usual coercion of threats.
أمره (amrihi): Explicit verbal command from the swearer to the person carrying him.
رضى (rida): Mental satisfaction or willingness without verbal instruction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not exit this house' and a person carries him out with his explicit verbal command, does he incur the oath (حنث) according to the Hanafi madhhab?
A) Yes, because the act of the commanded is attributed to the commander.
B) No, because coercion nullifies the attribution.
C) Yes, if he was satisfied, but not if coerced.
D) No, because he did not walk out himself.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, attribution of an action requires explicit command; satisfaction alone does not transfer legal responsibility.

Exceptions in Oaths of Exiting: Funerals, Mecca, and the Distinction Between Exiting and Coming
Exception for funeral and specific destination in oaths of exiting; distinction between khuruj and ityat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on oaths of exiting with exceptions (funeral, specific destination) and distinguish between exiting (khuruj) and coming (ityan) in terms of when intention vs arrival matters.

Core Idea
In oaths of exiting, intention at departure suffices; in oaths of coming, actual arrival is required.

This lesson covers two specific exceptions in oaths of exiting: (1) an oath to exit only for a funeral, and (2) an oath to exit to a specific place (like Mecca). It also clarifies the conceptual difference between 'exiting' (khuruj) and 'coming' (ityan) – a distinction that determines whether the oath is incurred based on intention at departure or actual arrival.

Guided Path
1
Understand the exception for funerals
If someone swears 'I will not exit except to a funeral' and exits intending the funeral, he does not incur the oath even if he subsequently goes to another matter. The exit is attributed to the funeral because the intention at departure was for it.
Exiting is defined as crossing the threshold; the intention at that moment determines the purpose.
2
Analyze the Mecca ruling
If someone swears 'I will not exit to Mecca' and exits intending Mecca but returns before arriving, he incurs the oath. The reason is that for exiting, the condition is the intention at departure, not arrival.
This contrasts with oaths of coming, where arrival is necessary.
3
Distinguish khuruj from ityat
Khuruj (exiting) is fulfilled by the initial act of departure with intention. Ityan (coming) requires actual reaching of the destination. This difference affects when hanath occurs.
The commentary mentions that for oaths of returning (perhaps to a person), arrival is required.
4
Apply the principle to the funeral case
The funeral exception is valid because the exit was solely for the funeral; the subsequent action is a separate act. The exit itself is not a continuous act, so moving to another matter does not nullify the exception.
Once the exit is completed (crossing the threshold), the oath condition is satisfied.

Source Text

ومثله لا يدخل أقساماً وحكماً

Chapter: Oaths regarding exiting, coming, riding, and other matters. He incurs [the oath] in [the case of] 'I will not exit' if he is carried and taken out by his command; not if he is taken out without his command, coerced or satisfied. Similarly, 'I will not enter' in terms of categories and rulings. Chapter: Oaths regarding exiting, coming, riding, and other matters. [1] 'He incurs...' etc.: meaning if he swore 'I will not exit from this house or this mosque,' then a person carried him and took him out by the command of the one who swore, he incurs the oath because the act of the commanded is attributed to the command, so the exit is realized from him. Contrary to if he was taken out without his command, coerced or satisfied, because the transfer [of action] is by command, not by mere satisfaction. (2) His statement: 'coerced': in the form of the passive participle, i.e., the one swearing being coerced or satisfied. Ibn al-Humam said in al-Fath: 'What is meant by being taken out coerced here is that he carries him and takes him out while he dislikes that, not the well-known coercion, which is that he threatens him until he does it. For if he threatens him and he goes out himself, he incurs the oath because coercion does not negate the act in our view.' [3] His statement: 'or satisfied': this is the most correct. It was said: he incurs [the oath] if he carries him with his satisfaction but not with his command, because when he was able to refrain and did not do so, he becomes like the commander. The basis of the correct view: the transfer of the act is by command, not by mere satisfaction; command was not present nor was the act from him, so the act is not attributed to him. If it were said that satisfaction transfers [attribution], it is refuted by an agreed-upon subsidiary: if he ordered him to destroy his property and he did so, the destroyer is not liable because the destruction is attributed to the owner by command. If he destroyed it while he was silent looking and did not stop him, he is liable without differentiation between whether he was satisfied or not. Thus in al-Fath. (r) [4] His statement: 'categories': of carrying and entering by command or otherwise, coerced or satisfied. (1) i.e., he does not incur the oath because the act is not transferred to him due to the absence of command, even if he was satisfied with exiting because transfer is by command not by mere satisfaction. See: Durar al-Hukkam (2:47). (2) End from Fath al-Qadir (5:108). (3) Fath al-Qadir (5:109). Book of Oaths: Oaths regarding entering, residing, exiting, coming, and riding. Page 61. And not in [the case of] 'I will not exit except to a funeral': if he exits to it, then to another matter. And he incurs in [the case of] 'I will not exit to Mecca': if he exits intending it and returns. That he exits by his command. And that he exits without his command, either coerced or satisfied. The ruling: incurrence in the first, and its absence in the latter two. (And not in [the case of] 'I will not exit except to a funeral': if he exits to it, then to another matter) : for he does not incur the oath because his exit was only to the funeral. [4] (And he incurs in [the case of] 'I will not exit to Mecca': if he exits intending it and returns). [11] His statement: 'that he exits by his command...' etc.: it would be better to say 'that he enters by his command and that he enters without his command, either coerced or satisfied' because the purpose is to mention the categories of entering. [2] His statement: 'if he exits to it': i.e., intending the exit to the funeral at the moment of his departure from the door of his house, whether he walks with it or not, because the excepted matter is the exit with the intention of the funeral, and exiting is the departure from inside to outside, and it does not require arriving at it to walk with it or pray over it. (2) Hence it was said in al-Bada'i: If he said: 'If you exit except to the mosque then you are divorced,' and she exited intending the mosque, then it occurred to her [otherwise] and she went to other than the mosque, she is not divorced. It was mentioned in al-Bahr: If he was in the courtyard of his house and exited to its yard then returned, he does not incur the oath unless he exits from the door of the house, because he is not considered 'one who exits to the funeral of so-and-so' as long as he is in his house. [3] His statement: 'then to another matter': i.e., after exiting to the funeral he exited to another matter. [4] His statement: 'and he incurs': because the condition in exiting, and likewise in going and returning, is the intention at the moment of departure, not arrival. In coming, visiting, and calling, arrival at the place is required. So if he swore to return to someone or to visit him, and he came to his door but was not permitted to enter and returned, not reaching him, he does not (1) Because exiting is the departure from inside to outside, and it exists with respect to the funeral but not with respect to the other matter, for what exists in the case of 'coming' is arrival, which is not exiting, and continuity of exiting is also not exiting because it lacks [initial] extension. See: Fath Bab al-'Inaya (2:265). (2) Bada'i' al-Sana'i' (3:42). (3) al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (4:337).
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of rulings in the chapter of oaths (kitab al-ayman) from the Hanafi madhhab. The base text (matn) likely from al-Wiqayah presents the core rules, and the commentary (sharh) explains them with legal reasoning. The opening section (فصل) outlines oaths regarding exiting, coming, riding, and similar actions. The first ruling: If someone swears 'I will not exit' from a specific place, then a person carries him and takes him out by his command, he incurs the oath (حنث) because the act of the commanded person is attributed to the commander. The proof given is that the action of the commanded is attributed to the command (فعل المأمور مضاف إلى الأمر). Conversely, if he is taken out without his command—whether he is coerced (مكرهاً) or satisfied (راضياً)—he does not incur the oath because the transfer of the action is by command alone, not mere satisfaction. The commentary clarifies that 'coerced' here means being carried out unwillingly, not the usual coercion where one is threatened to the point of acting. The same ruling applies to oaths of not entering. Internal Hanafi difference: Some said that if he is carried out with his satisfaction (without verbal command), he incurs the oath because he could have resisted. However, the relied-upon position (الأصح) is that satisfaction alone does not attribute the action to him; explicit command is required. A supporting example: If A orders B to destroy A's property, B is not liable; but if A merely watches silently while B destroys it, B is liable. Thus, satisfaction without command does not transfer attribution. The passage then moves to two specific exceptions: First, if someone swears 'I will not exit except to a funeral' and he exits intending the funeral but then goes to another matter, he does not incur the oath because the initial exit was for the funeral. The commentary explains that exiting is defined as the physical departure from inside to outside; the intention at the moment of departure matters, not the destination. Second, if someone swears 'I will not exit to Mecca' and he exits intending Mecca but returns without reaching it, he does incur the oath. The reason is that for oaths of exiting, going, and returning, the condition is the intention at the moment of departure (النية عند الانفصال), not actual arrival. However, for oaths of coming (إتيان), visiting (عيادة), and calling (زيارة), actual arrival at the place is required. The commentary emphasizes the distinction between exiting (خروج) and coming (إتيان).

Key Terms
خروج (khuruj): Physical departure from inside a defined space (e.g., house, mosque) to outside.
إتيان (ityan): Coming to a place or person, requiring actual arrival.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone swears 'I will not exit except to a funeral' and exits intending the funeral but then goes to the market, does he incur the oath?
A) Yes, because he went to the market
B) No, because his intention at departure was for the funeral
C) Yes, because he did not attend the funeral
D) No, because the market is not a funeral

Key Takeaway
In oaths of exiting, intention at departure determines the ruling; in oaths of coming, actual arrival is required.

When Is an Oath Broken? Absolute vs Condition-Bound Oaths
Ruling on absolute vs condition-bound oaths with proof from Quran and Hanafi preference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which an absolute oath is broken according to the Hanafi school and apply the condition of ability (istita'ah) to determine whether an oath is broken.

Core Idea
An oath without a time limit is broken only when hope ends; an oath conditioned on ability stands if obstacles prevent the act.

Oaths (al-ayman) are serious legal commitments. The Hanafi school carefully examines the wording and conditions of each oath to determine when breaking (hanth) occurs. In this passage, we learn two key rulings: one for absolute oaths without a time limit, and another for oaths tied to a condition of ability. The distinction hinges on whether the act remains possible or has become impossible within the oath-taker's capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oath types
Read the passage and pick out the two categories: (1) absolute oath without time limit, e.g., 'By Allah, he will come to Mecca'; (2) time-bound or condition-bound oath, e.g., 'By Allah, he will come tomorrow if able.'
2
Determine the ruling for each type
For absolute oaths: the oath is not broken until the end of life or death of the relevant person. For condition-bound oaths: the oath is broken if the act is not done despite the absence of a valid obstacle (illness, imprisonment, etc.).
Fulfillment is hoped for until death.
3
Locate the Quranic proof
Find the verse cited: 'Indeed I am going to my Lord' (37:99). Understand the wajh al-dalalah: the word 'going' indicates intention, not necessity of arrival. Thus, an oath to 'come' is not broken by failure to reach, as reaching is not fully within one's control.
The proof supports the absolute oath ruling.
4
Analyse the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note that some scholars (البعض) equate an absolute oath with a negative oath (immediate breaking). The text prefers the first view ('الأول أصح') because it aligns with the Quranic evidence and the realistic possibility of fulfillment.
The preferred view is the majority Hanafi position.
5
Apply the condition of ability
Understand that الاستطاعة means soundness of means and removal of obstacles. Obstacles can be physical (illness, insanity) or moral (fear of authority). If an obstacle exists, the condition is not met and the oath is not broken.
Forgetfulness also prevents breaking.

Source Text

كتاب الأيمان اليمين في الدخول والسكنى والخروج والإتيان والركوب
وفي : ليأتين مكة ولم يأتِها لا يحنث إلا في آخر حياته، وحنت في ليأتينه غداً إن استطاع إن لم يأتِه بلا مانع كمرض أو سلطان
وعند البعض : هو مثلُ لا يأتي ، والأول أصح ؛ لقوله تعالى : إِنِّي ذَاهِب إِلَى رَبِّ ، أي متوجه إليه ، وأما الوصول فليس في وسعه.
[1] قوله : إلا في آخر حياته ؛ أي الجزء المتصل بموته ؛ لأنَّ البر قبل ذلك مرجو، وكذا لو حلف ليأتين فلاناً لا يحنث إلا بموت أحدهما ، وهكذا في كل يمين مطلقة، فكلُّ فعل حلف أن يفعله في المستقبل، ولم يقيده بوقت لم يحنث فيه، حتى يقع اليأس على البر، مثل: ليضربن زيداً أو ليطلقن زوجته كذا في «البحر»).
[2] قوله : إن لم يأته بلا مانع ؛ فإنّ الاستطاعة محمولة على سلامة الآلات ورفع الموانع فإن وجد مانع معنوي أو حسي كالمرض والجنون وخوف سلطان ونحو ذلك، فلم يأته لم يحنث ؛ لأنه قيد الإتيان بالاستطاعة ، ولم توجد عند وجود المانع، وكذا لا يحنث لو نسي اليمين. كذا في «البحر»).
Book of Oaths: Oath regarding entering, dwelling, going out, coming, and riding. And in [the oath] 'By Allah, he will come to Mecca' and he did not come, he does not break the oath except at the end of his life. And he breaks the oath in 'By Allah, he will come tomorrow' if he is able if he does not come without an obstacle like illness or a sultan. And according to some, it is like 'he will not come', and the first is more correct due to the saying of the Most High: 'Indeed I am going to my Lord', i.e., heading towards Him, and reaching is not within his capacity. [1] His saying 'except at the end of his life' means the part connected to his death because fulfillment before that is hoped for. Similarly, if he swore 'By Allah, he will come to so-and-so', he does not break the oath except with the death of one of them. And thus in every absolute oath: every action he swore to do in the future and did not restrict by time, he does not break the oath in it until hope of fulfillment is lost, such as 'By Allah, he will strike Zayd' or 'By Allah, he will divorce his wife'—thus in al-Bahr. [2] His saying 'if he does not come without an obstacle' means that ability is based on soundness of means and removal of obstacles. If there is a moral or physical obstacle like illness, insanity, fear of a sultan, and the like, and he does not come, he does not break the oath because he conditioned coming on ability, and it was not present with the obstacle. Similarly, he does not break the oath if he forgot the oath—thus in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Oaths clarifies two rulings. First, an oath to perform a future act without any time specification (like 'he will come to Mecca') is not broken unless hope of fulfillment is lost, which occurs at the end of life. The Hanafi proof is the Qur'anic verse where Ibrahim says 'I am going to my Lord,' meaning heading towards, not necessarily arriving. Second, an oath tied to a condition of ability (like 'he will come tomorrow if able') is examined: if an obstacle prevents the act, the oath is not broken because the condition of ability was not met. The commentator emphasizes that obstacles include both physical and moral hindrances. The preferred view overrides those who say such an oath is like a negative oath. This shows the Hanafi method of interpreting oaths according to apparent meaning and realistic capacity.

Key Terms
ليأتين (laya'tinna): A third-person oath formulation meaning 'by Allah, he will come'.
استطاعة (istita'ah): Ability conditioned for an action, interpreted as soundness of means and removal of obstacles.
حنث (hanath): Breaking an oath, incurring sin and requiring expiation (kaffarah).
بر (birr): Fulfillment of the oath, which is hoped for until death.
يمين مطلقة (yameen mutlaqah): An absolute oath without a time limit specifying when the action must occur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'By Allah, my son will go to Mecca' but his son never goes. When does he break his oath according to the Hanafi school?
A) Immediately when the son fails to go
B) Only when the son dies without having gone
C) Only when the man himself dies
D) After one year has passed

Key Takeaway
An absolute oath is not broken until the end of life or death of the relevant party; a condition of ability prevents breaking if a valid obstacle exists.

Diyana vs Qada: Intent of True Ability in Oaths
Defining real vs feasible ability and acceptance in diyana vs qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real ability (istita'ah haqiqiyyah) and feasible ability (istita'ah mumkinah), and explain why a court accepts only the customary meaning in oath cases.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, custom ('urf) determines the legal meaning of ambiguous terms in court, while Allah accepts the true intention.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah addresses a subtle but important point in oath jurisprudence: what happens when someone swears by a condition of ability, then fails to act, and claims he meant a very specific philosophical kind of ability? The Hanafi approach draws a clear line between what is accepted before Allah (diyana) and what is accepted in court (qada). Understanding this distinction requires mastering the two types of ability defined in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of ability
Read the passage and note the two definitions: real ability (complete power that necessitates the action and coexists with it) and feasible ability (soundness of means and removal of obstacles, which precedes the action). Understand that only the second type is used for religious obligations (taklif).
Real ability is like the cause that always produces the effect; feasible ability is the availability of means and absence of barriers.
2
Understand the ruling on the oath-taker's claim
If a man swears 'I will come if I am able' and then does not come, claiming he intended real ability, he is believed diyanatan (between him and Allah) but not qada'an (in court). The oath is considered not broken in religious terms, but the court presumes he meant feasible ability.
The court follows the apparent customary meaning, not the hidden intention.
3
Analyze the Quranic evidence for customary meaning
The passage cites Quran 3:97 regarding Hajj: 'whoever has the ability to find a way to it.' This verse uses 'ability' in the sense of feasible ability (soundness of means), showing that the customary meaning in Islamic law is feasible ability, not real ability.
This proof establishes that 'urf (custom) has given 'ability' a specific legal meaning.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on homonymy vs. custom
Some scholars (قيل) argued that because 'ability' is homonymous (shared between two meanings), the oath-taker should be believed in court if he intended the other meaning. But the preferred view (الأول أوجه) is that custom has given the word a dominant meaning, so the court does not accept the rare meaning.
This dispute shows how Hanafis prioritize 'urf over linguistic homonymy in judicial matters.
5
Apply the principle to a new example
Consider a similar ambiguous term in a contract or oath: e.g., a man swears 'I will not eat meat' but intends only red meat. The court will interpret 'meat' according to custom (including chicken and fish) unless evidence shows a special customary usage. His private intention is between him and Allah.
The same diyana/qada distinction applies to all ambiguous terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودين بنية الحقيقية : أي إن قال : عنيت الاستطاعة الحقيقيَّة وهي القدرة التامة التي يجب عندها صدور الفعل، فهي لا تكون إلا مقارنة للفعل يصدق ديانة لا قضاء ؛ لأنها تطلقُ في العرفِ على سلامة الأسباب والآلات، فالمعنى الآخر خلافُ الظَّاهر، فلا يصدقُ قضاء
[1] قوله : دين؛ - بصيغة المجهول - : أي صدق ديانة – أي فيما بينه وبين الله - إن قال : أردت بالاستطاعة الاستطاعة الحقيقيّة، فإنّ الاستطاعة والقدر تطلق على معنيين على ما فصل في كتب الأصول :
أحدهما: القدرة الحقيقية التي يكون الفعل معها معية زمانية، وتتقدم عليها بالذات فقط لا بالزمان، فإنّها علّة تامة للفعل، فلا يتخلّف الفعلُ عنها، وهذه القدرة لیست مدار تكليف العباد بالأحكام ؛ لأنها لا تكون سابقة على الفعل حتى يكلف بها. وثانيهما: القدرة الممكنة، وتُفَسِّرُ بصحة الآلات وسلامة الأسباب مع رفع الموانع، وهذه هي التي كلّفَ العبادُ بها لكونها متقدّمة على الفعل، وهي المقصودة من قوله : وَلِلَّهِ عَلَى النَّاسِ حِجُّ الْبَيْتِ مَنِ اسْتَطَاعَ إِلَيْهِ سَبِيلًا
[2] قوله : يصدق ؛ قال في «الفتح» : فإذا لم يأته لعذر أو لغيره لا يحنث، كأنه قال : لآتينك إن خلق الله إتياني، وهو إذا لم يأتِ لم يخلق إتيانه، ولا استطاعته المقارنة وإلا لأتي.
[3] قوله : لا قضاء ؛ قال في «الفتح» : وقيل : يصدق قضاء ؛ لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه ؛ لأنَّ اسم الاستطاعة يطلقُ بالاشتراكِ على كلّ من المعنيين، والأول أوجه ؛ لأنه وإن كان مشتركاً بينهما ، لكن تعورف استعماله عند الإطلاق عن القرينة لأحد المعنيين بخصوصه، فصار ظاهراً فيه بخصوصه، فلا يصدقه القاضي بخلاف الظاهر.
And he is believed regarding the intention of the true [ability]: i.e., if he said, 'I meant the real ability' which is the complete power that necessitates the occurrence of the action; it only exists concurrent with the action. He is believed as a matter of diyana (between him and Allah) but not in court (qada) because it is customarily used for soundness of means and instruments, so the other meaning contradicts the apparent meaning, so he is not believed in court.
[1] His saying 'diyana' (passive form) means: he is believed diyanatan – i.e., between him and Allah – if he said, 'I intended by ability the real ability'. For ability and power are used for two meanings as detailed in the books of usul:
One: real ability, with which the action coexists in time, and it precedes [the action] in essence only, not in time; it is the complete cause of the action, so the action does not lag behind it. This ability is not the basis of obligation of rulings upon the servants because it does not precede the action so that obligation could be attached to it.
The second: feasible ability, interpreted as soundness of instruments and means with removal of obstacles. This is what the servants are obligated by, because it precedes the action, and it is what is meant by His statement: 'And for Allah is the pilgrimage to the House for whoever has the ability to find a way to it.'
[2] His saying 'he is believed'; he said in al-Fath: So if he did not come due to an excuse or otherwise, he does not break the oath – as if he said, 'I will come to you if Allah creates my coming.' And if he does not come, Allah did not create his coming, nor his concomitant ability; otherwise he would have come.
[3] His saying 'not in court'; he said in al-Fath: And it is said: he is believed in court because he intended the reality of his speech, since the word 'ability' is used homonymously for both meanings. But the first is more appropriate because although it is homonymous, its usage without a qualifier has become conventional for one particular meaning, so it has become apparent in that meaning specifically. Hence the judge does not accept [the other meaning], unlike the apparent meaning.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage discusses the interpretation of 'ability' (استطاعة) in oaths. It distinguishes two types: real ability (قدرة حقيقية) which is inseparable from the action and thus cannot be a basis for obligation, and feasible ability (قدرة ممكنة) which precedes the action and is used in religious rulings like Hajj. When a person swears with a condition of ability and later fails, if he claims he intended the real ability, he is believed before Allah (diyana) but not in court (qada). The reason is that the customary use of 'ability' in oaths refers to feasible ability, not the real one. The commentator from al-Fath explains that if he intended the real ability, his oath is like saying 'if Allah creates my coming' – and since he didn't come, Allah didn't create it, so no breaking occurs. But in court, the judge follows the apparent meaning, which is feasible ability, so the oath is considered broken unless a clear obstacle exists. This shows the Hanafi principle of prioritizing 'urf (custom) in interpreting ambiguous terms.

Key Terms
ديانة (diyanatan): Belief or acceptance in matters between the servant and Allah, not enforceable in court.
قضاء (qada): Judicial decision based on apparent evidence; court ruling.
استطاعة حقيقية (istita'ah haqiqiyyah): Real ability that coexists with the action and cannot be separated; not a basis for obligation.
استطاعة ممكنة (istita'ah mumkinah): Feasible ability consisting of sound means and removal of obstacles; basis for religious obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'By Allah, I will come to you if I am able.' He does not come and claims he intended 'real ability' (which only exists concurrently with the action). How does the court rule?
A) The court accepts his claim and declares he did not break the oath
B) The court rejects his claim because customary usage refers to feasible ability
C) The court accepts if he takes an oath
D) The court declares the oath invalid from the start

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between diyana (Allah knows the heart) and qada (court follows custom); in oaths, 'ability' customarily means feasible ability, so a contrary private intention does not change the judicial ruling.

Two Oath Formulas: 'Except With His Permission' vs 'Except If He Permits'
Explaining two oath formulas and their legal effects via linguistic analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two oath formulations 'لا تخرج إلا بإذنه' and 'لا تخرج إلا أن أذن' and state the legal consequence for each regarding repeated exits.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'except with his permission' requires separate permission for each act; 'except if he permits' lifts the prohibition after one permission.

Oaths often involve conditional phrases that determine whether breaking occurs. A common example is a husband prohibiting his wife from leaving the house. The precise wording of the exception—whether it uses 'بإذنه' (with his permission) or 'أن أذن' (that he permits)—changes the obligation. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi linguistic analysis behind these two formulations and their legal effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oath formulations
The passage contrasts two versions of an oath: 'لا تخرج إلا بإذنه' (Do not go out except with his permission) and 'لا تخرج إلا أن أذن' (Do not go out except if he permits). Note the key difference: 'بإذنه' uses the preposition 'ba', while 'أن أذن' uses the conjunction 'أن' with a verb.
These are standard examples in the Hanafi chapter on oaths.
2
Analyze the function of 'ba' in 'بإذنه'
The letter 'ba' here indicates attachment (الإلصاق), meaning the permission must be attached to each specific act of exiting. Therefore, the exception is an exception of attachment: she may only go out with permission specifically tied to that exit. Consequently, each exit requires a fresh permission.
This is a linguistic argument (talil) based on the literal meaning of 'ba'.
3
Analyze the function of 'إلا أن' in 'إلا أن أذن'
The phrase 'إلا أن' functions as a limit or endpoint (الغاية), similar to 'إلى أن' (until). It indicates that the prohibition lasts only until permission is given. Once permission occurs, the prohibition is lifted entirely, and subsequent exits do not require new permission.
The commentators argue that 'أن' with a verb is like a verbal noun, and permission is not of the same genus as exiting, so a true exception is impossible; it is used metaphorically for the limit.
4
Apply the distinction to a practical scenario
If a husband says 'لا تخرج إلا بإذني', each time she wants to go out she needs a new permission; if she goes out without it, she breaks the oath. If he says 'لا تخرج إلا أن آذن', one permission suffices for all future exits. The passage also discusses a legal trick (حيلة): if the husband says 'كلما أردت الخروج فقد أذنت لك' (whenever you want to go out, I have permitted you), subsequent prohibitions are ineffective according to Abu Yusuf.
This trick avoids repeated need for permission under the first formulation.

وفي «التلويح»: «لقائل أن يقول : هناك وجة ثالث يقتضي وجوب الإذن لكلّ خروج، وهو أن يكون على حذف الباء، فيصير بمنزلة : إلا بإذني، وحذف حرف الجر مع أن وإن شائع كثير، وعند تعارض الوجهين الأولين يبقـى هـذا الـوجه سالماً عـن المعارض، وأشار في «المبسوط» إلى الجواب بأنّ قولنا : إلا خروجاً بإذني كلام مستقيم،

The Book of Oaths: The oath concerning entering, dwelling, leaving, coming, and riding. The condition for not breaking the oath in "Do not go out except with his permission" is that every exit requires permission. Not in "except if he permits" meaning: if he says: do not go out unless I permit, permission is not required for every exit, but if he permits once and she goes out, then goes out again without his permission, she does not break the oath. They said: because 'in' with a verb is like a verbal noun, and permission is not of the same genus as exiting, so the literal meaning of exception cannot be intended, and it is a metaphor for the limit. The connection between exception and limit is clear, so the meaning is 'until I permit', so exiting is forbidden until the time of permission, and it has occurred once, so the prohibition is lifted. I say: it can be interpreted on another basis, namely that 'an' with the present tense verb is like a verbal noun, and the verbal noun sometimes indicates time due to the capaciousness of language, so its meaning would be 'do not go out at a time except at the time of my permission', so each exit would require permission. On this interpretation it can be answered that she breaks the oath if she goes out again without permission, but on the first interpretation she does not, and in doubt she does not. End of his words. And in al-Talwih: one may say there is a third interpretation that necessitates permission for each exit, that it is with the omission of the preposition, so it becomes like 'except with my permission', and the omission of the preposition with 'an' and 'in' is widespread. When the first two interpretations conflict, this interpretation remains free of opposition. And al-Mabsut indicates the answer that our saying 'except an exit with my permission' is a sound sentence, unlike our saying 'except an exit that I permit you', for that is defective and its usage is unknown. (1) His saying: 'so if he permits...' etc.; an objection was raised against it by quoting the verse: 'O you who have believed, do not enter the houses of the Prophet except when you are permitted for a meal, without waiting for its completion. But if you are invited, enter, and when you have eaten, disperse, without seeking to remain for conversation. Indeed, that was troubling the Prophet, and he is shy of you. But Allah is not shy of the truth.' (Surah al-Ahzab, verse 53) For the aforementioned reasoning would imply that permission is not required for each entry into the Prophet's houses, but a single permission would suffice, which is contrary to consensus.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of two common formulations of oaths concerning a wife's movement. The first formulation is 'لا تخرج إلا بإذنه' (Do not go out except with his permission). The letter 'ba' in Arabic indicates attachment (الإلصاق), meaning the permission must be attached to each specific act of exiting. Therefore, the exception here is an 'exception of attachment' — she may only go out with permission specifically tied to that exit. Consequently, each exit requires a fresh permission. The second formulation is 'لا تخرج إلا أن أذن' (Do not go out until I permit). The phrase 'إلا أن' functions as a limit or endpoint (الغاية), similar to 'إلى أن' (until). Thus, once permission is given, the prohibition is lifted entirely, and subsequent exits do not require new permission. The commentator points out that this is because 'أن' plus a verb is like a verbal noun, and permission is not of the same genus as exiting, so it cannot be a true exception; it is used metaphorically to indicate the end of the prohibition. Some scholars suggested a third interpretation: that the 'ba' has been omitted, so the meaning is 'except with my permission', which would require permission for each exit. However, the author of al-Mabsut rejects this because the phrase 'except an exit that I permit you' is grammatically irregular. The passage also addresses an objection based on the Quranic verse about entering the Prophet's houses only when permitted. The objection claimed that if 'إلا أن' indicated a limit, then a single permission would suffice for all future entries, which contradicts the fact that each time one wants to enter, a new permission is required. The response, implied but not fully expanded here, is that the verse is about entering for a meal under specific conditions, not a general rule, and the custom of the Prophet (peace be upon him) required repeated permissions.

Key Terms
الإلصاق (al-ilṣāq): Attachment — the linguistic meaning of the letter 'ba' when used to indicate that one thing is attached or connected to another. Here, it means permission must be attached to each specific exit.
الغاية (al-ghāyah): Limit or endpoint — used here to explain that 'إلا أن' indicates the end of the prohibition, similar to 'until'.
الحيلة (al-ḥīlah): Legal stratagem or trick — a permissible way to avoid breaking the oath while still achieving a desired outcome. Here, the trick is to grant blanket permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the oath 'لا تخرج إلا بإذنه', how many permissions are required for the wife to go out without breaking the oath?
A) One permission suffices for all exits.
B) Permission is required for each exit.
C) No permission is ever required.
D) Permission is needed only for the first exit.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of the particle in the exception determines whether each act requires separate permission or a single permission suffices.

Immediate Action in Conditional Oaths (Yamin al-Fawr)
Ruling on conditional oaths requiring immediate action based on context

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on yamin al-fawr and apply the concept of qarina haliyya to determine when an oath's condition triggers immediate action.

Core Idea
Context restricts conditional oaths to immediate action; delay defeats the condition.

Conditional oaths often carry an implied urgency. In this lesson, we explore when a conditional oath such as 'If you go out, you are divorced' is understood to apply only if the action occurs immediately. The Hanafi school uniquely recognizes that circumstantial evidence (qarina haliyya) diverts the oath from its absolute literal meaning to an immediate context. We will see how custom (urf) serves as the proof for this restriction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional oath scenario
The passage discusses two examples: a husband saying to his wife at the door 'If you go out, you are divorced' and a master saying to his slave 'If I strike you, you are free'. Note the context: the statement is made when the action is about to happen.
The context (qarina haliyya) is crucial.
2
Understand the ruling on immediate action
The violation (divorce/freedom) only occurs if the action (going out/striking) is done immediately (fawran). If the wife delays going out, the oath is not violated.
Fawr here means without significant delay, roughly within an hour.
3
Recognize the proof: custom (urf)
The basis for restricting the oath to immediate action is customary understanding. People in such contexts intend to prevent the action at that moment, not absolutely.
The dalil is urf, which is a valid source in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Note the Hanafi uniqueness
Abu Hanifa was unique in making this evident (تفرد أبو حنيفة بإظهاره). Other imams may interpret such oaths literally without the immediate restriction.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو ضرب عبد فعلهما فوراً). (1): أي شُرِطَ للحنث في إن خرجت، وإن ضربتِ فعلهما فورا. وفي: إن تغديت بعد أن يقال: تعال تغد معي، ذكر في كتب الأصول: إنّ القرينة في هذه اليمين للصرف عن إطلاقها، هي القرينة الحالية، فإن قصد الحالف في مثل هذا يكون المنع عن ذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت، لا المنع مطلقاً، فتتقيد اليمين به. تفرد أبو حنيفة بإظهاره. ووجهه: أن مراد المتكلم الردُّ عن تلك الضربة والخرجة عرفاً، ومبنى الأيمان عليه.
Or if he strikes a slave (the condition) must be done immediately). That is, the violation of the oath (if she goes out, or if she strikes) is conditional on doing it immediately. And in: 'If I have lunch after it is said: Come have lunch with me', it is mentioned in the books of usul that the contextual evidence in such an oath, to divert it from its absolute meaning, is the circumstantial evidence, for the intent of the one swearing in such a case is to prevent that action at that time, not to prevent it absolutely, so the oath is restricted by this. Abu Hanifa was unique in making this evident. Its reasoning: that the speaker's intention is to prevent that specific strike or departure by custom, and oaths are based on custom.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a ruling about a specific type of conditional oath (yamin al-fawr) where the condition must be fulfilled immediately. The author explains that if someone says to his wife 'If you go out, you are divorced' or to his slave 'If I strike you, you are free' at the moment she intends to go out or he intends to strike, the oath only takes effect if the action is done immediately without delay. The reason is that the context (qarina haliyya) shows the speaker's intention is to prevent the action at that very moment, not later. This ruling is uniquely attributed to Abu Hanifa and is based on the customary understanding of such statements.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath invoking divorce, manumission, or other serious consequences conditional on an action.
فور (fawr): Immediate action without delay; the condition must be fulfilled right away.
قرينة حالية (qarina haliyya): Circumstantial evidence that indicates the speaker's intent to restrict the oath to the immediate situation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife as she is about to leave the house, 'If you go out, you are divorced,' and she delays going out for a day, does the divorce occur according to Abu Hanifa?
A) Yes, because she eventually went out.
B) No, because the oath requires immediate action.
C) Yes, because the wording is absolute.
D) No, because the husband must repeat the oath.

Key Takeaway
When contextual evidence indicates urgency, a conditional oath must be fulfilled immediately for the penalty to apply.

Oath in Response to an Invitation: Contextual Interpretation
Ruling on an oath made in response to an invitation, requiring eating with the inviter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a response-based oath and an independent oath, and apply the Hanafi rule that contextual evidence (qarina) restricts the scope of an oath.

Core Idea
An oath made in response to an invitation is interpreted contextually — only eating with the inviter violates it.

Imagine you are invited to lunch and you respond with an oath: 'If I have lunch, my wife is divorced.' Do you violate the oath if you eat alone at home? The Hanafi school says no, because the context of the invitation restricts the oath to eating with the inviter. This lesson unpacks the principle of contextual interpretation (qarinat al-jawab) and how adding a temporal condition like 'today' transforms the oath into an independent statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act? Making an oath in response to an invitation. The ruling: the oath is restricted to the specific invited action.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is contextual evidence (qarina) and analogy to other cases where context determines meaning.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The response context shows the intent is to prevent that particular meal, not all meals.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools may interpret the oath literally. The Hanafi response is that qarina controls.
Practical difference: in Hanafi, responder can eat alone without violation; in literal view, any eating triggers violation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي: إن تغديت بعد أن يقال تعال تغد معي، تغديه معه، وكفى مطلق التغدي إن ضم اليوم. يعني لو قال رجل لرجل: تعال تغد معي، فقال المدعو: إن تغديت فعبدي حرّ أو فامرأتي طالق، يشترط في الحنثِ تغدّيه معه، حتى لو تغدى في بيته لا معه لم يحنث؛ لأن قرينة كونه جواباً لكلام الطالب ترجّح كون الممنوع ذلك الفعل لا مطلقه، بخلافِ ما إذا زاد معه قوله: اليوم ونحوه، فإنّه يحملُ حينئذ على أنه كلام مستقل. قال الشارح له في تنقيح الأصول: «اللفظ الذي ورد بعد سؤال أو حادثة إمّا أن لا يكون مستقلاً أو يكون، فحينئذ إما أن يخرج مخرج الجواب قطعاً، أو الظاهر أنه جواب مع احتمال الابتداء أو بالعكس. ونحو: تعال تغد معي، فيقول: إن تغديت فكذا، يحمل على الجواب، ولو قال: إن تغديت اليوم مع زيادة على قدرِ الواجب، ففي الثلاثة الأول يحمل على الجواب، وفي الرابع: يحمـل علـى الابتداء عندنا، حملاً للزيادة على الإفادة. ولو قال: عنيت الجواب صدق ديانة».
And in: 'If I have lunch after it is said: Come have lunch with me,' it is eating with him (the inviter). And absolute eating suffices if he includes 'today'. Meaning: if one man says to another: 'Come have lunch with me,' and the invitee says: 'If I have lunch, my slave is free or my wife is divorced,' the violation of the oath is conditional on eating with him, so if he eats in his own house not with him, he does not violate; because the context that it is a response to the speaker's words makes it more likely that the prohibited action is that specific act, not absolute. Contrary to if he adds 'today' or similar, then it is considered an independent statement. The commentator in Tanqih al-Usul said: 'A word that comes after a question or event is either not independent or is independent. Then either it is definitely a response, or it apparently is a response with the possibility of being an independent statement, or vice versa. Examples: 'Come have lunch with me,' and he says: 'If I have lunch, then such-and-such'—this is treated as a response. If he says: 'If I have lunch today' with an addition beyond what is obligatory, then in the first three (examples) it is treated as a response, and in the fourth (with 'today') it is treated as an independent statement according to us, because the addition is considered for benefit. And if he says: 'I meant it as a response,' he is believed as a matter of religious duty.'
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of an oath made in response to an invitation. If a person is invited to lunch and he responds with an oath like 'If I eat lunch, my slave is free,' the oath is interpreted contextually: it is conditioned on eating with the inviter, not on eating in general. Therefore, if he eats alone at home, the oath is not violated. However, if the person adds a temporal condition like 'today,' the oath becomes an independent statement and applies to any eating that day. The author references 'Tanqih al-Usul' to explain the principles: the context of being a response determines the interpretation; an addition like 'today' indicates independence. This demonstrates the Hanafi reliance on contextual evidence (qarina) in oath interpretation.

Key Terms
قرينة الجواب (qarinat al-jawab): The contextual evidence that a statement is a direct response to a previous speaker, restricting its meaning.
كلام مستقل (kalam mustaqil): An independent statement not tied to a previous utterance, interpreted literally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If A says to B, 'Come have lunch with me,' and B replies, 'If I have lunch, my wife is divorced,' and then B has lunch alone at home, does the divorce occur?
A) Yes, because he had lunch.
B) No, because the oath was conditioned on eating with A.
C) Yes, because the oath is absolute.
D) No, because B did not intend divorce.

Key Takeaway
An oath made in response to an invitation is contextually restricted to the invited action unless an independent expression is used.

Oath concerning the Mount of a Permitted Slave
Hanafi ruling on whether a master's oath about a slave's mount is binding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions under which a master breaks his oath by riding the mount of his permitted slave and explain why the ruling differs for a non-permitted slave and a mukaatab.

Core Idea
A master's oath about a mount is only broken if the mount belongs to a permitted slave with no overwhelming debt and the master intended it.

This passage from the Book of Oaths (Kitab al-Ayman) addresses a nuanced question: when a master swears about riding a specific mount, does riding his slave's mount violate the oath? The Hanafi school distinguishes between types of slaves based on their legal capacity and financial status. Understanding this ruling requires careful attention to the conditions attached to the permitted slave's property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the slave types
The text distinguishes three types: ma'dhun (permitted to trade), ghayr ma'dhun (non-permitted), and mukaatab (under contract of manumission). For each, ownership attribution differs.
This classification is unique to Hanafi fiqh and affects oath rulings.
2
Determine property attribution for each type
For a non-permitted slave, property belongs absolutely to the master. For a permitted slave, property is quasi-independent: it is attributed to the master only under specific conditions. For a mukaatab, property is fully independent and never attributed to the master.
Attribution is key for the oath: the master's oath about 'his mount' only covers what is legally his.
3
Apply the conditions for the ma'dhun
The text states that the master does not break his oath by riding the ma'dhun's mount unless two conditions are met: (1) the master intended the slave's property to be included in his oath, and (2) the slave does not have a debt that equals or exceeds his total property (mustaghriq).
Both conditions must be present; if either is missing, the oath is not broken.
4
Understand the mustaghriq condition
A 'mustaghriq' debt is one that overwhelms the slave's property, meaning the creditors have a prior claim. Such property effectively belongs to them, not the master, so it cannot be considered the master's for oath purposes.
This condition ensures that only property actually under the master's control counts.
5
Compare with other slave types
For a non-permitted slave, intention alone suffices (and agreement). For a mukaatab, even with intention, he does not break the oath because ownership is completely separate.
The difference: for ma'dhun, both intention and no debt are needed; for ghayr ma'dhun, only intention; for mukaatab, never.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومركب المأذون ليس لمولاه في حق الحلف إلا إذا لم يكن عليه دين مستغرق ونواه
[۲] قوله : المأذون؛ أي العبد المأذون له للتجارة، وأما غير المأذون فيحنث في قوله : لا يركب دابة فلان بركوب دابة عبده الغير المأذون، إذا نواه اتفاقاً، ولو ركب دابة مكاتب لا يحنث؛ لأن ملكه لا يضاف إلى المولى لا ذاتاً ولا يداً. كذا في «المحيط».
[۳] قوله : في حق الحلف؛ يعني إذا حَلَفَ لا يركب على مركب فلان فركب مركب عبده المأذون، فإنّه لا يحنث إلا بشرطين: أحدهما أن ينويه. وثانيهما : أن لا يكون عليه دين مستغرق.
[٤] قوله : مستغرق؛ - بكسر الراء -؛ أي دين يستغرق العبد بأن يكون مساوياً لقيمته.
And the mount of the permitted slave (al-ma'dhun) is not for his master in the matter of the oath unless there is no overwhelming debt on him and he intends it. [2] His saying: 'al-ma'dhun' means the slave permitted to trade. As for the non-permitted slave, he (the master) breaks the oath by riding the mount of his non-permitted slave if he intends it, by agreement. If he rides the mount of a mukaatab (slave under contract of manumission), he does not break the oath, because his ownership is not attributed to the master neither in essence nor in possession. So in al-Muheet. [3] His saying: 'in the matter of the oath' means if he swears 'I will not ride the mount of so-and-so' and then rides the mount of his permitted slave, he does not break the oath except under two conditions: one that he intends it (the slave's property), and two that there is no overwhelming debt on the slave. [4] His saying: 'overwhelming' (mustaghriq) - with kasra on the ra - meaning a debt that overwhelms the slave, i.e., equal to his value.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage from the 'Book of Oaths' (Kitab al-Ayman) addresses the case of a master who swears an oath concerning riding a specific mount (markab), but then rides the mount of his slave who has been permitted to trade (al-ma'dhun). The matn (base text) states: 'And the mount of the permitted slave is not for his master in the matter of the oath unless there is no overwhelming debt on him and he intends it.' The commentary (sharh) breaks this down. First, the term 'al-ma'dhun' refers to a slave whom the master has authorized to engage in trade. For such a slave, his property is considered distinct from the master's due to the permission. The general rule: if the master swears 'I will not ride the mount of so-and-so' (referring to himself), and he rides the mount of his permitted slave, he does not break his oath unless two conditions are met: (1) The master had the intention (niyyah) that the slave's property is included in his oath. (2) The slave does not have a debt that equals or exceeds the value of his property (debt mustaghriq), because if the slave is overwhelmed by debt, the creditors have a prior claim on his assets, and they are not considered the master's property. The commentary further clarifies the ruling for other types of slaves: For a non-permitted slave (ghayr al-ma'dhun), the master does break his oath if he rides the slave's mount, regardless of intention, because the slave's property is fully owned by the master. For a mukaatab (a slave under a contract of manumission), riding his mount does not break the oath because the mukaatab's ownership is independent and not attributed to the master. This passage illustrates the Hanafi principle that ownership attribution in oaths depends on the legal capacity of the slave and the master's intent.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave whom the master has granted permission to engage in trade; such a slave's property is considered distinct for the purpose of oaths.
مستغرق (mustaghriq): A debt that equals or exceeds the total value of the slave's property, thereby 'overwhelming' it and giving creditors priority.
دين (dayn): A debt or financial liability that the slave incurs in his trade.
مركب (markab): A mount or riding animal (e.g., horse, donkey) that is the subject of the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master swears, 'I will not ride the mount of my servant,' and then rides the mount of his slave who is permitted to trade (ma'dhun), when does the master break his oath?
A) He always breaks his oath because the slave belongs to him.
B) He breaks his oath only if the slave has no overwhelming debt.
C) He breaks his oath only if he intended the slave's property and the slave has no overwhelming debt.
D) He never breaks his oath because the slave's property is separate.

Key Takeaway
The master's oath about a mount is only broken if the mount belongs to a permitted slave, the master intended it, and the slave has no overwhelming debt.

Interpreting Oaths: Mubtada' vs Jawab
Ruling on interpreting ambiguous oath phrases as response vs independent statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on accepting an oath-taker's intention when the phrase is ambiguous between an independent statement and a response, and apply the principle to similar oath scenarios.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a claim of intention in oaths is accepted only if the words or context allow it.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya discusses a subtle issue in oaths: when the wording could be either an independent statement (mubtada') or a response (jawab), how do we interpret the oath-taker's intention? The ruling distinguishes between religious belief (dayāna) and legal judgment, and sets limits on when intention can override the apparent meaning. The key is that intention is only given weight when the situation or the words themselves support the claimed meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous oath phrase
Read the example: 'If I lunch today'. This could be an independent conditional statement (mubtada') or a response to an earlier condition (jawab). The addition of 'today' signals that it is likely mubtada'.
The phrase 'today' was added beyond the original condition, making it independent.
2
Determine the ruling based on apparent meaning
If the phrase is mubtada', the oath is broken by any lunch that day, without needing to lunch with a specific person. If it were jawab, the oath might be conditioned on an earlier statement.
The apparent meaning governs unless the oath-taker's intention is plausible and supported by context.
3
Apply the rule for accepting intention religiously
If the oath-taker claims he intended the phrase as a response, he is believed in religious matters (dayāna) because the possibility of it being a response exists and does not clearly contradict the apparent meaning.
This leniency is granted when the situation is ambiguous.
4
Recognize the limits of intention
Intention is not accepted when it contradicts both the uttered words and the situation. For example, if he intended a future day like 'tomorrow' when the context indicates 'today', or if he swears 'not to marry women' and intends only a specific number, the intention is invalid.
The Hanafi principle: intention only operates within the scope of the uttered words and contextual clues.
5
Relate to analogous cases
The passage gives analogous examples: swearing not to marry women while intending a specific number, or not to eat food while intending a specific amount. In both, the intention is not accepted because it contradicts the general apparent meaning.
This shows the consistency of the rule across different oath scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلما زاد اليوم، عُلِمَ أَنه كلامٌ مبتدأ ، فيحنتُ بمطلق التغدي في هذا اليوم، ولا يُشْتَرَطُ للحنث التَّغدي معه. والحق أن أصل [؟] ولا تخفى سخافته، فإنّ مثل هذا لا يشتبه على مثل موسى | الجواب أن لا يزيد على أصل المراد، وقد يعدلُ عنه لفائدة وضرورة، ولذا قالوا في : إن تغديت اليوم لو نوى الجواب صدق، وإنّما أطال موسى الة الكلام لا لما مر من الاشتباه، بل ليطول الخطاب والكلام مع الله ، ويظهر احتياجه في رفع الحوائج إلى عصاه ؛ ولهذا يطال الكلام مع الأحباء، وعند ذكر الأعراض. قوله : مبتدأ ؛ أي مستقل أريد الابتداء به غير متعلق بكلام الداعي، ولو قال في هذه الصورة: إنّي نويت الجواب دون الابتداءِ صُدّق ديانةً ؛ لأن احتمال كونه جواباً قائم لا قضاء لمخالفة الظاهر، فما فيه تخفيف عليه. ولو قال : إن تغديت ونوى ما بين الفور والأبد، كاليوم أو الغد لم يصدق أصلاً ؛ لأنَّ النّيّة إنما تعمل في الملفوظ ، والحالُ لا تدلّ عليه، فانتفى دلالة الحال ودلالة المقال، كما لو حلف لا يتزوج النساء ونوى عدداً ، أو لا يأكل طعاماً ونوى لقمة أو لقمتين لم يصح. كذا في «شرح تلخيص الجامع الكبير».
When he said 'today', it was known that it is an independent statement, so he breaks the oath by any lunch in this day, and it is not required for the oath to be broken that he lunch with him. The truth is that the original [؟] and its absurdity is not hidden, for such a thing is not ambiguous for someone like Moses. The answer is that he does not increase beyond the intended meaning, and he may deviate from it for benefit and necessity. Therefore they said concerning 'If I lunch today': if he intends a response, he is believed. Moses lengthened the speech not for the ambiguity mentioned, but to prolong the address and speech with God, and to show his need in raising needs to his staff; hence speech with loved ones is prolonged, and when mentioning ailments. He said: 'mubtada' means independent, intended to be begun, not connected to the speech of the caller. If in this case he says: 'I intended the response, not the beginning,' he is believed religiously, because the possibility of it being a response exists; there is no judgment contradicting the apparent, so there is leniency for him. If he says: 'If I lunch' and intends between immediacy and forever, like today or tomorrow, he is not believed at all, because intention only operates in the uttered words, and the situation does not indicate it; thus the indication of the situation and the indication of the words are absent, just as if he swears not to marry women and intends a number, or not to eat food and intends one or two morsels, it is not valid. As in Sharh Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage discusses the interpretation of an oath phrase when the wording could be either an independent statement (مبتدأ) or a response (جواب) to another's statement. The example given is someone saying "If I lunch today" in a context where it could be a conditional statement or a reply. The ruling is that if the phrase is recognized as an independent statement (e.g., because he added 'today' beyond what was in the original condition), then the oath is broken by any lunch on that day, without requiring that he lunch with a specific person. The author then explains that if the oath-taker claims he intended it as a response, he is believed in religious matters (ديانة) because the possibility exists and the apparent meaning does not contradict it. However, if he intends a time frame not indicated by the situation (like a future day), his intention is not accepted because there is no contextual clue. Analogous cases are given: swearing not to marry women while intending a specific number, or not to eat food while intending a specific amount, are not accepted. This shows that intention is only effective when it aligns with the uttered words or the context.

Key Terms
مبتدأ (mubtada'): An independent statement that begins a new speech, not connected to previous speech.
جواب (jawab): A response to another's statement, here specifically the conditional answer to an implied condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'If I lunch today' and intends it as a response to an earlier statement, is his intention accepted in religious matters?
A) No, because the apparent meaning is independent.
B) Yes, because the possibility of being a response exists.
C) Only if he also specifies a time.
D) No, because intention is never considered in oaths.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an oath-taker's claim of a specific intention is accepted in religious matters only if the wording or context does not contradict it.

Riding a Slave's Mount and Breaking an Oath
States the ruling on riding a slave's mount in oaths with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions under which riding a slave's mount breaks an oath not to ride the master's mount, and explain the legal reasoning behind each condition.

Core Idea
A slave's mount is not the master's for oaths unless the slave is debt-free and the oath-taker intends it.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah unpacks a nuanced rule about oaths and property attribution. It shows how the Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between the master's direct ownership and the slave's conditional property rights, and how intention and debt status affect the binding force of an oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومركب المأذون ليس لمولاه في حق الحلف إلا إذا لم يكن عليه دين مستغرق ونواه. قوله: المأذون؛ أي العبد المأذون له للتجارة، وأما غير المأذون فيحنث في قوله: لا يركب دابة فلان بركوب دابة عبده الغير المأذون، إذا نواه اتفاقاً، ولو ركب دابة مكاتب لا يحنث؛ لأن ملكه لا يضاف إلى المولى لا ذاتاً ولا يداً. كذا في «المحيط». قوله: في حق الحلف؛ يعني إذا حلف لا يركب على مركب فلان فركب مركب عبده المأذون، فإنه لا يحنث إلا بشرطين: أحدهما أن ينويه. وثانيهما: أن لا يكون عليه دين مستغرق. قوله: مستغرق؛ أي دين يستغرق العبد بأن يكون مساوياً لقيمته. أي إن حلف لا يركب دابة زيد فركب دابة عبده المأذون، فإن كان عليه دين مستغرق لرقبته وكسبه لا يحنث؛ لأن هذه الدابة ليست لزيد، وإن لم يكن عليه دين مستغرق، فإن نوى بدابة زيد دابته الخاصة لا يحنث، وإن نوى دابة هي ملك زيد أعم من أن تكون خاصة له، أو تكون دابة عبده المأذون فحينئذ يحنث. قوله: أي إن حلف لا يركب دابة زيد...الخ؛ اعلم أنه وضع في «الهداية» وغيرها هذه المسألة في الدابة، وغيره المصنف حيث أورد لفظ المركب تعميماً للحكم، فإن الدابة اسم لما يدب على الأرض مطلقاً، ويختص في اليمين بما يركب الناس عليه عرفاً كالفرس والحمار والبغل، وإن كان الحالف من أهل البدو والجمالين يدخل في حلفه البعير أيضاً، وإن كان من أهل الهند يدخل فيه الفيل أيضاً. كذا في «الفتح» و«منح الغفار». وبالجملة: تختص الدابة بما يركب عليه غالباً من الدواب بحسب اختلاف العرف، وفي الحلف بقوله: لا يركب مركب فلان يدخل غير الدواب أيضاً كالسفينة وغيرها، كما في الظهيرية، وقد غفل الشارح له عن هذه النكتة التي اعتبرها المصنف له في وضع المركب موضع الدابة، ففسّر كلامه بالتصوير في الدابة فاحفظه، فإنه من سوانح الوقت. قوله: لا يحنث؛ أي وإن نوى؛ لأن ملك العبد المديون بقدر قيمته ليس ملكاً حقيقياً، لا شرعاً ولا عرفاً. قوله: دين مستغرق؛ يشمل هذا ما إذا لم يكن عليه دين أصلاً، وما إذا كان عليه دين غير مستغرق لرقبته وكسبه. قوله: دابته الخاصة؛ أي الذي هو ملكه الخاص، وكذا إذا لم ينو شيئاً، فإنه يفهم من إضافة الدابة إليه، وهي لاختصاص ملكه الخاص. قوله: فحينئذ يحنث؛ لأن ملك العبد غير المديون بدين مستغرق للمولى، لكنه مضاف إلى العبد عرفاً وشرعاً؛ لذا قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «من باع عبداً وله مال، فماله للبائع إلا أن يشترطه المبتاع»، أخرجه أصحاب الصحاح الستة، فإذا نوى بإضافة
The mount of the authorized slave does not belong to his master for the purpose of an oath unless the slave has no debt covering his value and [the oath-taker] intends it. He said: 'al-ma'dhun' meaning the slave authorized for trade. As for the non-authorized, he breaks the oath by riding the mount of his non-authorized slave if he intends it, by agreement. If he rides the mount of a mukatab slave, he does not break the oath, because his ownership is not attributed to the master neither in essence nor in possession. As in al-Muhit. He said: 'for the purpose of an oath' meaning if he swears not to ride the mount of so-and-so and then rides the mount of his authorized slave, he does not break the oath except with two conditions: one that he intends it, and two that the slave has no debt covering his value. He said: 'mustaghriq' meaning a debt that covers the slave, equal to his value. That is, if he swears not to ride the riding animal of Zayd and then rides the riding animal of Zayd's authorized slave, then if the slave has a debt covering his person and earnings, he does not break the oath, because this riding animal is not Zayd's. If the slave has no debt covering his value, then if he intended by 'Zayd's riding animal' his specific riding animal, he does not break the oath. If he intended a riding animal that is Zayd's property, whether it is specifically his or the riding animal of his authorized slave, then in that case he breaks the oath. He said: 'That is, if he swears not to ride the riding animal of Zayd... etc.' Know that it is set in al-Hidayah and others that this issue concerns the riding animal (dabba), but the author changed it by using the word 'mount' (markab) to generalize the ruling. Dabba is a name for anything that crawls on the earth absolutely, and in oaths it is specific to what people ride customarily, like horse, donkey, mule. If the oath-taker is from the Bedouins or camel-drivers, camels are included; if from the people of India, elephants are included. As in al-Fath and Manh al-Ghaffar. In summary, dabba is specific to what is commonly ridden among riding animals according to the variation of custom. In the oath 'I will not ride so-and-so's mount,' non-riding-animals like ships and others are also included, as in al-Zahiriyyah. The commentator overlooked this subtlety which the author considered in putting 'mount' in place of 'riding animal,' and he interpreted his speech by depicting it as a riding animal, so safeguard it, for it is one of the inspirations of the time. He said: 'He does not break the oath' meaning even if he intends [the slave's mount], because the property of the slave who has debt covering his value is not real ownership, neither legally nor customarily. He said: 'debt covering his value' includes the case where he has no debt at all, and the case where he has a debt not covering his person and earnings. He said: 'his specific riding animal' meaning his private property. Likewise if he did not intend anything, for it is understood from the addition of the riding animal to him, and it is for his private ownership. He said: 'then in that case he breaks the oath,' because the property of the slave without a debt covering his value belongs to the master, but it is attributed to the slave customarily and legally. Hence the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'He who sells a slave who has property, his property belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates it.' Reported by the authors of the six authentic collections. So if he intends by the addition...
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling on whether riding the mount of another person's authorized slave (عبد مأذون) breaks an oath not to ride that person's mount. The general principle is that the slave's mount is not considered the master's property for oath purposes, so riding it does not break the oath. However, there are two exceptions (conditions): (1) The slave must not have a debt covering his entire value (دين مستغرق) — if he does, his property is so encumbered that it is not considered the master's in any meaningful sense. (2) The oath-taker must specifically intend (ينوي) the slave's mount when he says 'mount of so-and-so.' If he intends only the master's personal mount, or if he has no specific intention, the default interpretation is that it refers to the master's private property. The author also discusses the terminology: the base text uses 'mount' (مركب) rather than 'riding animal' (دابة) to include all conveyances like ships, not just riding animals. The ruling is supported by a hadith that when a slave with property is sold, his property goes to the seller (the previous master) unless the buyer stipulates it, indicating that the slave's property is legally attributed to the master only in certain contexts.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade.
دين مستغرق (dayn mustaghriq): A debt that covers the entire value of the slave (his raqabah and kasb), leaving no net equity.
مركب (markab): Any mount or conveyance, including riding animals and vehicles like ships.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the authorized slave has a debt covering his entire value, and the oath-taker intends the slave's mount, does riding the slave's mount break the oath?
A) Yes, because intention overrides.
B) Yes, because the slave's property is always the master's.
C) No, because the debt severs attribution.
D) No, because intention is irrelevant when there is debt.

Key Takeaway
The slave's mount is attributed to the master for oaths only when the slave is debt-free and the oath-taker intends it; otherwise, it remains the slave's for oath purposes.
