Oath Not to Ride Zayd's Animal: The Case of the Slave's Animal and Intention
Ruling on oath broken by riding slave's animal, with conditions and ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether riding a slave's animal breaks an oath not to ride the master's animal, and explain how the slave's debt and the swearer's intention affect the ruling.

Core Idea
An oath not to ride Zayd's animal is not broken by riding his slave's animal if the slave's debt exhausts his value; otherwise, it depends on intention.

This lesson examines a detailed ruling from the Hanafi school on oaths. The key scenario: a person swears not to ride Zayd's animal, then rides the animal of Zayd's authorized slave. The ruling is not straightforward; it hinges on whether the slave is burdened by a debt that consumes his entire value and earnings, and on what the swearer intended by 'Zayd's animal'. We will explore the conditions, the proof from hadith, and the disagreement among the early Hanafi masters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and General Ruling
Consider the basic case: a person swears not to ride Zayd's animal, then rides the animal of Zayd's authorized slave. The ruling is that if the slave has a debt that exhausts his value and earnings, the oath is not broken. This is the starting point.
The slave's debt must be 'mustaghriq' (exhaustive) — consuming both the slave's capital (raqabah) and earnings (kasb).
2
Analyze the Condition of the Slave's Debt
Examine the two subcases: (1) exhaustive debt: the animal is not considered Zayd's property, so no hanth. (2) Non-exhaustive or no debt: the animal is legally Zayd's (by hadith), but custom attributes it to the slave. Then intention becomes relevant.
The hadith 'من باع عبدا وله مال، فماله للبائع' establishes legal ownership for the master, but custom (urf) attributes it to the slave.
3
Examine the Role of Intention (Niyya)
When the slave has no exhaustive debt, the oath is broken only if the swearer intended Zayd's absolute ownership (i.e., to include property attributed to the slave). If he intended only Zayd's personal animal, or had no intention, the oath is not broken.
Abu Hanifa's view: intention makes a difference. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad have stricter views.
4
Study the Hadith Proof and Its Legal Implication
The hadith proves that the slave's property is legally the master's. This supports the ruling that the animal is Zayd's when no exhaustive debt exists. However, custom (urf) attributes it to the slave, hence the need for intention to bridge the gap.
The hadith is narrated by the six authentic collections (Sunnah ahad, zanni al-thubut but qati al-dalala in this context).
5
Compare the Views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad
Abu Hanifa: hanth depends on intention (if he intends absolute ownership). Abu Yusuf: hanth in all cases if he intends (even if he intends only personal animal? The text says 'إذا نواه' meaning if he intends Zayd's ownership generally). Muhammad: hanth even without intention. Note the practical differences.
The commentator does not explicitly resolve the ikhtilaf, but Abu Hanifa's view is presented in detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان 
اليمين في الدخول والسكنى والخروج والإتيان والركوب
أي إن حلف لا يركب دابة زيد فركب دابة عبده المأذون، فإن كان عليه دين مستغرق لرقبته وكسبه لا يحنث؛ لأن هذه الدابة ليست لزيد، وإن لم يكن عليه دين مستغرق، فإن نوى بدابة زيد دابته الخاصة لا يحنث، وإن نوى دابة هي ملك زيد أعم من أن تكون خاصة له، أو تكون دابة عبده المأذون فحينئذ يحنث. قوله: أي إن حلف لا يركب دابة زيد...الخ؛ اعلم أنه وضع في «الهداية» وغيرها هذه المسألة في الدابة، وغيره المصنف حيث أورد لفظ المركب تعميما للحكم، فإن الدابة اسم لما يدب على الأرض مطلقا، ويختص في اليمين بما يركب الناس عليه عرفا كالفرس والحمار والبغل، وإن كان الحالف من أهل البدو والجمالين يدخل في حلفه البعير أيضا، وإن كان من أهل الهند يدخل فيه الفيل أيضا. كذا في «الفتح» و«منح الغفار». وبالجملة: تختص الدابة بما يركب عليه غالبا من الدواب بحسب اختلاف العرف، وفي الحلف بقوله: لا يركب مركب فلان يدخل غير الدواب أيضا كالسفينة وغيرها، كما في الظهيرية، وقد غفل الشارح له عن هذه النكتة التي اعتبرها المصنف له في وضع المركب موضع الدابة، ففسر كلامه بالتصوير في الدابة فاحفظه، فإنه من سوانح الوقت. قوله: لا يحنث؛ أي وإن نوى؛ لأن ملك العبد المديون بقدر قيمته ليس ملكا أصولي، لا شرعا ولا عرفا. قوله: دين مستغرق؛ يشمل هذا ما إذا لم يكن عليه دين أصلا، وما إذا كان عليه دين غير مستغرق لرقبته وكسبه. قوله: دابته الخاصة؛ أي الذي هو ملكه الخاص، وكذا إذا لم ينو شيئا، فإنه يفهم من إضافة الدابة إليه، وهي لاختصاص ملكه الخاص. قوله: فحينئذ يحنث؛ لأن ملك العبد غير المديون بدين مستغرق للمولى، لكنه مضاف إلى العبد عرفا وشرعا؛ لذا قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «من باع عبدا وله مال، فماله للبائع إلا أن يشترطه المبتاع»، أخرجه أصحاب الصحاح الستة، فإذا نوى بإضافة الدابة إلى زيد مطلق الملك أعم من أن يكون مضافا إليه فقط، أو يكون مضافا إلى غيره أيضا، دخل ملك العبد أيضا فيه بخلاف ما إذا لم ينويه. وقال أبو يوسف: يحنث في الوجوه كلها إذا نواه. وقال محمد: يحنث وإن لم ينو.
Book of Oaths. The oath concerning entering, dwelling, exiting, coming, and riding. That is, if he swore not to ride Zayd's animal, and then rode the animal of Zayd's authorized slave, then if the slave has a debt that exhausts his value and earnings, he does not break his oath, because this animal is not Zayd's. And if he does not have an exhaustive debt, then if he intended by 'Zayd's animal' his specific personal animal, he does not break his oath. And if he intended an animal that is Zayd's property, whether it is specifically his or is the animal of his authorized slave, then he breaks his oath. His statement: 'That is, if he swore not to ride Zayd's animal...' Know that in al-Hidayah and other works, this issue was placed under 'animal', but the author changed it by using the word 'mount' to generalize the ruling, for 'dābba' refers to anything that crawls on the earth absolutely, but in oaths it is restricted to what people customarily ride such as horses, donkeys, and mules. If the swearer is from the desert people and camel drivers, camels also enter into his oath; if from India, elephants also enter. Likewise in al-Fath and Minah al-Ghaffar. In summary: dābba is restricted to that which is usually ridden among animals according to custom. In swearing by saying 'I will not ride the mount of so-and-so', non-animals like ships also enter, as in al-Zahiriyyah. The commentator overlooked this point which the author considered in placing 'mount' in the position of 'animal', so he interpreted his words by depicting the animal, so preserve it, for it is one of the inspirations of the moment. His statement: 'He does not break his oath' meaning even if he intended, because the property of an indebted slave to the extent of his value is not considered ownership legally, neither by Shari'ah nor custom. His statement: 'Debt that exhausts' includes the case when he has no debt at all, and when he has a debt that does not exhaust his value and earnings. His statement: 'His specific personal animal' meaning what is his exclusive property; likewise if he had no intention, for it is understood from the attribution of the animal to him that it is for his exclusive ownership. His statement: 'Then he breaks his oath' because the property of the slave who is not indebted by an exhaustive debt belongs to the master, but it is attributed to the slave by custom and Shari'ah; thus the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever sells a slave who has property, his property belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates it,' narrated by the authors of the six authentic collections. So if he intends by the attribution of the animal to Zayd absolute ownership, whether it is attributed only to him or attributed to others as well, the property of the slave also enters into it, unlike when he does not intend it. And Abu Yusuf said: He breaks his oath in all cases if he intends it. And Muhammad said: He breaks his oath even if he does not intend.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a ruling on oaths concerning riding. It begins by presenting a scenario: a person swears not to ride Zayd's animal, then rides the animal of Zayd's authorized slave (i.e., a slave permitted to trade). The ruling depends on two factors: (1) whether the slave has a debt that consumes his entire value and earnings, and (2) the swearer's intention (niyya). If the slave is burdened with a debt that fully exhausts his value and earnings, then the animal is effectively not owned by Zayd (since creditors have first claim), so the oath is not broken. If the debt is less than exhaustive or none, then Zayd does own the animal, but the animal is still attributed to the slave in custom and law. In that case, the oath is broken only if the swearer intended Zayd's absolute ownership (including property attributed to his slave). If he intended only Zayd's personal property, the oath is not broken. The commentator then expands on the definition of 'dābba' (animal) in oaths, noting that it is customarily what people ride, and the author of the base text used 'mount' (markab) to generalize the ruling to include non-animal conveyances like ships. This is a subtle point the commentator says was missed by some. The passage then quotes a hadith: when a slave with property is sold, the property belongs to the seller unless stipulated otherwise, indicating that the slave's property is legally the master's. This supports the ruling that Zayd's ownership of the slave's animal is real. The passage concludes with the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf holds that the oath is broken in all cases if the swearer intended Zayd's ownership, while Muhammad holds it is broken even without intention. The commentator implies that Abu Hanifa's position is that intention matters, and the hadith supports the view that the slave's property is legally the master's, but custom attributes it to the slave.

Key Terms
دابة (dābba): In oaths, dābba is customarily what people ride, such as horses, donkeys, mules; for Bedouins includes camels; for Indians includes elephants. It is not all crawling creatures.
دين مستغرق (dayn mustaghriq): A debt that exhausts the slave's value (رقبة) and earnings (كسب), meaning the slave's entire worth is consumed by debt, so he is effectively not owned by the master for the purposes of property.
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath, which entails expiation (kaffarah).
نوى (nawā): Intention (niyya) of the swearer regarding the scope of the oath's subject.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a man swears not to ride Zayd's animal, and then rides the animal of Zayd's authorized slave who has a debt that fully exhausts the slave's value and earnings, does the oath break?
A) Yes, because the animal belongs to Zayd.
B) No, because the animal is not considered Zayd's due to the exhaustive debt.
C) Yes, if the swearer intended Zayd's absolute ownership.
D) No, because the slave's animal is never considered Zayd's.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on oaths involving slave-owned property balances ownership (proved by hadith) with custom and intention, leading to a nuanced position that distinguishes between exhaustive debt and non-exhaustive debt, and between specific and general intention.

Oath on Slave's Property: Intra-Hanafi Positions on Intention
Commentary on intra-Hanafi dispute regarding inclusion of slave's property in an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on whether an oath about an animal includes the slave's animal and distinguish between the roles of intention and debt in determining hanth.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a slave's property is the master's, so it is automatically included in oaths — but the three imams differ on whether intention matters.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: If a man swears regarding a particular animal owned by Zaid, and Zaid's slave also owns an animal of the same type, does the oath cover the slave's animal? Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani each gave different answers depending on the role of intention and the financial situation of the oath-taker. We will parse their arguments and the rationale behind them.

Guided Path
1
State the facts of the case
A master swears about Zaid's animal; Zaid's slave owns a similar animal. The master takes the slave's animal. Does he break his oath?
The oath is about a specific type of property owned by a specific person.
2
Identify the three positions
Abu Hanifa: breaks only if he intended the slave's animal. Abu Yusuf: breaks if he intended (but not if he didn't). Muhammad: breaks even without intention.
3
Analyse the rationale for Muhammad's position
The slave's property is the master's; therefore the animal is included automatically. No need for intention.
4
Apply the debt classification
The phrase 'in all cases' (الوجوه كلها) refers to whether the oath-taker has no debt, full debt, or partial debt. This classification affects the legal consequence?
The commentator mentions this classification but does not elaborate fully here.
5
Note the reference to Abu Hanifa's defence
The commentator alludes to a previous discussion defending Abu Hanifa's view that intention is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو يوسف الله : يحنث في الوجوه كلها إذا نواه.
وقال محمد ه: يحنث وإن لم ينو
الدابة إلى زيد مطلق الملكِ أعم من أن يكون مضافاً إليه فقط ، أو يكون مضافاً إلى غيره أيضاً، دخل ملك العبد أيضاً فيه بخلاف ما إذا لم ينويه. [1] قوله : يحنث في الوجوه كلّها ؛ أي فيما إذا لم يكن عليه دين ، أو كان دين مستغرق أو غير مستغرق.
[٢) قوله : يحنث ؛ أي في الوجوه كلّها ؛ لأن دابة العبد مملوك له ؛ لأنَّ العبد وما في يده ملك لمولاه، فلا يحتاج في دخوله في الحلف إلى النية ، وقد عرفت الجواب عنه وعن
ما ذكره أبو يوسف له أثناء التقرير السابق لتوجيه مذهب أبي حنيفة .
And Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) said: He breaks the oath in all cases if he intended it. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: He breaks the oath even if he did not intend. The animal belonging to Zaid, absolute ownership is more general than if it is added only to him, or if it is added to another as well; the ownership of the slave is also included in it, unlike if he did not intend it. [1] His statement: 'He breaks the oath in all cases' — meaning whether he had no debt, or had a debt that covers all assets or not all. [2) His statement: 'He breaks the oath' — meaning in all cases, because the slave's animal is owned by him, because the slave and what is in his hand are the property of his master, so there is no need for intention for its inclusion in the oath. And you have known the answer to this and to what Abu Yusuf mentioned in the previous discussion concerning the clarification of Abu Hanifa's position.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn that presents the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions of Abu Hanifa) regarding an oath that involves property of a slave. The matn states that Abu Yusuf holds that the oath is broken in all cases only if the person intended the slave's property, while Muhammad holds that it is broken even without intention. The commentator then explains that the phrase 'in all cases' covers both situations: when the oath-taker has no debt, or when he has debt that is either total or partial. The rationale provided is that the slave's animal is considered the property of the master because everything a slave owns belongs to his master. Therefore, when the master swears an oath regarding a specific animal, the slave's version of that animal is automatically included without requiring a separate intention. The commentator then references a previous discussion that defends Abu Hanifa's position (likely that the oath is not broken unless intended). This shows the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on this issue.

Key Terms
يحنث (yahinthu): He breaks his oath (يخالف يمينه) and becomes liable for the consequence (expiation or nullification).
دابة العبد (dābbat al-‘abd): An animal (riding beast) owned by the slave.
الوجوه كلها (al-wujūh kullihā): All cases: specifically whether the oath-taker has no debt, a debt covering all assets, or a partial debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, if a man swears 'By Allah, I will not take Zaid's animal' and Zaid's slave owns an animal, and the man takes it without intending the slave's animal, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, he breaks the oath even without intention.
B) No, because he did not intend the slave's animal.
C) It depends on whether he has debt.
D) It depends on the type of animal.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, the property of a slave is attributed to the master, but the three imams differ on whether intention is required to include it in the oath.

Defining Eating and Drinking in Hanafi Oath Law
Definitions of eating and drinking with illustrative examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definitions of 'eating' (akl) and 'drinking' (shurb) and apply them to determine whether an oath is broken in scenarios such as swallowing an egg whole, sucking grapes, or consuming milk with bread.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, eating includes swallowing whole solids; a liquid becomes 'eaten' when combined with food.

This lesson examines the foundational definitions of 'eating' and 'drinking' as they apply to oaths. The Hanafi school provides precise criteria: eating is delivering to the stomach anything chewable, regardless of chewing; drinking is delivering liquids alone. These definitions have direct implications for oath-breaking. We will work through the definitions and apply them to concrete examples from the source text, including eggs, grapes, and milk with bread.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definitions
Find where the text defines 'akl' and 'shurb'. Note that eating involves chewable substances, drinking involves non-chewable liquids.
2
Analyze the components of 'akl'
Eating includes delivery to the stomach of something that can be chewed, whether chewed or not. Swallowing whole (e.g., an egg) counts.
3
Analyze the components of 'shurb'
Drinking is delivery of liquids that cannot be chewed. If a liquid is consumed with a solid, it becomes 'eaten' (e.g., milk with bread becomes eaten).
4
Apply to the examples
Test the definitions: egg (swallowing = eating → broken oath), grapes (sucking without swallowing = not eating → oath not broken), milk with bread (milk becomes eaten → broken oath).
The text clarifies that if milk is consumed alone, it is drinking; with bread, it is eating.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل اليمين في الأكل والشرب
( ويتقيد الأكل من هذه النخلة" بثمرها (۳) (۱) ؛ لأن المعنى الحقيقي مهجور
[١] قوله : الأكل ؛ هو عبارة عن إيصال ما يحتمل المضغ بفيه إلى الجوف كخبز وفاكهة مضغ أو لم يمضغ، كما إذا ابتلعه. والشرب: عبارة عن إيصال ما لا يحتمل المضغ من المائعات بفيه إلى الجوف : كماء وعسل غير جامد، فلو حلف لا يأكل بيضة يحنث ببلعها، وفي لا يأكل عنباً لا يحنث بمصه، ولو حلف لا يأكل هذا اللبن فأكله بخبز أو تمر حنث ؛ ؛ لأنَّ المائع إنما يسمّى
مشروباً إذا تناوله وحده، وإلا فهو مأكول كذا في «البدائع» و«تنوير الأبصار»(۳). [٢] قوله : من هذه النخلة - بالفتح - شجرُ التمر، وكذا من النخلة بدون هذه، ومثله الكرم ؛ أي شجرُ العنب، قال في «التلويح»: «حلف لا يأكـل مـن هـذه الشجرة، فإن نوى ما يحتمله الكلام فعلى ما نوى، وإلا فإن كانت الشجرةُ مما يؤكل كالريباس فعلى الحقيقة، وإلا فإن كانت مثمرة كالنخلة فعلى ثمرتها، وإلا فعلى ثمنها، كشجرة الخلاف»(۳).
[۳] قوله : بثمرها ؛ - بفتح التاء المثلثة والميم - ، والمراد به ما يعم التمر، فإنّه يحنث بأكل الجمار أيضاً، وهو شيء أبيض لين في رأس النخلة، ولا يحنث بما خرج منها بصنعة كالخل والدبس ونحوهما.
[٤] قوله : مهجور حساً ؛ أي متروك حسّاً، فإنّه لا يؤكل عين النخلة، فوجبَ
حمله على المجاز، وهو ما يتولّد منها.
(۱) وكذا دبسها غير المطبوخ ؛ لأنه أضاف اليمين إلى ما لا يؤكل فينصرف إلى ما يخرج منها بلا صنع أحد تجوزاً باسم السبب، وهو النخلة في المسبب، وهو الخارج ؛ لأنها سبب فيه لكن شرط أن لا يتغير بصفة حادثة. ينظر : «مجمع الأنهر» (١ : ٥٥٦ - ٥٥٧).
(۲) تنوير الأبصار (٣: ٧٦٥ - ٧٦٦).
(۳) انتهى من «التلويح»(۱ : ۱۷۷).
Section on Oaths Related to Eating and Drinking
And eating from this palm tree is restricted to its fruit, because the literal meaning is abandoned.
[1] His statement: 'Eating' is the delivery to the stomach of something that can be chewed, like bread and fruit, whether chewed or not, such as if he swallows it. And 'drinking' is the delivery to the stomach of liquids that cannot be chewed, like water and non-solid honey. So if he swears not to eat an egg, he breaks the oath by swallowing it. If he swears not to eat grapes, he does not break the oath by sucking them. If he swears not to eat this milk and eats it with bread or dates, he breaks the oath, because a liquid is only called a drink if taken alone; otherwise it is considered food. This is as in al-Bada'i' and Tanwir al-Absar.
[2] His statement: 'From this palm tree' — with fatha (on the letter) — it is the date tree, and similarly 'from the palm tree' without 'this', and likewise the grapevine. He said in al-Talwih: 'If he swears not to eat from this tree, then if he intended what the speech could bear, it is according to his intention. Otherwise, if the tree is edible like rhubarb, then it is upon the literal meaning. If it is fruitful like a palm tree, then it is upon its fruit. Otherwise, it is upon its value, like the willow tree.'
[3] His statement: 'Its fruit' — with fatha on the ta and mim — and what is meant is what includes dates. For he breaks the oath by eating the jamar (soft white core) as well, which is a white soft thing at the top of the palm tree. He does not break the oath by something produced from it through processing, like vinegar, molasses, and the like.
[4] His statement: 'Abandoned sensually' means left sensually, for the actual palm tree is not eaten, so it must be carried metaphorically to what is generated from it.
(1) And similarly its uncooked molasses, because he added the oath to what is not eaten, so it turns to what comes out of it without anyone's effort, metaphorically by the name of the cause, which is the palm tree, for the effect, which is the product; because it is a cause for it, provided it does not change by an emerging quality. Refer to Majma' al-Anhur (1:556-557).
(2) Tanwir al-Absar (3:765-766).
(3) End from al-Talwih (1:177).
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on oaths related to eating and drinking. The matn states that when someone swears 'I will not eat from this palm tree,' the oath is restricted to the fruit of that tree. The commentator provides detailed definitions and applications. First, 'eating' is defined as delivering to the stomach something that can be chewed, regardless of whether it is actually chewed — even if swallowed whole. Examples: if one swears not to eat an egg, swallowing it whole breaks the oath; but if one swears not to eat grapes, just sucking the juice without swallowing the pulp does not break the oath. 'Drinking' is defined as delivering liquids to the stomach. However, if a liquid is consumed with solid food (like milk with bread), it is considered 'eating' not 'drinking,' so the oath is broken. The commentary then explains the rule for trees: an oath about 'this palm tree' is by default about its fruit, because the tree itself is not eaten. The commentator cites al-Talwih to clarify that if the tree is edible (like rhubarb), the oath applies to the tree itself; if it is fruitful (like a palm), to the fruit; otherwise, to its value. Further, 'fruit' includes not only dates but also the soft white core (jamar) at the top of the palm. However, items produced from the fruit by human processing (like vinegar or molasses) are not included unless the oath was intended for them. The reason given is that the literal meaning (the tree itself) is abandoned because it is not eaten, so the oath is interpreted metaphorically as referring to what the tree produces.

Key Terms
الأكل (al-akl): Delivering something that can be chewed (solid) to the stomach, whether chewed or not.
الشرب (al-shurb): Delivering liquids that cannot be chewed to the stomach.
ثمر (thamr): Fruit of a tree; for the palm tree, it includes dates and the soft white core (jamar).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition, which of the following would break an oath not to eat?
A) Swallowing a whole egg without chewing.
B) Sucking the juice from a grape and spitting out the pulp.
C) Drinking milk mixed with bread but not swallowing the bread.
D) Drinking water alone.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath law, 'eating' includes swallowing whole solids, and a liquid becomes 'eaten' when combined with food.

Interpreting Oaths About Trees and Their Fruit
Rules for interpreting oaths about trees and defining 'fruit'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how a Hanafi judge interprets an oath involving a tree, distinguishing between edible trees, fruitful trees, and non-fruitful trees, and identify what counts as 'fruit' for a palm tree.

Core Idea
An oath about a palm tree automatically refers to its fruit; the fruit includes the core (jamar) but excludes processed derivatives like vinegar.

This lesson explores the Hanafi rules for interpreting oaths that mention trees. When someone swears 'I will not eat from this palm tree,' the literal meaning is abandoned because the tree is inedible. Instead, the oath is understood metaphorically as referring to the fruit. However, the scope of 'fruit' requires careful definition: it includes dates and even the soft white core (jamar), but not items produced through human processing such as vinegar or molasses. We will examine the classification of trees and the principles of linguistic interpretation that govern these oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of tree
Determine if the tree is edible (e.g., rhubarb), fruitful (e.g., date palm, grapevine), or neither (e.g., willow). The interpretation varies accordingly.
2
Apply the default interpretation
For edible trees, the oath applies literally to the tree itself. For fruitful trees, it applies metaphorically to the fruit. For non-fruitful trees, it applies to the value (thaman).
3
Define 'fruit' for a palm tree
Fruit includes dates (tamar) and the white core (jamar) that grows at the top. Processed items like vinegar, molasses (unless uncooked and unchanged) are not included.
The text excludes items produced with human effort (bina' san'ah). Uncooked molasses is included if not changed by an emerging quality.
4
Apply to scenarios
Consider if the oath covers eating jamar: yes. Does it cover date vinegar: no, because it is produced through processing. Does it cover uncooked molasses: yes, as per the footnote.
The reasoning: the oath is metaphorically directed to what the tree produces without human intervention.

Definitions of Eating and Drinking in Oaths
Defining akl and shurb for oath jurisprudence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of akl and shurb and apply them to determine whether an oath is broken in scenarios involving food and drink.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, eating includes all chewable matter conveyed to the stomach; drinking is liquids consumed alone—mixing with solids turns drink into food.

This passage from the Hanafi juristic tradition clarifies what counts as 'eating' (akl) and 'drinking' (shurb) under oath. The definitions are based on the nature of the substance and how it is consumed. Understanding these definitions is crucial for determining when an oath is broken (hanith).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act being defined is either eating (akl) or drinking (shurb) in the context of oaths. The purpose is to determine if the oath is broken.
Oaths often use terms like 'eat' or 'drink' which have specific legal meanings.
2
State the definition of akl
Eating (akl) is conveying any chewable substance to the stomach, regardless of whether it is chewed or swallowed whole.
Chewing is not a requirement; swallowing whole counts.
3
State the definition of shurb
Drinking (shurb) is conveying a non-chewable liquid to the stomach when consumed alone.
If a liquid is consumed with a solid, it becomes part of eating (akl).
4
Apply definitions to borderline cases
Analyze examples from the text: swallowing an egg whole (akl), sucking grapes without swallowing (not akl), eating milk with bread (akl).
The key distinction: chewability and whether the substance is consumed alone.
5
Derive the ruling for the oath
If the act matches the definition of akl or shurb (according to the oath), the oath is broken (hanith).
The ruling depends on the exact wording of the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل اليمين في الأكل والشرب
ويتقيد الأكل من هذه النخلة بثمرها

[١] قوله : الأكل ؛ هو عبارة عن إيصال ما يحتمل المضغ بفيه إلى الجوف كخبز وفاكهة مضغ أو لم يمضغ، كما إذا ابتلعه. والشرب: عبارة عن إيصال ما لا يحتمل المضغ من المائعات بفيه إلى الجوف : كماء وعسل غير جامد، فلو حلف لا يأكل بيضة يحنث ببلعها، وفي لا يأكل عنباً لا يحنث بمصه، ولو حلف لا يأكل هذا اللبن فأكله بخبز أو تمر حنث ؛ لأنَّ المائع إنما يسمّى مشروباً إذا تناوله وحده، وإلا فهو مأكول كذا في «البدائع» و«تنوير الأبصار»(۳).
Chapter on the oath regarding eating and drinking. And the eating from this palm tree is restricted to its fruit. [1] He said: eating; it is the conveying of what can be chewed by the mouth to the stomach, like bread and fruit, whether chewed or not chewed, as when one swallows it. And drinking: it is the conveying of what cannot be chewed from liquids by the mouth to the stomach, like water and non-solid honey. So if one swears not to eat an egg, he breaks the oath by swallowing it. And if he swears not to eat grapes, he does not break the oath by sucking them. And if he swears not to eat this milk and then eats it with bread or dates, he breaks the oath, because the liquid is only called a drink if consumed alone; otherwise it is eaten. This is found in al-Bada'i' and Tanwir al-Absar.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage from the commentary defines the terms 'eating' (akl) and 'drinking' (shurb) specifically for the context of oaths. The author begins by restating the matn: 'The oath regarding this palm tree is restricted to its fruit.' He then explains the meaning of the word 'eating' (akl) as the act of conveying something that can be chewed to the stomach, whether chewed or swallowed whole. Drinking (shurb) is conveying liquids that cannot be chewed. The examples clarify borderline cases: swallowing an egg whole is considered 'eating' and breaks the oath; sucking grapes without swallowing does not. Eating liquid milk together with bread or dates is considered 'eating' because the liquid is consumed as part of a meal, not alone. This classification is based on the principle in Hanafi fiqh that liquids consumed with solids are treated as food, not drink.

Key Terms
الأكل (al-akl): The act of conveying chewable matter to the stomach, regardless of chewing.
الشرب (al-shurb): The act of conveying non-chewable liquids to the stomach.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition, if someone swears not to eat an egg and swallows it whole without chewing, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because swallowing whole counts as eating.
B) No, because he did not chew.
C) Yes, only if he chews.
D) No, because eggs are not typically eaten whole.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definitions of akl and shurb pivot on the chewability of the substance and the mode of consumption, not on chewing.

Oath from a Palm Tree: Scope and Application
Ruling with proof from al-Talwih on interpreting oaths about trees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for interpreting an oath involving a fruit-bearing tree and apply it to determine whether specific items break the oath.

Core Idea
An oath concerning a fruit-bearing tree applies to its fruit, not the tree itself.

When someone swears by a palm tree, does the oath apply to the tree, its fruit, or something else? This passage clarifies that the ruling depends on the context and the type of tree. The key principle is that for fruit-bearing trees, the oath is restricted to the fruit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object of the oath
Determine what the oath is about – e.g., 'I will not eat from this palm tree'.
2
Check the person's intention
If the person had a specific intention that the words can bear, follow that intention. Otherwise, apply default rules.
The default rules depend on the tree type.
3
Classify the tree
Is the tree itself edible (like quince), fruit-bearing (like date palm), or neither (like poplar)?
4
Apply the default rule
If edible: oath applies to the tree itself (literal). If fruit-bearing: oath applies to its fruit. If neither: oath applies to its wood.
5
Include or exclude specific items
Fruit includes natural parts like the palm heart (jammar). Processed products like vinegar and molasses are excluded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتقيد الأكل من هذه النخلة بثمرها [٢] قوله : من هذه النخلة - بالفتح - شجرُ التمر، وكذا من النخلة بدون هذه، ومثله الكرم ؛ أي شجرُ العنب، قال في «التلويح»: «حلف لا يأكـل مـن هـذه الشجرة، فإن نوى ما يحتمله الكلام فعلى ما نوى، وإلا فإن كانت الشجرةُ مما يؤكل كالريباس فعلى الحقيقة، وإلا فإن كانت مثمرة كالنخلة فعلى ثمرتها، وإلا فعلى ثمنها، كشجرة الخلاف»(۳). [٣] قوله : بثمرها ؛ - بفتح التاء المثلثة والميم - ، والمراد به ما يعم التمر، فإنّه يحنث بأكل الجمار أيضاً، وهو شيء أبيض لين في رأس النخلة، ولا يحنث بما خرج منها بصنعة كالخل والدبس ونحوهما.
And the eating from this palm tree is restricted to its fruit. [2] He said: from this palm tree - with fatha on nun - it is the date tree, and likewise from the palm tree without 'this', and similarly the vine, i.e., the grapevine. He said in al-Talwih: 'If someone swears not to eat from this tree, and he intends what the word can bear, then it is according to his intention. Otherwise, if the tree is edible, like the quince, then it is according to the literal meaning. If it is fruit-bearing, like the date palm, then it is according to its fruit. If not, then according to its wood, like the poplar tree.' [3] He said: by its fruit - with fatha on ta and mim - and it means what includes dates, so he breaks the oath by eating the jammar (palm heart) also, which is a white soft thing at the top of the palm tree, and he does not break the oath by what comes out of it through processing, like vinegar and molasses and similar.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the matn's ruling that an oath related to a specific palm tree is restricted to its fruit. The commentator explains that the term 'palm tree' linguistically refers to the date tree. He then cites al-Talwih, which provides a general rule: if someone swears by a tree, the interpretation depends on his intention. If he had no specific intention, then: if the tree is itself edible (like a quince tree), the oath applies to the actual tree (eating the fruit or the tree itself? Actually, for quince, the fruit and tree? The text says 'على الحقيقة' meaning literal meaning, which for an edible tree would be eating its edible parts). If it is a fruit-bearing tree (like a date palm), the oath applies to its fruit. If it is not fruit-bearing (like a poplar), the oath applies to its wood (or value). The second comment clarifies that 'fruit' includes the palm heart (jammar), which is the soft white core, but excludes products made from the fruit through human processing, such as vinegar or molasses. This distinction is based on whether the item is directly from the tree (fruit) or altered by human action.

Key Terms
النخلة (al-nakhla): The date palm tree.
الكرم (al-karm): The grapevine.
الجمار (al-jammar): The white soft core at the top of the palm tree, considered fruit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to eat from a specific date palm tree and then eats vinegar made from its dates, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the fruit is from the tree.
B) No, because vinegar is a manufactured product.
C) Yes, if the vinegar is raw.
D) No, because the oath was on the tree itself.

Key Takeaway
An oath concerning a fruit-bearing tree applies to its natural fruit, not processed products.

Dispute on Eating Barley by Crushing: Literal vs. Customary Meaning
Discussion of Abu Hanifa's and Sahibayn's disagreement on oath regarding barley.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the basis of the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding whether crushing barley breaks an oath, and apply the usul principle of haqiqah vs. majaz to similar cases.

Core Idea
The literal meaning of a word prevails until the metaphorical becomes customary to the point of abandoning the literal.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about oaths involving barley. The matn presents Abu Hanifa's position, and the commentary uncovers the underlying usul conflict: when a word has both a literal and a customary metaphorical meaning, which governs? This case demonstrates how the Sahibayn's preference for custom can override the literal, while Abu Hanifa insists on the literal unless necessity dictates otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Facts of the Case
Identify the scenario: an oath not to eat barley, then consuming it by crushing (qadm). Determine the legal question: does this break the oath?
2
Positions of the Imams
State that Abu Hanifa holds the oath is not broken; Sahibayn hold it is broken.
This is the ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
3
Underlying Usul Rule
Recognise that the dispute is based on a usul conflict: when a word has a literal meaning (haqiqah) that is still in use, and a metaphorical meaning (majaz) becomes customary, which governs?
The principle is about the priority of haqiqah vs. muta'arif majaz.
4
Reasoning of Each Side
Abu Hanifa: the original (asl) is not abandoned without necessity; predominance of metaphorical does not make literal marjuh. Sahibayn: the literal becomes like mahjur, so the metaphorical, being preponderant, replaces it.
The debate involves the concepts of 'al-marjuh yasqut' and 'al-illa la tatarajjah bi al-ziyada min jinsiha'.
5
Practical Consequence
Identify the thamarah: if the oath is broken, the swearer must expiate (kaffarah yamin); if not, no expiation. This case illustrates how a difference in usul leads to different fiqh outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا البر بأكله قضماً
( وهذا البر بأكله قضماً) ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له خلافاً لهما، بناء على أن اللفظ إن كان له معنی حقیقی مستعمل

[١] قوله : قضماً ؛ يقال قضمت الدابة تقضمه من باب تعب، ومن باب ضرب أيضا ، لغة: كسرته بأطراف الأسنان، قال في «الفتح»: «وليس المراد حقيقة القضم، بل أن يأكل عينها بأطراف الأسنان أو سطوحها (1)
:

[٢) قوله : بناء ؛ يعني هذا الخلاف مبني على خلاف آخر في مسألة متعلقة بالأصول، قال التفتازاني في «التلويح»: «الحقيقة إذا كانت مهجورة، فالعمل بالمجاز اتفاقاً، وإلا فإن لم يصر المجاز متعارفاً : أي غالباً في التعامل عند بعض المشايخ، وفي التفاهم عند البعض، فالعملُ بالحقيقة اتفاقاً، وإن صار متعارفاً فعنده العبرة بالحقيقة ؛ لان الأصل لا يترك إلا ضرورة، وعندهما العبرة بالمجاز ؛ لأنَّ المرجوح في مقابلة الراجح ساقط بمنزلة المهجور، فيترك ضرورة. وجوابه : إن غلبة استعمال المجاز لا تجعل الحقيقة مرجوحة ؛ لأنَّ العلة لا تترجّح بالزيادة من جنسها، فيكون الاستعمال في حدّ التعارض، وهذا مشعر بترجح المجاز المتعارف عندهما، سواء كان عاماً متناولاً للحقيقة أو لا، وفي كلام فخر الإسلام وغيره ما يدل على أنه إنما يترجح عندهما إذا تناول الحقيقة بعمومه، كما في مسألة الحنطة. حيث قالوا : إن هذا الاختلاف مبني على اختلافهم في جهة خلفية المجاز، فعندهما : لما كانت الخلفية في الحكم كان حكم المجاز لعمومه حكم الحقيقة أولى، وعنده لما كان في التكلّم كان جعل الكلام عاملاً في معناه الحقيقي أولى». انتهى

[٣] قوله : مستعمل ؛ احترز به عمّا إذا ترك استعماله في الحقيقي، فإنه حينئذ المصير إلى المجاز اتفاقاً كما مرّ، سواء تعذر، وهو ما لا يمكن الوصول إليه بمشقة كأكل النخلة، فإن معناه الحقيقي وهو أكل عينها متعدّراً ، وكان ممكناً، إلا أنَّ الناس هجروه وتركوه، نحو: لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان، فإنّ حقيقته ؛ أي وضع القدم حافياً متروك عندهم، فإنهم يريدون به الدخول.
And this barley [eaten] by crushing it... (This barley by eating it by crushing) — this is according to Abu Hanifa, in opposition to them (the two), based on the fact that the word, if it has a literal meaning that is used... [1] He said: qadman: they say 'qadamat al-dabba taqdamuhu' from the chapter of ta'iba and from the chapter of daraba also, linguistically: I broke it with the tips of the teeth. He said in al-Fath: 'And the intended meaning is not the literal qadm, but rather that he eats the grain itself with the tips of the teeth or their surfaces.' [2] He said: building on; meaning this disagreement is built on another disagreement in a matter related to usul. al-Taftazani said in al-Talwih: 'If the literal meaning is abandoned, then acting by the metaphorical is agreed upon. Otherwise, if the metaphorical has not become customary (ghalib) in usage according to some mashayikh, and in understanding according to others, then acting by the literal is agreed upon. And if it has become customary, then according to him (Abu Hanifa) the literal is considered, because the original is not left except out of necessity; and according to them (the two), the metaphorical is considered, because the preponderated in comparison to the preponderating is dropped like the abandoned, so it is left out of necessity. And its answer: that the predominance of usage of the metaphorical does not make the literal preponderated, because the cause is not preponderated by an increase of its own kind, so the usage is at the level of conflict. And this indicates the preponderance of the customary metaphorical according to them, whether it is general and includes the literal or not. And in the speech of Fakhr al-Islam and others, an indication that it only preponderates according to them if it includes the literal by its generality, as in the matter of wheat (al-hinta). They said: this disagreement is built on their disagreement regarding the nature of representation (khalfiyyah) of the metaphorical. According to them: since the representation is in the ruling, the rule of the metaphorical due to its generality is more entitled to be the rule of the literal. According to him: since it was in the speech, making the word operate in its literal meaning is more entitled.' End.' [3] He said: used (musta'mal); he distinguished with it from where its use in the literal meaning is abandoned, for then the recourse to the metaphorical is agreed upon as passed, whether it is impossible (as in eating the palm tree, where the literal meaning of eating its substance is impossible) or possible, but people have abandoned it, like 'he does not place his foot in so-and-so's house' — its literal meaning (placing the foot barefoot) is abandoned according to them, for they intend by it 'entering'.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an oath not to eat barley. The matn states 'This barley by eating it by crushing (qadman)'. The commentator explains that this ruling is according to Abu Hanifa, and it is disputed by the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The issue at hand is: if someone swears not to eat barley, and then eats it by crushing it with the front teeth (qadm), does he break his oath? Abu Hanifa says no, because the literal meaning of eating barley involves grinding and swallowing, not merely crushing. The disciples say yes, because the customary way of eating barley in many cases is by crushing it. The basis of this disagreement is a fundamental principle in usul al-fiqh: when a word has a literal meaning that is still used but a metaphorical meaning becomes customary, which should take precedence? According to Abu Hanifa, the literal meaning remains the default; it is not abandoned except under necessity. According to the disciples, the customary metaphorical meaning, if it becomes predominant (muta'arif), takes precedence over the literal meaning. The commentary from al-Taftazani's al-Talwih elaborates on this, explaining the conditions under which the metaphorical meaning is adopted. The discussion also includes the concept of 'representation' (khalfiyyah) of the metaphorical in relation to the literal. If the metaphorical includes the literal by its generality (i.e., the metaphorical term applies to the literal as well), the disciples prefer the metaphorical; otherwise, they might not. Abu Hanifa consistently holds to the literal meaning.

Key Terms
القضم (al-qadm): Eating by crushing or breaking with the front teeth; specifically for barley, it means eating the grain by crushing without grinding.
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal meaning of a word.
المجاز (al-majaz): The metaphorical meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if someone swears not to eat barley and then eats it by crushing it with his front teeth (qadm), does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he is still eating.
B) No, because the literal meaning of eating barley requires grinding.
C) Yes, because crushing is customary.
D) No, because the oath only applies to cooked barley.

Key Takeaway
The literal meaning (haqiqah) remains the default in oaths unless it is completely abandoned; a customary metaphorical meaning does not override it without necessity.

Oaths on Food: Literal vs Metaphorical Meaning in Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on oaths: literal vs metaphorical meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the interpretation of oaths regarding food and apply the Sahibayn's rule to determine when an oath is broken.

Core Idea
When a literal meaning is abandoned in common usage, the customary metaphorical meaning governs the oath, per the Sahibayn.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on whether an oath on a specific food is broken by non-literal consumption. The ruling hinges on linguistic principles: Abu Hanifa prioritizes the literal meaning while the Sahibayn prioritize the customary metaphorical meaning when the literal is no longer in common use. Understanding this dispute reveals how custom (urf) shapes legal interpretation in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
State the dispute
Identify the core disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding oaths on food items.
The dispute centers on which meaning (literal or customary metaphorical) controls when both exist.
2
Examine the linguistic principle
Note the difference in prioritizing haqiqah vs majaz mutaarif.
Abu Hanifa prefers haqiqah; Sahibayn prefer majaz when literal is less common.
3
Apply to the barley case
See how each imam rules on an oath about barley.
Barley's literal meaning (gnawing whole grains) is still used, so Abu Hanifa restricts to that; Sahibayn extend to all forms of consumption.
4
Analyze the flour and roast cases
Note where consensus exists and why.
For flour, literal is abandoned, so all agree metaphor governs. For roast, customary meaning is meat, not vegetables.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Identify which position is relied upon in later Hanafi fatwa.
Later scholars like Al-Marghinani prefer the Sahibayn's view when the metaphorical meaning is prevalent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا البر بأكله قضماً) ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له خلافاً لهما، بناء على أن اللفظ إن كان له معنى حقيقي مستعمل ومعنى مجازي متعارف، فأبو حنيفة له يرجح المعنى الحقيقي، وهما يرجحان المعنى المجازي، فالمراد عندهما أكل باطنه مجازاً، فيحنث بأكله سواء كان بالقضم أو غيره، فيعملان بعموم المجاز. (وهذا الدقيق بأكل خبزه، فلا يحنث لو استفه كما هو) : أي يحنث بأكل ما يتخذ منه كالخبز ونحوه؛ لأن المعنى الحقيقي مهجور، فيراد المجازي. (وأكل الشواء باللحم لا الباذنجان، والجزر اتفاقاً.
And this barley [oath] is [broken by] eating it by gnawing: This is according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], based on the principle that if a word has a literal meaning that is used and a metaphorical meaning that is customary, Abu Hanifa gives weight to the literal meaning, and the two give weight to the metaphorical meaning. So according to the two, what is intended is eating its inside metaphorically, so he breaks the oath by eating it whether by gnawing or otherwise, thus they act upon the generality of the metaphor. And this flour [oath is broken by] eating its bread, so he does not break the oath if he eats it raw as it is: meaning he breaks the oath by eating what is made from it like bread and the like, because the literal meaning is abandoned, so the metaphorical is intended. And the eating of roast [meat is broken] by meat, not by eggplant and carrots, by agreement.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage discusses the rulings of oaths regarding specific foods, focusing on the internal Hanafi difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The issue arises when someone swears an oath about a type of food, and then eats it in a form that is not the literal meaning but a common metaphorical usage. For example, if one swears 'I will not eat this barley', the literal meaning is eating the whole grain raw by gnawing. Abu Hanifa holds that he only breaks the oath by literally eating the grain that way, because the literal meaning is still used, even if a metaphorical meaning is also common. The Sahibayn argue that since the metaphorical meaning is customary and more common, the oath is broken by eating the barley in any form, including cooked or ground, because the metaphor encompasses all ways of eating the substance. This is based on a fundamental difference in linguistic principles: Abu Hanifa prioritizes the original literal meaning unless it becomes impossible to use, while the Sahibayn consider the common metaphorical usage as dominant, elevating it to the same status as the literal for the purpose of oaths. The text then gives two examples where there is consensus: flour (دقيق) and roast meat (شواء). For flour, if someone swears not to eat this flour, the literal meaning (eating raw flour powder) is abandoned by common practice, so everyone agrees the oath is broken by eating bread or anything made from the flour. For roast meat, the customary understanding is that it refers to roasted meat, not roasted vegetables like eggplant or carrots, so by agreement, eating roasted vegetables does not break the oath. This illustrates how custom (عرف) determines the intended meaning in oaths when the literal is no longer in common use.

Key Terms
قضم (qadhm): Eating by gnawing or chewing with the front teeth; in this context, the literal way of eating whole barley grains.
حقيقة (haqiqah): The original literal meaning of a word as it was first used.
مجاز متعارف (majaz muta'arif): A metaphorical meaning that has become customary and common in everyday usage.
استفه (istaffahu): To eat something dry without cooking or mixing; eating flour raw as a powder (safuf).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, in the case of an oath about 'this flour' (هذا الدقيق), what is the ruling if the person eats it raw as a powder?
A) He has broken the oath according to all Hanafi imams.
B) He has not broken the oath according to Abu Hanifa, but does according to the Sahibayn.
C) He has not broken the oath according to all Hanafi imams because the literal meaning is not intended.
D) He has broken the oath only if he eats it in the form of bread.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of oaths depends on whether the literal meaning is still in use; when abandoned, the customary metaphorical meaning takes precedence, especially in the Sahibayn's view.

Oath Regarding Flour: Customary Interpretation
Ruling on oath about flour with customary interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of customary interpretation (urf) to oaths involving food items and state the ruling when raw flour is consumed.

Core Idea
When literal meaning is abandoned in custom, oath is interpreted by common usage – raw flour does not break an oath on flour.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah illustrates a key Hanafi principle in oaths: interpretation according to custom (urf). When someone swears not to eat 'this flour', the oath covers what is normally made from flour (bread, porridge), not raw consumption. Understanding this helps avoid mistakes in oaths and expiations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central issue
What did the person swear? He swore not to eat 'this flour' (dāqīq). The question is: does eating raw flour (sufūf) break the oath?
The oath is about the substance, but its interpretation depends on how people use it.
2
Parse the key phrase: 'al-ḥaqīqah mahjūrah'
The text states that the literal meaning (ḥaqīqah) is abandoned (mahjūrah) because it is not commonly used. This means the customary/metaphorical meaning takes precedence.
Resist the urge to always apply literal meanings; custom (urf) is a recognized source in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Understand the consequence
Since the literal meaning is abandoned, eating raw flour does not violate the oath. Only eating what is customarily made from flour (bread, halwa, porridge) counts as a violation.
The ruling is 'sahih' – the oath remains intact because no failure occurred.
4
Note the agreement
The text says 'ittifāqan' (by agreement) – all Hanafi scholars concur on this ruling. There is no intra-madhhab dispute.
Consensus (ijmā) within the madhhab strengthens the authority of this ruling.
5
Extract the general principle
The underlying principle is that oaths are interpreted according to custom. This applies beyond flour – any substance whose raw form is not customarily consumed will be treated similarly.
This principle is part of the broader maxim: al-umur bi-maqasidiha (matters are judged by their purposes).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وهذا الدقيق بأكل خبزه، فلا يحنث لو استفه كما هو) أي يحنث بأكل ما يتخذ منه كالخبز ونحوه؛ لأن المعنى الحقيقي مهجور، فيرادُ المجازي. [٤] قوله : وهذا الدقيق؛ أي إذا حلف لا يأكل من هذا الدقيق، يتقيَّد الحلفُ بأكلّ كلّ ما يتخذ منه كالخبز والحلوى والعصيدة وغيرها، لا لو استفه؛ لأنّ الحقيقة مهجورة، فيعمل بالمجاز اتفاقاً.
(And this flour, [the oath is] by eating its bread, so he does not violate if he eats it raw as it is.) That is, he violates by eating what is made from it like bread etc.; because the literal meaning is abandoned, so the metaphorical is intended. [4] He said: 'And this flour'; meaning if he swears not to eat from this flour, the oath is restricted to eating everything that is made from it like bread, halwa, porridge, etc., not if he eats it raw; because the reality is abandoned, so the metaphorical is acted upon by agreement.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
Ruling statement on oath regarding flour. The text explains that if someone swears not to eat 'this flour' (دقيق), the oath customarily refers to what is normally prepared from it (e.g., bread, porridge), not the raw flour itself. Therefore, eating the flour raw (سفوف) does not break the oath. This is agreed upon by the Hanafi scholars (اتفاقاً) because the literal meaning of 'flour' as a raw grain is abandoned in common usage in favor of its common products. The key principle: oaths are interpreted according to custom (عرف).

Key Terms
سفوف (sufūf): Eating something dry or raw without preparation (e.g., eating raw flour).
الحقيقة مهجورة (al-ḥaqīqah mahjūrah): The literal meaning is abandoned because it is not commonly used.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, oaths are interpreted according to custom (urf), so eating raw flour does not break an oath on flour because the literal meaning is abandoned.

Oath Regarding Roasted Meat: Customary Restriction
Ruling on oath about roasted meat excluding roasted vegetables

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that oaths are interpreted according to custom and determine whether eating roasted vegetables violates an oath against roasted meat.

Core Idea
Custom restricts oaths: 'roasted meat' means meat, not vegetables.

In Hanafi fiqh, oaths are built on customary usage ('urf). When someone swears not to eat 'roasted meat' (شواء), the term is understood according to common practice, which limits it to roasted meat. This lesson explains why roasted vegetables like eggplant and carrots do not break such an oath, based on a clear ruling from the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Oath and Object
Determine the exact wording of the oath: 'I swear not to eat shiwa'.' What does the term 'shiwa' mean in common usage?
Shiwa' is the noun for roasted meat.
2
Apply the Principle of Custom ('Urf)
In Hanafi law, oaths are interpreted according to the custom of the people. Does custom include vegetables under 'shiwa'?
Custom restricts 'shiwa' to meat, not all roasted items.
3
Examine the Example: Eggplant and Carrot
The text specifically mentions roasted eggplant and carrot. Are these considered 'shiwa' in custom?
No; they are not roasted meat.
4
Conclude the Ruling
Since the oath is limited to meat by custom, eating roasted vegetables does not violate the oath. The oath remains valid (sahih) and no expiation is required.
This is the agreed Hanafi position.

The ruling is agreed upon by the Hanafi scholars (اتفاقاً).

Key Terms
شواء (shiwā'): Roasted meat specifically, not including vegetables.
عرف (‘urf): Customary usage; the common understanding of a term in a given context.

Oath Regarding Cooked Food: Limited to Meat
Ruling that an oath on cooked food is limited to meat cooked with water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath regarding 'cooked food' and identify which foods violate it based on custom.

Core Idea
An oath on 'cooked food' is only violated by meat cooked with water, not roasted meat or vegetable stews.

In Hanafi oaths, the meaning of ambiguous terms is determined by custom. When someone swears not to eat 'cooked food' (طبيخ), the default understanding is meat cooked with water—not roasted meat or vegetables. This lesson clarifies the scope of the oath and the key distinction between cooked and roasted meat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its subject
Note that the oath is sworn on the term 'طبيخ' (cooked food). Determine what this term refers to in common usage.
Custom is the basis for interpretation.
2
Distinguish cooking methods
Understand that 'طبيخ' implies cooking with water (stewing), while 'شواء' (roasting) is done without water. The presence of water is essential for the term.
Roasted meat is not considered 'طبيخ'.
3
Apply the ruling
Evaluate whether eating roasted chicken or vegetable curry would break the oath. According to Hanafi custom, only meat cooked with water (e.g., beef stew) would violate it.
Vegetables cooked in water are not included—only meat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(والطبيخ بما طُبخَ من اللحم) [١] قوله : والطبيخ؛ الفرق بين الشواء والطبيخ أنَّ الماء مأخوذ من مفهوم الطبيخ، فما يشوى بدون ماء فهو شواء لا طبيخ.
(And cooked food [is] with what is cooked from meat.) [1] He said: 'And cooked food'; the difference between roasted meat and cooked food is that water is taken from the concept of cooked food, so what is roasted without water is roasted meat, not cooked food.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
Ruling on oath regarding cooked food (طبيخ). The text states that if someone swears not to eat 'cooked food', the oath is restricted to what is cooked with water (i.e., meat cooked in a liquid). This distinguishes it from roasted meat (شواء), which is cooked without water. The ruling is based on custom, as 'طبيخ' in common usage refers to meat dishes prepared with water (stews, etc.).

Key Terms
طبيخ (tabīkh): Food cooked with water, particularly meat stew.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to eat 'cooked food' (طبيخ), does eating roasted chicken (without water) break the oath?

C) Yes, because it is meat.
D) No, because it is not meat.

Key Takeaway
The term 'طبيخ' in oaths is limited by custom to meat cooked with water; roasted meat and vegetables do not violate the oath.

Oath Regarding Head: Customary Restriction
Ruling on oath about head limited to heads commonly sold in markets

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that oaths are interpreted according to custom ('urf) to determine whether a specific act violates an oath.

Core Idea
An oath about 'head' is restricted by custom to what is commonly sold and eaten, not every literal head.

This passage addresses a common scenario: a person swears not to eat 'a head'. Does eating a tiny bird head break the oath? The Hanafi answer depends not on the dictionary definition but on what the community understands by 'head'. This lesson unpacks the principle that oaths are built on custom, a key maxim in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the disputed term
Read the oath: 'I swear not to eat a head.' The key term is 'head' (رأس). The question is whether this includes all animal heads or only some.
Literal meaning: any head of a living creature.
2
Apply the principle that oaths are built on custom
The commentary states that oaths are restricted to what is understood customarily (عرفاً). This means the local market practice determines the scope. In the text, 'head' refers to heads that are roasted and sold in the oven, typically of larger animals like sheep or cows.
Custom is a general principle (قاعدة كلية) in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Determine the specific ruling for the given case
Since sparrow heads are not customarily roasted and sold in markets, eating them does not break the oath. The same logic applies to any animal head not commonly treated as 'head' in that locality.
If custom varies by region, the ruling may differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(والرأس برأس يُكْبَسُ في التنانير ويباع في مصره) [٢] قوله : والرأس; يعني إذا حلف لا يأكل رأساً يتقيد ذلك بالرأس الذي يشوى ويطبخ في التنور، ويباعُ في تلك البلدة في السوق، فلا يحنث بأكل رأس العصافير. (٣) قوله : مبنية عليه; أي على العرف وهذه قاعدة كلية تتفرع عليها فروع هذا الباب، فيتقيّد الحلفُ فيها بما يفهم من الألفاظ عرفاً، أو بما يستعمل عرفاً، ولا يعتبر فيه المعنى اللغوي ولا الشرعي.
(And the head [is] with a head that is pressed in ovens and sold in his city.) [2] He said: 'And the head'; meaning if he swears not to eat a head, it is restricted to the head that is roasted and cooked in the oven, and sold in that town in the market, so he does not violate by eating the head of sparrows. (3) He said: 'Built upon it'; meaning upon custom, and this is a general principle from which branches of this chapter derive; the oath is restricted to what is understood from the words customarily, or what is used customarily, and the literal or legal meaning is not considered.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
Ruling on oath regarding 'head' (رأس). The text states that if someone swears not to eat a 'head', the oath is restricted to heads of animals that are customarily roasted and sold in the local market (e.g., sheep or cow heads), not the heads of small birds like sparrows. This is based on the general principle that oaths are built on custom (العرف), which is a key maxim in this chapter. The commentary emphasizes that oaths are interpreted according to common usage, not literal or legal definitions.

Key Terms
العرف (al-‘urf): Customary practice; the common understanding in a particular locality.
قاعدة كلية (qā‘idah kulliyyah): A comprehensive rule that governs many branches of fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because it is a head.
B) No, because customarily 'head' refers to larger animal heads (sheep/cow) sold in markets.
C) Yes, if it is roasted.
D) No, because chicken is not meat.

Key Takeaway
When interpreting oaths, the Hanafi school relies on local custom, not literal or legal definitions.

Oath Regarding Fat: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the definition of fat in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's definitions of 'fat' (شحم) in an oath and apply the mufta bihi based on intention.

Core Idea
Custom governs the scope of 'fat' in an oath; intention distinguishes kidney fat from meat fat.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about what constitutes 'fat' (شحم) when someone swears not to eat it. Abu Hanifa restricts the term to belly fat (including intestinal fat), while the Sahibayn extend it to back fat as well. Later Hanafi scholars refined the ruling based on the oath-taker's intention, showing how custom (urf) and intention (niyya) interact in legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Disputed Term
The act is swearing not to eat 'fat'. The disputed term is 'شحم' — does it include only belly fat or also back fat?
This is an oath of abstention (yamin) that triggers a kaffarah if broken.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Abu Hanifa holds that 'شحم' in an oath is restricted to belly fat (شحم البطن), which includes the fat on the rumen and between the intestines. Back fat is not included.
His view is based on customary usage in Iraq where 'شحم' commonly refers to internal fat.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that 'شحم' also includes back fat (شحم الظهر), as it is also called 'شحم' in common parlance.
They give a broader interpretation based on general linguistic usage.
4
Analyze the Refinement by al-Isbijabi
Al-Isbijabi (quoted in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) introduces a distinction: if the oath-taker intended kidney fat (internal organ fat), the Sahibayn's view is stronger; if they intended meat fat (fat attached to muscle), Abu Hanifa's view is stronger.
This refinement incorporates the concept of niyya (intention) to resolve the dispute.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi (Operative Ruling)
The mufta bihi depends on the intention of the oath-taker and the local custom. In general, if the type of fat is ambiguous, the narrower definition (Abu Hanifa) may be preferred, but the fatwa follows the more specific intention.
This shows the Hanafi method of tarjih within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(والشحم بشحم البطن))، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له، وأما عندهما يتناولُ شحم الظهر) [٤] قوله : والشحم; يعني إذا حلف لا يأكلُ الشحم يتقيد بشحم البطن، وهو ما أعلى الكرش، وما بين المصارين شحم الأمعاء هذا عنده، وعندهما: شحم الظهر أيضاً ، قال في «البحر»: «عن الاسبيجابي : إن أريـد بـه شـحـم كلية، فقولهما أظهر، وإن أريد به شحم اللحم فقوله أظهر (۳).
(And fat [is] with belly fat) — this according to Abu Hanifa, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) includes back fat. [4] He said: 'And fat'; meaning if he swears not to eat fat, it is restricted to belly fat, which is the top of the rumen, and what is between the intestines (intestinal fat) — this is according to him, and according to them includes back fat as well. He said in al-Bahr: 'From al-Isbijabi: if by fat is meant kidney fat, their view is clearer; if by fat is meant meat fat, his view is clearer.'
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the interpretation of 'fat' (شحم) in an oath. Abu Hanifa restricts the term to belly fat (including intestinal fat), while the Sahibayn extend it to back fat as well. The commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq distinguishes: if the person intends kidney fat (internal), the Sahibayn's position is stronger; if they intend meat fat (on the muscle), Abu Hanifa's view is stronger. This reflects a difference in customary understanding.

Key Terms
شحم البطن (šaḥm al-baṭn): Belly fat, including fat on the rumen and intestines.
شحم الظهر (šaḥm al-ẓahr): Back fat, the fat on the back of the animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Isbijabi's refinement, when is the Sahibayn's view (that back fat is included in an oath on 'fat') followed?
A) When the oath-torer intends meat fat.
B) When the oath-taker intends kidney fat.
C) When the oath-taker is from a region where back fat is called 'شحم'.
D) Always, because the Sahibayn are the majority.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school resolves disputes over ambiguous terms in oaths by considering intention and custom, not by rigidly following one position.

Oath Regarding Bread: Customary Grains
Ruling that an oath on bread is limited to wheat and barley bread

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the interpretation of an oath regarding 'bread' and explain the role of custom in limiting it to wheat and barley bread.

Core Idea
An oath on 'bread' is limited to wheat and barley bread because custom defines the term.

When someone swears not to eat 'bread', which types of bread are covered? This lesson examines the Hanafi position that the oath is interpreted according to local custom, which typically restricts 'bread' to bread made from wheat (burr) and barley (sha‘īr). Other types, such as rice bread, are not included unless they are the customary bread in that region.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is swearing not to eat 'bread'. The Hanafi ruling is that only bread made from wheat or barley breaks the oath, based on common custom.
2
Understand the custom-based interpretation
Custom (urf) is the basis for limiting the scope. In most regions, 'bread' refers to wheat or barley bread.
3
Apply to other grains
If local custom defines other grains (e.g., rice, corn) as 'bread', then that bread is also included. Otherwise, it is not.
The ruling is not rigid; it adapts to local custom.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may include all types of bread under the term, while Hanafis prioritize custom.
This is a practical difference in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(والخبز بخبز البر والشعير) [0] قوله : والخبز; أي إذا حلف لا يأكل خبزاً حمل ذلك على ما يتعارف أكله في البلد من خبز البر والشعير دون غيرهما.
(And bread [is] with bread of wheat and barley.) [0] He said: 'And bread'; meaning if he swears not to eat bread, it is interpreted according to what is customarily eaten in the country from wheat and barley bread, not others.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
Ruling on oath regarding bread (خبز). The text states that if someone swears not to eat 'bread', the oath is restricted to bread made from wheat (بر) and barley (شعير), as these are the customary grains used for bread in most regions. Other types of bread (e.g., from rice, maize) are not included unless customarily considered bread in that locality. This is based on the general principle that oaths are interpreted by custom.

Key Terms
بر (burr): Wheat.
شعير (sha‘īr): Barley.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) No, because customarily bread is made from wheat or barley.
C) Yes, if it is called bread.
D) No, because rice is not a grain.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, an oath on 'bread' is interpreted according to custom, which typically restricts it to wheat and barley bread.
