Oath Terms Interpreted by Custom: Dispute on Fruit and Fat
Sharh on matn explaining oath terms according to custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that oaths are interpreted by custom and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the terms 'fruit' and 'fat' along with its practical consequences.

Core Idea
Oaths follow custom, not dictionaries; but imams differ on what custom includes for fruit and fat.

When someone swears an oath using a general term like 'fruit' or 'fat', how do we know what they actually meant? The Hanafi school gives a clear answer: we look to the customary usage of that term in the oath-taker's community. This lesson explores that principle through the commentary of Al-Hidayah, focusing on two specific disputes between Abu Hanifa and his two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. By understanding these examples, you will see how the school applies the concept of 'urf in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal principle
The passage states that oaths are built upon custom. This means that the legal meaning is determined by customary understanding, not literal or technical definitions.
2
Examine the dispute over 'fruit'
Abu Hanifa excludes certain items (grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates) from the customary meaning of fruit. The Sahibayn include them. What reasoning does each side use?
3
Examine the dispute over 'fat'
Abu Hanifa restricts 'fat' to belly fat; the Sahibayn include back fat. How does this relate to the purpose of the oath?
4
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil)
Both disputes hinge on whether the item is primarily used for enjoyment (tafakkuh) or for sustenance/medicine. Identify the underlying logic.
5
Determine the practical consequence
For each term, what is the thamarah al-khilaf? How does this affect whether the oath is broken?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطبيخ بما طبخ من اللحم، والرأس برأس يُكْبَسُ في التنانير ويباع في مصره، عملاً بالعرف، فإنَّ الأيمان مبنية عليه (والشحم بشحم البطن)، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له، وأما عندهما يتناولُ شحم الظهر، (والخبز بخبز البر والشعير).
قوله: والطبيخ؛ الفرق بين الشواء والطبيخ أنَّ الماء مأخوذ من مفهوم الطبيخ، فما يشوى بدون ماء فهو شواء لا طبيخ.
قوله: والرأس؛ يعني إذا حلف لا يأكل رأساً يتقيد ذلك بالرأس الذي يشوى ويطبخ في التنور، ويباعُ في تلك البلدة في السوق، فلا يحنث بأكل رأس العصافير.
قوله: مبنية عليه؛ أي على العرف وهذه قاعدة كلية تتفرع عليها فروع هذا الباب، فيتقيّد الحلفُ فيها بما يفهم من الألفاظ عرفاً، أو بما يستعمل عرفاً، ولا يعتبر فيه المعنى اللغوي ولا الشرعي.
قوله: والشحم؛ يعني إذا حلف لا يأكلُ الشحم يتقيد بشحم البطن، وهو ما أعلى الكرش، وما بين المصارين شحم الأمعاء هذا عنده، وعندهما: شحم الظهر أيضاً، قال في «البحر»: «عن الاسبيجابي: إن أريد به شحم كلية، فقولهما أظهر، وإن أريد به شحم اللحم فقوله أظهر.
قوله: والخبز؛ أي إذا حلف لا يأكل خبزاً حمل ذلك على ما يتعارف أكله في ذلك البلد كخبز الحنطة والشعير، لا خبز الذرة والدخن والأرز.
لا خبز الأرز ببلدة لا يعتاد فيه، والفاكهة بالتفاح والمشمش، والبطيخ، لا العنب، والرمان، والرطب، والقثاء، والخيار: هذا عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما العنب، والرمان والرطب فاكهة.
قوله: ببلدة لا يعتاد فيه؛ متعلق بقوله: «لا خبز الأرز».
قوله: والفاكهة؛ قال في «الهداية»: «الأصل أن الفاكهة اسم لما يتفكه به قبل الطعام أو بعده؛ أي يتنعم به زيادة على المعتاد والرطب واليابس فيه سواء، بعد أن يكون التفكه به معتاداً، حتى لا يحنث بيابس البطيخ، وهذا المعنى موجود في التفاح وأخواته، فيحنث بها. وغير موجود في القثاء والخيار؛ لأنهما من البقول بيعاً وأكلاً، وأما العنب والرمان والرطب فهما يقولان: إن معنى التفكه موجود فيها، فإنها أعزّ الفواكه، والتنعم بها يفوق التنعم بغيرها، وأبو حنيفة يقول: هذه الأشياء مما يتغذى ويتداوى بها، فأوجب قصوراً في معنى التفكه للاستعمال في حاجة البقاء».
قوله: والمشمش؛ - بكسر الميمين، بينهما شين معجمة ساكنة، وبعدهما أيضاً شين معجمة بالفارسية: رر وآلو.
قوله: والبطيخ؛ - بكسر الباء الموحدة، وتشديد الطاء المهملة المكسورة، بعدها ياء مثناة تحتيّة ساكنة، ثم خاء معجمة – بالفارسية: خريزة.
قوله: والرمان؛ - بضم الراء المهملة، وتشديد الميم – بالفارسية: انار. والرُّطَب - بضم الراء المهملة، وفتح التاء المهملة - : خرمان تر، والتمر اليابس منه. والقثاء: - بكسر القاف وتشديد الثاء المثلثة – بالفارسية قفد وبالهندية: كهيرا، والخيار بالهندية: ككرى.
قوله: وعندهما؛ ذكر في «الفتح» وغيره: إنّ هذا الخلاف خلافُ زمان لا خلاف حجة وبرهان.
And 'al-tabikh' (cooked food) is what is cooked with meat, and 'al-ra's' (head) is the head that is pressed in ovens and sold in his town, acting upon custom, for oaths are built upon it. And 'al-shahm' (fat) is the fat of the belly according to Abu Hanifa, but according to the two companions, it includes the fat of the back. And 'al-khubz' (bread) is bread of wheat and barley.
His saying: 'al-tabikh': The difference between grilled and cooked is that water is taken from the concept of cooking; what is grilled without water is grilled, not cooked.
His saying: 'al-ra's': Meaning if he swears not to eat a head, it is restricted to the head that is grilled and cooked in the oven and sold in that town in the market, so he does not break his oath by eating the head of sparrows.
His saying: 'built upon it': i.e., upon custom, and this is a universal principle from which branches of this chapter derive; the oath is restricted in them to what is understood from the words customarily, or what is used customarily, and neither the linguistic nor the legal meaning is considered.
His saying: 'and the fat': Meaning if he swears not to eat fat, it is restricted to the fat of the belly, which is what is on the upper part of the rumen, and what is between the intestines, the fat of the intestines, this according to him. And according to both, also the fat of the back. He said in 'al-Bahr': 'From al-Isbijabi: If kidney fat is intended, their view is more apparent; if meat fat is intended, his view is more apparent.'
His saying: 'and the bread': i.e., if he swears not to eat bread, it is interpreted according to what is customarily eaten in that town, like bread of wheat and barley, not bread of corn, millet, or rice.
And not bread of rice in a town where it is not customary, and fruit is apples, apricots, and watermelon, not grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates, cucumbers, and gherkins: this according to Abu Hanifa, but according to the two companions, grapes, pomegranates, and fresh dates are fruit.
His saying: 'in a town where it is not customary' is connected to 'not bread of rice'.
His saying: 'and fruit': He said in 'al-Hidayah': 'The principle is that fruit is a name for what is enjoyed before or after food; i.e., one delights in it beyond the usual, fresh and dried being equal in it, provided that the enjoyment is customary, so that one does not break an oath with dried watermelon. This meaning is present in apples and their like, so one breaks an oath by them. It is not present in cucumbers and gherkins because they are vegetables in sale and eating. As for grapes, pomegranates, and fresh dates, the two say: The meaning of enjoyment is present in them, for they are the choicest fruits, and delight in them surpasses delight in others. Abu Hanifa says: These things are used for nourishment and medicine, so he required a deficiency in the meaning of enjoyment due to their use for the need of survival.'
His saying: 'al-mishmish' (apricot): - with kasra of the two meems, between them a silent shin, and after them also a shin, in Persian: rar and alu.
His saying: 'al-battikh' (watermelon): - with kasra of the ba, tashdid of the ta with kasra, after it a silent ya, then a kha – in Persian: kharizah.
His saying: 'al-rumman' (pomegranate): - with damm of the ra, tashdid of the mim – in Persian: anar. And 'al-rutab' (fresh dates): - with damm of the ra, fatha of the ta: fresh dates, and the dry ones are dried dates. And 'al-qiththa' (cucumber): - with kasra of the qaf and tashdid of the tha – in Persian: qufad and in Hindi: khira, and 'al-khiyar' (gherkin): in Hindi: kakri.
His saying: 'and according to the two companions': It is mentioned in 'al-Fath' and others: This difference is a difference of time, not a difference of proof and evidence.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn from Kitab al-Ayman of Al-Hidayah. It explains how oath phrases are interpreted according to customary usage rather than literal or legal definitions. The matn states that when one swears not to eat 'ra's' (head), it refers to the head that is customarily cooked and sold in the market, not the head of a sparrow. Similarly, 'shahm' (fat) is restricted to belly fat according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn include back fat. For 'khubz' (bread), it is the bread customarily eaten in that town, like wheat or barley bread, not corn or rice. Regarding 'fakiha' (fruit), Abu Hanifa excludes grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates, cucumbers, and gherkins because they are used for sustenance or medicine, while the Sahibayn consider grapes, pomegranates, and fresh dates as fruit. The commentary emphasizes that the underlying principle is custom ('urf), and oaths are built upon it. The difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on fruit is described as a difference of time and customary usage, not of evidence.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Customary usage that determines the interpretation of oath terms; oaths are built upon it.
الفاكهة (al-fakiha): Fruit; defined as something enjoyed (mutafakkah bihi) before or after food as a luxury, not a staple. Subject to customary classification.
الشحم (al-shahm): Fat; in oath context, it is restricted to belly fat according to Abu Hanifa, but includes back fat according to the Sahibayn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone swears not to eat 'fruit' (fakiha), which of the following would Abu Hanifa consider as breaking the oath?
A) Eating apples
B) Eating grapes
C) Eating pomegranates
D) Eating fresh dates

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) is the foundation for interpreting oath terms, and within the Hanafi school there are differing views on what constitutes fruit and fat, reflecting the principle that oaths follow customary usage.

Fruit in Oaths: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on defining fruit for oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether grapes, pomegranates, and dates break an oath not to eat fruit, and explain the reasoning behind each position using the principle of customary enjoyment.

Core Idea
In oaths, fruit is defined by customary enjoyment, not botanical classification.

When someone swears not to eat fruit, what counts as fruit? This seemingly simple question reveals a rich intra-Hanafi debate between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The disagreement hinges not on botanical definitions but on custom ('urf) and the underlying purpose of an oath. By examining this dispute, you will see how Hanafi jurists balance textual principles with the reality of changing times and local practices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed items
The dispute specifically concerns whether grapes, pomegranates, and ripe dates (al-rummān, al-ʻinab, al-rutab) fall under the term 'fruit' (fākiha) for oath purposes. Both sides agree on apples, apricots, and melons being fruit, and on cucumbers and gherkins not being fruit.
The base text lists items that ARE fruit (apple, apricot, melon) and those that are NOT (grapes, pomegranate, ripe dates, cucumber, gherkin). The disagreement is only on the middle three.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that grapes, pomegranates, and ripe dates are not considered fruit for oaths. His reasoning: these items are used primarily for nutrition (taghadhī) and medicine (tadāwī), which diminishes their character as mere enjoyment (tafakkuh). Enjoyment is the core meaning of fruit in oaths, and when an item serves a survival need, its enjoyment aspect is deficient.
Abu Hanifa does not deny they are enjoyed, but says that their practical use overrides their enjoyment status.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that grapes, pomegranates, and ripe dates are indeed fruit because they are among the noblest of fruits (aʻazz al-fawākih) and the delight (tanāʻum) they provide surpasses that of other fruits. Customarily people enjoy them before or after meals, so they meet the definition.
The Sahibayn focus on the customary practice of enjoyment, not on ancillary uses.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf): If a person swears not to eat fruit and then eats grapes, according to Abu Hanifa the oath is not broken (the person is not ḥānith), but according to the Sahibayn the oath is broken. This affects whether the person must perform kaffārah (expiation) for breaking an oath.
The dispute matters immensely in daily life and fatwa practice.
5
Consider the temporal dimension
The commentary in al-Fatḥ and elsewhere notes that this disagreement may be a 'difference of time' (khilāf zamān). In Abu Hanifa's era, these items may not have been customarily classified as fruits for oaths, but in later times they became so. Therefore, the Sahibayn's view may be more applicable in later periods, and the muftā bihī may shift with custom.
This illustrates how Ḥanafī fiqh remains dynamic, adjusting to changing 'urf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا خبز الأرز ببلدة لا يعتاد فيه، والفاكهة بالتفاح والمشمش، والبطيخ، لا العنب، والرمان، والرطب، والقثاء، والخيار) : هذا عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما العنب، والرمان والرطب فاكهة. ذلك البلد كخبز الحنطة والشعير، لا خبز الذرة والدخن والأرز. [قوله : والفاكهة ؛ قال في «الهداية»: «الأصل أن الفاكهة اسم لما يتفكه به قبل الطعام أو بعده ؛ أي يتنعم به زيادة على المعتاد والرطب واليابس فيه سواء، بعد أن يكون التفكه به معتاداً، حتى لا يحنث بيابس البطيخ، وهذا المعنى موجود في التفاح وأخواته، فيحنث بها. وغير موجود في القنّاء والخيار ؛ لأنهما من البقول بيعاً وأكلاً، وأما العنب والرمان والرطب فهما يقولان : إن معنى التفكه موجود فيها، فإنها أعزّ الفواكه ، والتنعم بها يفوق التنعم بغيرها، وأبو حنيفة لله يقول : هذه الأشياء مما يتغذى ويتداوى بها، فأوجب قُصُوراً في معنى التفكه للاستعمال في حاجة البقاء» [وعندهما ؛ ذكر في «الفتح» وغيره : إنّ هذا الخلاف خلافُ زمان لا ]
And the fruit (is interpreted) by apple, apricot, and melon, not by grapes, pomegranate, ripe dates, cucumber, and gherkin: this is according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (the two Sahibayn), grapes, pomegranate, and ripe dates are fruit. (The same for) bread of that town like wheat and barley bread, not corn, millet, and rice bread. He said: 'and fruit'; he said in al-Hidayah: 'The principle is that fruit is a name for what is enjoyed before or after the meal; i.e., one delights in it beyond the usual, and fresh and dry are equal in that, provided that the enjoyment is customary, so that he does not break the oath by dry melon. This meaning exists in apple and its likes, so he breaks the oath thereby. And it does not exist in cucumber and gherkin because they are considered vegetables in sale and consumption. As for grapes, pomegranate, and ripe dates, they (the two Sahibayn) say that the meaning of enjoyment exists in them, for they are the noblest of fruits, and the delight in them surpasses that in others. Abu Hanifa says: these things are used for nutrition and medicine, so there is a deficiency in the meaning of enjoyment due to their use for the necessity of survival.' And according to them (the Sahibayn): it was mentioned in al-Fath and elsewhere that this disagreement is a difference of time.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn), concerning the definition of 'fruit' (fakiha) for the purpose of oaths. The base ruling states that if a person swears not to eat fruit and then eats grapes, pomegranates, or ripe dates, according to Abu Hanifa the oath is not broken because these are not considered fruit in the customary sense; rather they are used for nutrition and medicine. The Sahibayn disagree: they argue that these are indeed fruits because they are enjoyed and are considered premium fruits. The commentary from Al-Hidayah explains the underlying principle: fruit is what is enjoyed before or after a meal, and this meaning is present in apples and apricots but absent in cucumbers and gherkins, which are treated as vegetables. The Sahibayn extend this to grapes, pomegranates, and dates because they are esteemed and enjoyed. Abu Hanifa's response is that these items are also consumed for sustenance (nutrition and medicine), thus reducing their classification as mere enjoyment. The passage also notes that this difference may be a difference of time: in Abu Hanifa's era these were not considered fruits, but later they became so. The practical consequence is that a person swearing not to eat fruit would break the oath by eating these items according to the Sahibayn but not according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
فاكهة (fākiha): In this passage, 'fruit' for oath purposes is defined as what is enjoyed before or after a meal, excluding items primarily used for nutrition or medicine.
كرع (kar'): Not used in this passage; included from the source chunk's key terms list but not relevant to the fruit dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are not mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah as fruit.
B) They are used for nutrition and medicine, which diminishes their enjoyment character.
C) They are not consumed before or after meals.
D) The Sahibayn disagree, so Abu Hanifa's view is automatically rejected.

Key Takeaway
Whether an item breaks an oath not to eat fruit depends on whether it is customarily considered fruit based on enjoyment, not on botanical classification, and this can change over time.

Interpretation of 'Min' in Oaths on Drinking
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the preposition 'min' in oaths regarding drinking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on the interpretation of 'min' in oaths regarding drinking and apply each to determine whether an oath is broken.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: 'min' = starting point; Sahibayn: 'min' = partiality — the mode of drinking determines oath-breaking.

Oaths are sensitive matters in Islamic law, and their wording can lead to significant differences in ruling. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on the preposition 'min' (from) in an oath like 'I will not drink from this river.' Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, differ on whether drinking using a vessel breaks the oath. Their disagreement stems from the linguistic function of 'min': does it mark the starting point of an action or indicate partiality? Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze oath wording and apply the Hanafi principles of linguistic interpretation and custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is the oath 'I will not drink from this river.' The dispute concerns the meaning of 'min' in the phrase 'from this river' and whether drinking by a vessel breaks the oath.
The passage specifically addresses drinking with a vessel, not direct mouth drinking (kar‘).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa interprets 'min' as ibtida' al-ghaya (starting point of the goal). Thus, the oath only prohibits drinking that begins at the river, i.e., drinking by directly putting the mouth to the water (kar‘). Drinking from a vessel does not start from the river, so the oath is not broken.
This is a restrictive interpretation.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad interpret 'min' as tab'id (partiality). The oath prohibits drinking any part of the river's water, regardless of the method—direct mouth, vessel, etc. Therefore, drinking from a vessel breaks the oath.
This is an expansive interpretation.
4
Recognize the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the person drinks using a cup, Abu Hanifa says the oath is not broken (no expiation necessary); the Sahibayn say it is broken (expiation required). This difference affects the person's legal and religious responsibility.
The same applies to drinking by hands or any non-kar' method.
5
Note the custom exception for wells
The passage notes that for wells and pits, the literal meaning is abandoned due to custom (urf). Drinking any water from a well breaks the oath, even by kar' if done in an unnatural way. This shows that custom can override the literal interpretation.
This exception suggests that for rivers, custom may also favor the general meaning (Sahibayn's view).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشرب من نهر بالكرع منه، فلا يحنث لو شرب منه بإناء) : هذا عند أبي حنيفة ، فإن : من ؛ عنده لابتداء الغاية، وعندهما للتّبعض خلاف برهان، فإنّ الإمام لم يكن العنب والرطب والرمان فاكهة في زمانه، وعدت منها في زمانهما. [قوله : والشرب... الخ ؛ يعني تقييد الحلف بعدم الشرب بقوله : لا يشرب من هذا النهر، أو من هذا البحر، أو قال ذلك بدون هذا بالكرع منه، وهو – بالفتح – في الأصل الشرب بإدخال الأكارع بالماء، وهي من الإنسان ما دون الركبة، ومن الدواب ما دون الكعب. ويقال : كَرّعَ الماء كرعاً من باب نفع، شرب بفيه من موضعه، فإن شرب منه بكفيه أو بشيء آخر فليس بكرع ، فيقال : كرعَ في الإناء أمالَ عنقه إليه فشرب منه بفيه. كذا في «المصباح». وذكر في «البحر» عن ((الظهيرية»: الكرعُ لا يكون إلا بعد الخوض في الماء. وذكر في «الكشف» و«التلويح ) و «النهر » ) وغيرها : إن الخوض في الماء ليس بشرط، فإنَّ الكرع يكون من الإناء أيضا، وهذا كله فيما يتأتى فيه الكرع، فإن حلف لا يشرب من هذه البئر ، أو من هذا الجب يحنث بالشرب من مائه مطلقاً ، سواء قال : من البئر أو من ماء البئر ؛ لكون الحقيقة مهجورة، حتى لو تكلّف الكرع بأن ينزل بأسفل البئر، فيشرب منه بفيه لا يحنث ؛ لعدم العرف. كذا في «الفتح». [قوله : وعندهما ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنَّ مَن في قوله : «لا يشرب من هذا النهر» للتبعيض عندهما ، فيكون المعنى : لا يشرب البعض ماء النهر، وهو أعم من أن يكون بالكرع أو بغيره. وأما عند أبي حنيفة فـ «من» لابتداء الغاية، أي لا يشرب منه مبدءاً وشروعاً بالكرع، فلو شرب منه بإناء لم يصدق عليه أنه شرب منه بالكرع، فلا يحنث.
And drinking from a river by cupping the mouth (kar‘) from it, so he does not break the oath if he drinks from it with a vessel: this is according to Abu Hanifa. So indeed, 'min' with him is for the starting point of the goal, and with them (the two Sahibayn) for partiality, contrary to the demonstration. Because the Imam (Abu Hanifa) considered that grapes, ripe dates, and pomegranates were not fruit in his time, but they became counted among them in their time. [His saying: 'and drinking...etc.' means that the restriction of the oath by not drinking is by his saying: 'I will not drink from this river,' or 'from this sea,' or he said that without this, by cupping from it. And it (kar‘) – with fatha – originally is drinking by inserting the legs (akari‘) into the water, which for humans is below the knee, and for animals below the ankle. And it is said: karra‘a al-ma‘a kar‘an (bab nafa‘a): he drank with his mouth from its place. If he drank with his hands or another thing, it is not kar‘. It is said: kar‘a fi al-ina‘: he inclined his neck to it and drank from it with his mouth. This is in al-Misbah. And it is mentioned in al-Bahr from al-Zahiriyyah: kar‘ does not occur except after wading into the water. And it is mentioned in al-Kashshaf and al-Talwih and al-Nahr and others: that wading into the water is not a condition, for kar‘ can also be from a vessel. All this is in what kar‘ is feasible. If he swears not to drink from this well or this pit, he breaks the oath by drinking from its water absolutely, whether he said 'from the well' or 'from the water of the well,' because the literal meaning is abandoned. Even if he makes an effort to do kar‘ by descending to the bottom of the well and drinking with his mouth, he does not break the oath due to lack of custom. This is in al-Fath. [His saying: 'and according to them ... etc.']: its summary is that 'min' in his saying 'I will not drink from this river' is for partiality according to them, so the meaning is: he should not drink some of the water of the river, which is more general, whether by kar‘ or otherwise. As for according to Abu Hanifa, 'min' is for starting point of the goal, i.e., he should not drink from it beginning and starting by kar‘. So if he drinks from it with a vessel, it is not true that he drank from it by kar‘, so he does not break the oath.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his disciples regarding the interpretation of the preposition 'min' in an oath like 'I will not drink from this river.' Abu Hanifa holds that 'min' indicates the starting point of the action (ibtida' al-ghaya): the oath is specifically about drinking by direct mouth cupping (kar‘). Therefore, if the person drinks using a vessel, the oath is not broken because he did not drink 'from' the river in the sense of starting at the river; he started from the vessel. The Sahibayn interpret 'min' as partiality (tab'id), meaning that the oath prohibits drinking any part of the river's water, regardless of how it is consumed—by direct mouth, vessel, or any other method. Thus, drinking from a vessel would break the oath according to them. The commentary elaborates on the definition of kar‘: it is drinking by putting the mouth directly to the water source, often by wading in. There is a subsidiary dispute about whether wading is a condition, but the text notes that in later authorities, wading is not required and kar‘ can even be from a vessel. The practical application is given: for wells and pits, the custom (urf) overrides the literal meaning, so drinking any water breaks the oath, but if one deliberately does kar‘ by descending, it does not break the oath because that is not customary. The key point is the different scope of the oath due to the linguistic meaning of 'min'.

Key Terms
كرع (kar'): Drinking by putting the mouth directly to the water source, either by wading in or by inclining into a vessel; contrasted with using a vessel or hands.
من (min): The preposition 'min' is interpreted differently: by Abu Hanifa as 'starting point' (ibtida' al-ghaya), by Sahibayn as 'partiality' (tab'id).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, because he drank the river's water.
B) No, because he did not drink by kar‘.
C) No, because drinking from a cup is not considered drinking from the river.
D) Yes, because the cup does not change the source.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of 'min' (from) in oaths splits the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa restricts oath-breaking to direct mouth drinking (kar‘), while the Sahibayn extend it to any consumption of water from the source.

Interpretation of 'Min' in Oaths of Drinking: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Explains intra-Hanafi dispute on the meaning of 'min' in oaths of drinking from a river, with application of dalil al-hal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the interpretation of 'min' in oaths of drinking from a river and apply the principle of customary usage (al-mutawarif) versus literal meaning in Hanafi oath jurisprudence.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'min' can mean 'beginning point' (Abu Hanifa) or 'partiality' (Sahibayn), affecting when an oath is broken.

When a person swears 'I will not drink from this river,' does using a cup break the oath? This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the meaning of 'min'. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that it indicates the starting point, so only drinking directly from the river (kari') violates the oath. His students, the Sahibayn, argue that 'min' implies partiality, so any drinking of the river's water—by cup or by mouth—breaks the oath. The commentator also illustrates how context (dalil al-hal) can restrict an oath, as in the case of a ruler's oath limited to his tenure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The core dispute is the interpretation of the preposition 'min' in the oath 'la ashrabu min hadha al-nahr' (I will not drink from this river).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that 'min' indicates ibtida' al-ghayah (beginning point). Thus, the oath only forbids drinking that starts at the river itself, which occurs only by kari' (direct mouth drinking).
Kari' is defined as drinking by inserting the mouth into the water.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that 'min' indicates tab'id (partiality). The oath forbids drinking any part of the river's water, whether by kari' or by vessel.
They rely on customary usage (al-mutawarif) as the understood meaning.
4
Analyze each side's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that the literal meaning of 'min' (ibtida') is still actively used for kari', so it prevents resorting to the metaphor. The Sahibayn argue that common speech uses 'min' for partiality, making the metaphor preferred over the literal meaning when the latter is less common.
The passage notes this as the key point of talil (legal reasoning).
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, drinking with a cup does not break the oath; under the Sahibayn, it does. This affects fatwa in cases of oaths involving rivers.
For wells and cisterns, the passage says the literal meaning is abandoned, so both agree that any drinking breaks the oath.
6
Examine dalil al-hal for the ruler's oath
The passage applies the same principle of contextual inference to restrict a ruler's oath to his tenure. The purpose (zajr al-dā‘ir) can only be achieved while he holds power, so the oath is limited to that period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشرب من نهر بالكرع منه، فلا يحنث لو شرب منه بإناء. هذا عند أبي حنيفة، فإن من عنده لابتداء الغاية، وعندهما للتبعيض خلاف برهان، فإن الإمام لم يكن العنب والرطب والرمان فاكهة في زمانه، وعدت منها في زمانهما. الكرع بالفتح في الأصل الشرب بإدخال الأكارع في الماء، وهي من الإنسان ما دون الركبة، ومن الدواب ما دون الكعب. ويقال كرع الماء كرعاً من باب نفع، شرب بفيه من موضعه، فإن شرب منه بكفيه أو بشيء آخر فليس بكرع، فيقال كرع في الإناء أمال عنقه إليه فشرب منه بفيه. ذكر في البحر عن الظهيرية: الكرع لا يكون إلا بعد الخوض في الماء. وذكر في الكشف والتلويح والنهر وغيرها: إن الخوض في الماء ليس بشرط، فإن الكرع يكون من الإناء أيضا، وهذا كله فيما يتأتى فيه الكرع، فإن حلف لا يشرب من هذه البئر أو من هذا الجب يحنث بالشرب من مائه مطلقاً، سواء قال من البئر أو من ماء البئر؛ لكون الحقيقة مهجورة، حتى لو تكلف الكرع بأن ينزل بأسفل البئر فيشرب منه بفيه لا يحنث؛ لعدم العرف. كذا في الفتح. وعندهما حاصله أن من في قوله لا يشرب من هذا النهر للتبعيض، فيكون المعنى لا يشرب البعض ماء النهر، وهو أعم من أن يكون بالكرع أو بغيره، وعنده لابتداء الغاية، فالمعنى كون الشرب مبتدأ من ماء النهر، وهذا لا يكون إلا بالكرع. وذكر في الهداية وحواشيها في توجيه الخلاف: إن الشرب بالإناء هو المتعارف المفهوم، فإن المفهوم من قولنا فلان يشرب من دجلة الشرب بالإناء منها، وقد مر أن المجاز المتعارف عندهما أولى من الحقيقة، فأوله أن كلمة من للتبعيض، وحقيقته في الكرع، وهي مستعملة، فمنعت المصير إلى المجاز وإن كان متعارفاً. بخلاف الحلف من مائه؛ أي إذا حلف لا يشرب من ماء هذا النهر لا يتقيد بالكرع، بل يحنث بالشرب من الإناء اتفاقاً؛ لأن الماء الذي في الإناء منسوب إلى النهر. وتحليف الوالي رجلاً؛ ليعلمه بكل داعر أتى البلدة بحال ولايته: أي يقيد تحليف الوالي رجلاً ليعلمه بكل مفسد أتى البلد بحال ولايته. وهذا التخصيص يثبت بدلالة الحال، وهو العلم بأن المقصود من هذا الاستحلاف زجره بما يدفع شرّه أو شرّ غيره بزجره؛ لأنه إذا انزجر داعر انزجر داعر آخر، وهذا لا يتحقق إلا في حال ولايته؛ لأنها حال قدرته على ذلك، فلا يفيد فائدته بعد زوال ولايته، وهو إما بالموت أو بالعزل. كذا في الفتح.
And drinking from a river by drinking directly with the mouth (kari'), so he does not break his oath if he drinks from it with a vessel. This is according to Abu Hanifa, for according to him 'min' (from) indicates the beginning point, while according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it indicates partiality, unlike Burhan, for the Imam (Abu Hanifa) did not consider grapes, fresh dates, and pomegranates as fruit in his time, and they were counted among them in their time. Kari' (with fatha) originally is drinking by inserting the legs into water, and from humans it is below the knee, and from animals below the ankle. It is said: kara'a al-ma'a kari'an from the paradigm of nafa'a, he drank with his mouth from its place. If he drank from it with his palms or something else, it is not kari'. It is said: kara'a fi al-ina', he tilted his neck to it and drank from it with his mouth. It is mentioned in al-Bahr from al-Zahiriyya: kari' does not occur except after wading into water. And it is mentioned in al-Kashf, al-Talwih, al-Nahr, and others: wading into water is not a condition, for kari' can be from a vessel as well. All this concerns cases where kari' is possible. If he swears not to drink from this well or this cistern, he breaks his oath by drinking its water absolutely, whether he said 'from the well' or 'from the water of the well', because the literal meaning is abandoned. Even if he goes to the trouble of kari' by descending to the bottom of the well and drinks from it with his mouth, he does not break his oath due to lack of custom. Thus in al-Fath. And according to them (the two disciples), the gist is that 'min' in his statement 'I will not drink from this river' is for partiality, so the meaning is: he will not drink some of the river's water, which is more general, whether by kari' or otherwise. And according to him (Abu Hanifa) it indicates the starting point, so the meaning is that drinking begins from the river's water, and this only occurs by kari'. It is mentioned in al-Hidaya and its glosses in explaining the difference: drinking with a vessel is the customary understood meaning, for the understood meaning from our statement 'so-and-so drinks from the Tigris' is drinking from it with a vessel, and it has been mentioned that customary metaphor according to them is preferable to literal meaning. So he (Abu Hanifa) interpreted the word 'min' as for partiality, and its literal meaning is in kari', and it is used, so it prevents resorting to metaphor even if it is customary. Contrary to the oath from its water; i.e., if he swears not to drink from the water of this river, it is not restricted to kari', rather he breaks his oath by drinking from a vessel by agreement, because the water that is in the vessel is attributed to the river. And the oath of the ruler (wali) of a man to inform him of every corrupt person who came to the city during his tenure: i.e., the oath of the ruler over a man is restricted to his tenure, to inform him of every mischief-maker who came to the city during his tenure. This specification is established by inference from the situation (dalil al-hal), which is the knowledge that the purpose of this swearing is to restrain him through what repels his evil or the evil of others through his restraint; because when a corrupt person is restrained, another corrupt person is restrained, and this only occurs during his tenure, because it is a time of his power over that, so it does not serve its purpose after his tenure ends, whether by death or dismissal. Thus in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya deals with two distinct but related issues in the chapter of oaths (al-ayman). The first and primary issue is the interpretation of the preposition 'min' (من) when used in an oath prohibiting drinking from a river. The author of the matn states that if someone swears 'I will not drink from this river,' the oath is only violated by drinking directly with the mouth (kari'), not by using a vessel. The commentator explains that this is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa. For Abu Hanifa, 'min' indicates ibtida' al-ghayah (the beginning point), so the act of drinking must begin at the river itself, which only occurs through kari'. In contrast, his two students Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that 'min' indicates tab'id (partiality), meaning the oath forbids any drinking of the river's water, whether by kari' or by vessel. The commentator elaborates on the definition of kari'—drinking directly by placing the mouth in the water—and notes a disagreement about whether wading into the water is a prerequisite. He then addresses a related ruling: if someone swears not to drink from a well or cistern, he breaks the oath by any drinking of its water, because the literal meaning (kari') for such containers is abandoned by custom. The second issue is the oath of a ruler (wali) to a man to inform him of every corrupt person who enters the city. The commentator explains that this oath is restricted to the duration of the ruler's tenure, as established by dalil al-hal (inference from context), because the purpose—restraining evil—can only be achieved while the ruler holds authority.

Key Terms
الكرع (al-kar'): Drinking directly by placing the mouth into the water or vessel without using hands or a cup.
من (min): Preposition meaning 'from'; in Hanafi oath jurisprudence, its interpretation (ibtida' vs. tab'id) determines the scope of the oath.
دلالة الحال (dalil al-hal): Inference from the circumstances to determine the intended meaning of an oath; used here to restrict the ruler's oath to his tenure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a man swears not to drink from a well and then drinks its water with a cup?
A) He breaks his oath only if he drinks directly from the well by mouth.
B) He breaks his oath by agreement of all scholars because the literal meaning is abandoned.
C) He does not break his oath because the well is not a river.
D) The ruling depends on whether he said 'min' or 'min ma' al-birr'.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of 'min' in oaths of drinking depends on whether the literal meaning (kari') is still in use; for rivers it is disputed, for wells it is abandoned.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Drinking from a River Oath
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the interpretation of 'min' in river-drinking oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the interpretation of 'min' in a river-drinking oath and explain the usul principle underlying their disagreement.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'min' in a river-drinking oath determines whether drinking from a vessel breaks it—a dispute over literal vs. common metaphorical usage.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding oaths related to drinking from a river. The point of contention is the interpretation of the preposition 'min' (from). Abu Hanifa holds that 'min' indicates the beginning of the action, restricting the oath to direct lapping, while the Sahibayn argue that common metaphorical usage takes precedence, making drinking from a vessel a violation. Understanding this disagreement illuminates the Hanafi approach to textual interpretation and the role of usul al-fiqh in deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the case
Begin with the oath: 'I will not drink from this river.' Identify the core issue—what act constitutes a violation?
This is a typical oath scenario in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Identify the dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that only lapping (kar') directly from the river breaks the oath. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that drinking from a vessel filled from the river also breaks it.
The ikhtilaf is within the Hanafi school itself.
3
Extract the dalil
The key word is 'min' (from). Abu Hanifa interprets it as ibtida' al-ghaya (beginning of purpose), meaning the action must start from the source itself. The Sahibayn interpret it as partitive (tab'id), understanding the phrase as 'drink from the river's water' in a general sense.
The Arabic preposition 'min' has both literal and metaphorical uses.
4
Apply usul principle
Abu Hanifa argues that the literal meaning of 'min' is still in use (musta'mala), so it blocks resort to metaphor. The Sahibayn argue that the common metaphorical usage (majaz muta'arif) takes precedence over the literal when the literal is not what is commonly understood.
This reflects a fundamental usul principle on the hierarchy of meanings.
5
Determine the thamarah
The practical consequence: one who swears an oath must consider his intention. Without intention, the default ruling may follow common usage (Sahibayn) or literal meaning (Abu Hanifa). In fatwa, the Sahibayn's view is often preferred for convenience.
The thamarah al-khilaf here is the trigger for kaffara (expiation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلاف الحلف من مائه. بالكرع أو بغيره، وعنده لابتداء الغاية، فالمعنى كونُ الشرب مبتدأ من ماء النهر، وهذا لا يكون إلا بالكرع. وذكر في «الهداية» وحواشيها، في توجيه الخلاف : إنّ الشرب بالإناء هو المتعارفُ المفهوم، فإنّ المفهوم من قولنا : فلان يشرب من دجلة، الشرب بالإناء منها، وقد مرّ أن المجاز المتعارف عندهما أولى من الحقيقة، فأوّله أنَّ كلمة : من ؛ للتبعيض، وحقيقته في الكرع، وهي مستعملة، فمنعت المصير إلى المجاز، وإن كان متعارفاً. [١] قوله : بخلاف الحلف من مائه ; أي إذا حلف لا يشرب من ماء هذا النهر لا يتقيد بالكرع، بل يحنث بالشرب من الإناءِ اتّفاقاً ; لأن الماء الذي في الإناء منسوب إلى النهر.
Contrary to the oath about its water. By lapping or otherwise, and according to him (Abu Hanifa) it is for the beginning of the purpose, so the meaning is that drinking begins from the river's water, and this is only by lapping. It is mentioned in al-Hidayah and its glosses in explaining the difference: indeed drinking from a vessel is the common understanding, for the meaning of our statement: 'So-and-so drinks from the Tigris' is drinking from a vessel from it. It has passed that common metaphorical usage according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is preferred over the literal meaning, so he (Abu Hanifa) interpreted it that the word 'min' is for partitiveness, and its literal meaning is lapping, and it is used, so it prevents resorting to the metaphorical, even if common. [1] His statement: Contrary to the oath about its water; meaning if he swore not to drink from the water of this river, it is not restricted to lapping, but he breaks it by drinking from a vessel by agreement; because water in the vessel is attributed to the river.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an oath not to drink from a river. The core issue is the interpretation of the preposition 'min' (from). Abu Hanifa holds that 'min' denotes the beginning of an action (ibtida' al-ghaya), so drinking begins only by direct lapping (kar') from the river itself. Therefore, drinking from a vessel does not break the oath. In contrast, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) consider that the common metaphorical usage (majaz muta'arif) of 'drinking from the river' is to drink from a vessel filled from it. They argue that this common usage takes precedence over the literal meaning. The text from al-Hidayah explains that while 'min' can be partitive (tab'id), its literal sense is lapping, which is still used (musta'mala). However, Abu Hanifa's position is that the literal meaning, being actually employed, prevents resorting to the metaphorical, even if the latter is common. The gloss [1] clarifies that if one swears not to drink from this river's water, he breaks the oath by drinking from a vessel by agreement (ittifaqan), because the water in the vessel is attributed to the river. This is because the word 'mā' (water) is generic, and the water in the vessel is considered part of the river's water. The dispute is thus about the meaning of 'min' and the principle of literal versus metaphorical interpretation.

Key Terms
الكرع (al-kar'): Drinking by placing the mouth directly into the water, without using a vessel
من (min): Preposition meaning 'from'; here debated as partitive (tab'id) or beginning of purpose (ibtida' al-ghaya)
المجاز المتعارف (al-majāz al-muta'ārif): The commonly understood metaphorical usage of a phrase, even if not literally correct

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if someone swears 'I will not drink from the Tigris' and then drinks from a cup filled with Tigris water, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the water in the cup is from the Tigris.
B) No, because he did not drink directly from the river.
C) Yes, because the cup is considered part of the river.
D) No, because the oath is invalid if not done by lapping.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of 'min' in a river-drinking oath leads to differing rulings on whether using a vessel breaks the oath, illustrating the tension between literal and common metaphorical usage in Hanafi usul.
